The Buyer's Option of Inspection and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Rescission Without the Seller
Commentary on the seller's lack of option of inspection and an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the buyer's option of inspection, explain why prior satisfaction is ineffective, and distinguish the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) from Abu Yusuf's view regarding the necessity of the seller's presence for rescission.

Core Idea
The buyer's option vests upon seeing; prior satisfaction is void. The seller has no option.

The option of inspection (khiyār al-ru’yah) gives the buyer who purchased something unseen the right to rescind upon actually seeing it. This lesson examines a key passage from the Hanafi tradition that clarifies when this right can be exercised. The passage also reveals an important intra-Hanafi dispute: does rescission require the seller's presence? Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the option correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage states emphatically that 'there is no option for its seller' (la li-bā'i‘ih). The seller who sells something unseen has no right to rescind upon seeing it. This is the starting point.
The buyer's option is exclusive.
2
Understand the nature of the buyer's option
The option is established only upon seeing (al-ru’yah). Before seeing, it is legally non-existent. Therefore, any expression of satisfaction (riḍā’) before seeing is ineffective. The buyer may still rescind after seeing.
This answers the objection: if the option is non-existent before seeing, how can the buyer rescind before seeing? The answer: rescission before seeing is based on the contract being non-binding (ghayr lāzim), not on the option.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The commentator poses a hypothetical objection: if the option is non-existent before seeing, the buyer should not have the right to rescind before seeing. The response distinguishes between rescission based on the option (which requires seeing) and rescission because the contract is not yet binding. Prior satisfaction cannot make the contract binding because complete consent requires knowledge of the object.
This is a classic method of farsh al-khilāf.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the seller's presence
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, rescission (faskh) under the option of inspection is only valid in the presence of the seller. Abu Yusuf holds that it is valid even without his presence. The text notes that riḍā’ (satisfaction) is valid without the seller's presence, but rescission itself is different.
The relied-upon position is that of the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad).
5
Apply the ruling to a practical scenario
Suppose a buyer buys a car unseen, inspects it later, and decides to rescind. If the seller is not present, according to the majority the rescission is invalid. The buyer must wait until the seller is present or seek his consent.
This shows the thamarah al-khilāf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية

لا لبائعه
[1] 63 أي إن رَضِيَ قبل الرُّؤيةِ يكون له حق الفسخ " إذ رآه ، لكن لو فسخ قبلَ الرُّؤيةِ ينفذ الفسخ ؛ بحكم أنَّهُ " عقد غيرُ لازم حتى لا يجوز إجازته عند الرؤية ، (لا لبائعه ) : أي إذا باع شيئا لم يَرَهُ لا يكون له الخيار إذ رآه .
(١) قوله : يكون له حق الفسخ ؛ لأنَّ هذا الخيار تعلق ثبوته بالرؤيا فكان عدماً قبلها، فلا يصح إسقاطه قبلها.
فإن قيل : لو كان عدماً قبل الرؤية لما كان له حق الفسخ قبلها. أجيب : بأن حق الفسخ قبلها ليس برضاء ؛ لأنّ الرّضاءَ به لا يتحقق إلا برؤيتِهِ بحكم الخيار، بل لأنه عقد غيرُ لازم، حتى لو ردّه قبل الرؤية ليس له أن يرضى عندها. واعلم أن الفسخ بخيار الرؤية لا يحتاج إلى قضاء ولا رضاء ، لكنَّه لا يصح إلا بحضرة البائع عند أبي حنيفة و محمد ، وقال أبو يوسف : يصح بغير حضرته أيضاً، والرضاء يصح بدون حضرته. كذا ذكره البرجنْدِي نقلاً عن «شرح الطحاوي».
Book of Sales: Option of Inspection. [Margin: 'Not for its seller.'] [1] 63 That is, if he expressed satisfaction before seeing, he has the right to rescind when he sees it, but if he rescinds before seeing, the rescission is effective, because it is a non-binding contract, so its ratification at the time of seeing is not valid. (Not for its seller): That is, if he sells something he has not seen, he does not have the option when he sees it. (1) His saying: 'he has the right to rescind' because this option's establishment is connected to seeing, so it was non-existent before it, thus dropping it before seeing is not valid. If it is said: If it was non-existent before seeing, he would not have the right to rescind before it. The answer: The right to rescind before seeing is not satisfaction, because satisfaction with it is not realized except by seeing it by virtue of the option, but rather because it is a non-binding contract, so that if he returns it before seeing, he cannot be satisfied upon seeing. Know that rescission under the option of inspection does not require a court ruling or satisfaction, but it is not valid except in the presence of the seller according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said: It is valid without his presence as well, and satisfaction is valid without his presence. Thus mentioned by al-Barjandi quoting from Sharh al-Tahawi.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn 'لا لبائعه' (not for its seller), stating that the seller does not have the option of inspection. The commentary explains that the buyer's option vests upon seeing, not before. If the buyer expressed satisfaction before seeing, it is ineffective, and he may still rescind upon seeing. The option is non-existent before seeing, so it cannot be dropped beforehand. The objection that if it were non-existent then the buyer could not rescind before seeing is answered: the rescission before seeing is not based on the option but on the contract being non-binding; satisfaction is only complete after seeing. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the seller's presence for a valid rescission, while Abu Yusuf allows it without. The relied-upon position is that of the majority.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): The option of inspection; the right of a party to a contract to rescind upon seeing the object of sale if it was unseen at the time of contract.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; the act of canceling a contract unilaterally by virtue of a legal option.
رضاء (riḍā’): Satisfaction; the inner consent that completes a contract. For a contract to be binding, complete satisfaction is required.
حضرة البائع (ḥaḍrat al-bā‘i‘): The presence of the seller; a condition for valid rescission according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), what is required for a valid rescission under the option of inspection?
A) The presence of a judge
B) The presence of the seller
C) The buyer's written statement
D) A witness to the rescission

Key Takeaway
The buyer's option of inspection vests upon seeing; prior satisfaction is irrelevant, and the majority requires the seller's presence for a valid rescission.

Definition of 'Seeing' in the Option of Inspection
Explanation that 'seeing' means knowledge of the intended attributes, not literal sight only

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'ru’yah' in the context of khiyār al-ru’yah and identify the means by which knowledge of the object is acquired according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
In the option of inspection, 'seeing' means knowing the intended attributes — not just eyesight.

The Hanafi school takes a practical approach to the option of inspection. Rather than insisting on literal sight, the law gives effect to the purpose of the option: ensuring the buyer knows the essential qualities of the item. This lesson unpacks how 'seeing' is defined and extended to cover smell, description, and any means that delivers knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the key term 'ru’yah' in the passage and note that its scope is broader than literal sight.
2
Understand the author's expansion
Recognise that the author uses metaphorical generalisation (majāz) to equate 'seeing' with 'knowledge of the intended [attributes]'.
3
Examine the examples given
Work through the three examples: musk (smell), changed item (re‑evaluation), blind person (description). For each, see how the option vests at the moment knowledge is acquired.
The examples show that the method of acquiring knowledge varies.
4
Derive the legal principle
The common thread is that the option protects the buyer's right to be satisfied with the item's intended attributes. Any reliable means of obtaining that knowledge suffices.
This aligns with the Hanafi maqāsid (objectives) approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : إذ رآه ؛ المراد بالرؤية : العلم بالمقصود ، فهو من عموم المجاز ؛ لوجودِ مسائل اتفاقية لا يكتفى بالرؤية فيها ؛ مثلاً إذا كان المبيع مما يعرف بالشم كمسك اشتراه وهو يراه، فإنّه إنما يثبت الخيارُ له عند شمه ، فله الفسخُ عند شمه لعدم رؤيته، وكذا لو رأى شيئا ثم اشتراه فوجده متغيّراً ؛ لأنّ هذه الرؤية غير معرفة للمقصود الآن، وكذا شراء الأعمى يثبت له الخيارُ عند الوصف له، فأقيم فيه الوصف مقام الرؤية. كذا ذكره العلامة الحموي.
His saying: 'when he sees it'; what is meant by 'seeing' is knowledge of the intended [attributes], so it is within the scope of metaphorical generalization, because there are agreed-upon cases where mere sight is not sufficient. For example, if the sold item is known by smell, like musk which one buys while seeing it, then the option is established for him upon smelling it, so he has the right to rescind upon smelling it due to not seeing [its essential quality]. Similarly, if he saw something and then bought it and found it changed, because that sight does not inform him of the intended [attributes] now. Similarly, the purchase of a blind person: the option is established for him upon description, so description is made to stand in place of sight. Thus mentioned by al-‘Allāmah al-Ḥamawī.
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is the definition and scope of 'seeing' in the option of inspection. The commentator explains that 'seeing' does not mean literal vision alone; rather it means knowledge of the intended attributes of the object. This is a metaphorical extension (majāz). Three examples are given: (1) If the item is known by smell (e.g., musk), the option vests upon smelling, not just seeing. (2) If the buyer saw the item earlier but it has changed by the time of purchase, the option vests upon the new inspection, as the earlier sight no longer gives knowledge. (3) A blind person acquires the option upon receiving a description of the item, since sight is impossible for him. Thus, description substitutes for sight. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the option's purpose is to ensure knowledge of the desired attributes.

Key Terms
الرؤية (al-ru’yah): In the context of khiyār al-ru’yah, it means obtaining knowledge of the intended attributes of the object, which may be through sight, smell, or description.
مجاز (majāz): Metaphorical or extended meaning; here the literal term 'seeing' is used for a broader concept (knowledge) due to commonality of purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He does not have this option because he cannot see
B) He acquires it upon receiving a description of the item
C) He must appoint a sighted agent to inspect
D) He has no option because his contract is invalid

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, 'seeing' in the option of inspection is interpreted broadly as knowledge of the intended attributes, allowing the right to rescind when that knowledge is obtained through any reliable means.

Seller's Option of Inspection: Abu Hanifa's Initial View and Retraction
Abu Hanifa's initial view and retraction on seller's option of inspection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa retracted his initial view on the seller's option of inspection and identify the proofs he used.

Core Idea
The option of inspection is exclusive to the buyer; the seller cannot rescind simply because the item exceeded expectations.

In this lesson, we examine a fascinating episode in Hanafi fiqh: Abu Hanifa's initial ruling that the seller also has the option of inspection, derived by qiyas from other options. He later retracted this view, adopting the final position that only the buyer has this option. We will follow his reasoning, the textual proof that led to the retraction, and the rational distinction that solidifies the school's position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial view and its basis
Abu Hanifa initially held that the seller has the option of inspection, using qiyas (analogy) with the option of defect (khiyār al-‘ayb) and the option of condition (khiyār al-sharṭ). Recognize that the option of defect is not limited to the buyer; if the price is counterfeit, the seller may return it analogously.
This initial view is a valid ijtihad based on the principle that both parties need complete satisfaction.
2
Understand the rationale for the initial view
The contract becomes binding only with complete satisfaction (riḍā). The seller's satisfaction is incomplete without knowing the exact attributes of the item, which requires seeing it. Therefore, the argument was that the seller should also have the right to rescind upon seeing.
This reasoning appeals to the general objective of contractual fairness.
3
Examine the retraction proof: the hadith
The hadith 'Whoever buys something and does not see it, then he has the option when he sees it' explicitly attaches the option to the buyer (man ishtara). By implication, it restricts the option to the buyer, overriding the qiyas.
The hadith is cited as a primary textual evidence (dalīl naqlī).
4
Examine the retraction proof: the precedent of Talhah and Uthman
The historical case narrated by al-Ṭaḥāwī and al-Bayhaqī: Jubayr ibn Muṭ‘im ruled that only the buyer (Talhah) had the option, not the seller (Uthman). This supports the hadith-based ruling.
This demonstrates the practice of the Companions (ṣaḥābah) affirming the hadith.
5
Analyze the rational distinction (farq)
The buyer returns the item due to loss of a desired attribute (the item is less than expected), which necessitates an option. The seller returns the item because it is more than expected (a gain), which does not necessitate an option. This is analogous to selling a slave as defective who turns out sound – the seller has no option by consensus.
This farq addresses the essence of the option: protecting against loss, not against gain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكان أبو حنيفة الله يقول أولاً : إِنَّ الخيار للبائع أيضاً اعتباراً بخيار العيب والشرط، فإنَّ خيار العيب لا يختص بالمشتري ، بل إذا وجد البائع الثمن زيفاً فهو بالخيار إن شاءَ جَوَّز ، وإن شاءَ ردّ كالمشتري إذا وجد المبيع معيباً، لخر العقد لا ينفسخ برد الثّمن، وينفسخ بردّ المثمن ؛ لأنه أصل دون الثمن، وخيار الشرط يصح من الجانبين.
ووجه هذا القول : أنَّ العقد إنّما يلزم بتمام الرّضاء حتى لا يزول ملك البائع إلا برضائه ولا يثبت الملك للمشتري إلا برضائه، وتمامُ الرّضاء إنما يتحقَّقُ إِذا عَلِمَ أوصاف المبيع، والعلم بأوصاف المبيع يتعلق بالرؤية، فإنّ بالرؤية يحصل الاطلاع على وقائع لا تحصل بالعبارة ، فلم يكن البائع راضياً بزوال ملكه، فالعقد غير لازم من جهة، فله الفسخ.
ثم رجع إلى أنه لا خيار له ؛ لأنَّ خيار الرؤية معلق بالشراء ؛ لقوله : «مَن اشترى شيئا فلم يره فله الخيار إذا رآه ، فلا يثبت دونه. ولِمَا رَوَى الطحاوي والبيهقي عن علقمة بن أبي وقاص : «إنَّ طلحة اشترى من عثمان مالاً ، فقال : لي الخيار لأني بعت ما لم أره ، وقال طلحة : لي الخيار لأني اشتريت ما لم أره ، فحكما بينهما جُبير بن مطعم فقضى أن الخيار لطلحة ، ولا خيار لعثمان». كذا في «كمال الدراية».
والفرق أنَّ المشتري إنما يردُّ المبيع بحكم عدم الرؤية باعتبار فوات وصف مرغوب فيه عنده ، وهذا يوجب الخيار، والبائع إنّما يردّه باعتبار أن المبيع أزيد مما ظنّه ، وهذا لا يوجب الخيار له ، كما لو باع عبدا على أنه معيب، فإذا هو سليم ؛ فإنّه لا يثبت له الخيار بالإجماع.
And Abu Hanifa used to say initially that the option is for the seller as well, by analogy with the option of defect and the option of condition. For the option of defect is not specific to the buyer; rather, if the seller finds the price counterfeit, he has the option: if he wishes he may validate, and if he wishes he may return it, like the buyer when he finds the sold item defective. Because the contract is not rescinded by returning the price, but it is rescinded by returning the object sold, since it is the principal, not the price. And the option of condition is valid from both sides. The basis of this view is that the contract only becomes binding with complete satisfaction, so the seller's ownership does not leave except with his satisfaction, and the buyer's ownership is not established except with his satisfaction, and complete satisfaction is only realized when he knows the attributes of the sold item, and knowledge of the attributes is connected to seeing, for by seeing one obtains information about realities that are not obtained by verbal description. So the seller is not satisfied with the removal of his ownership, thus the contract is non-binding from his side, and he has the right to rescind. Then he retracted to the view that he has no option, because the option of inspection is attached to purchase; due to his saying: 'Whoever buys something and does not see it, then he has the option when he sees it.' So it is not established without purchase. And due to what al-Tahawi and al-Bayhaqi narrated from ‘Alqamah ibn Abī Waqqāṣ: 'Talḥah bought some property from ‘Uthmān. He said: I have the option because I sold what I did not see. And Talḥah said: I have the option because I bought what I did not see. So they appointed Jubayr ibn Muṭ‘im as arbitrator between them, and he ruled that the option belongs to Talḥah, and there is no option for ‘Uthmān.' Thus in Kamāl al-Dirāyah. And the difference is that the buyer returns the sold item due to lack of seeing, considering the loss of a desired attribute, and this necessitates the option. The seller returns it considering that the sold item is more than what he thought, and this does not necessitate the option for him, just as if he sold a slave as defective, then he turned out to be sound; then he has no option by consensus.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage details Abu Hanifa's initial view that the seller also has the option of inspection, and his retraction to the final position that only the buyer has it. The initial view was based on qiyās (analogy) with the option of defect and option of condition, where both parties have symmetrical rights. The rationale: both parties need complete satisfaction, which requires knowledge of the item's attributes; since the seller also lacks full knowledge (he does not see the item exactly as it is), the contract should be non-binding for him too. However, Abu Hanifa later retracted, adopting the position that the option is exclusive to the buyer. Two proofs: (1) The hadith 'Whoever buys something...' explicitly attaches the option to the buyer. (2) The historical case of Talḥah and ‘Uthmān, where Jubayr ruled that only Talḥah (the buyer) had the option. A rational distinction (farq) is drawn: the buyer may lose a desired attribute (the item is less than expected), while the seller's concern is that the item is more valuable than expected, which does not justify an option. This is analogous to selling a defective item that turns out to be sound — the seller has no option by consensus.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyār al-‘ayb): Option of defect; the right to rescind a sale if a defect is found in the object, available to the buyer (and in some cases to the seller if the price is defective).
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): Option of stipulation; a conditionally agreed right to rescind within a specified period, available to either party by agreement.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): Option of inspection; the right of the buyer to rescind a sale after seeing the goods if they were unseen at the time of contract, available only to the buyer in the final Hanafi view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did Abu Hanifa retract his initial view that the seller also has the option of inspection?
A) Because the hadith specifies the option for the buyer only
B) Because the seller can always inspect before selling
C) Because the seller's potential gain does not warrant an option
D) Both A and C

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection is a right exclusive to the buyer; the seller cannot rescind a sale merely because the item exceeded expectations.

Seller's Right of Option of Sight (Khiyar al-Ru'ya)
Ruling with proof on seller's option of sight

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the seller's option of sight and explain the proof from hadith and the story of Talhah and 'Uthman.

Core Idea
The option of sight is for the buyer only; the seller has no right to rescind based on not seeing the item.

Does a seller who sells an item he has not seen have the right to rescind the contract upon seeing it? This lesson examines the Hanafi position, which restricts the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) exclusively to the buyer. We will analyze the primary proof, a hadith, and a historical arbitration, and understand why the seller's potential dissatisfaction does not grant him an option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and the issue
First, recognize that the core question is whether the seller has khiyar al-ru'ya. The Hanafi ruling is that he does not. Note that a contrary view is presented and then refuted.
The opposite view is based on the idea that full consent requires sight; it is rejected by the majority of Hanafis.
2
Locate the primary dalil from hadith
The hadith 'Whoever buys something and does not see it has the option when he sees it' is cited. Notice it mentions 'buys', so the option is attached to the act of buying, not selling.
The conditional clause 'if he buys' restricts the option to the buyer.
3
Analyze the secondary dalil from the story of Talhah and 'Uthman
The story shows that two Companions disputed and the arbiter Jubayr ibn Mut'im ruled the option for Talhah (buyer) and not for 'Uthman (seller). This is a practical precedent supporting the hadith.
4
Understand the distinction (farq) between buyer and seller
The buyer's option is due to a missing desirable attribute; the seller's potential reason is that the item turned out better than expected. The latter does not give an option by consensus (e.g., selling a slave as defective who turns out sound).
This distinction is crucial to understand why the seller's claim is rejected.
5
Consider the effect of a defect on the option
The passage begins by stating that both khiyar al-ru'ya and khiyar al-shart are invalidated if the object becomes defective in the buyer's possession. This protects the seller from a defective return.
The footnote clarifies that the option is invalidated to prevent harm to the seller, as the object left his ownership sound.

Source Text

And it invalidates it, and the option of stipulation invalidates it. If the price is counterfeit, he has the option: if he wishes he may accept, and if he wishes he may return, like the buyer when he finds the object sold defective. For the contract is not annulled by returning the price, but is annulled by returning the object sold; because it is the principal, not the price. And the option of stipulation is valid from both sides. Complete consent is only realized when he knows the attributes of the sold object, and knowledge of attributes depends on sight. For by sight one gains knowledge of particulars that cannot be obtained by description. So the seller was not satisfied with the removal of his ownership, thus the contract is not binding from one aspect, so he may rescind. Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the seller's option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) and the effect of a defect. The passage begins by stating that both the option of sight and the option of stipulation are invalidated by defect. Then it presents a view that the seller also has an option of sight, reasoning that consent requires knowledge of the object's attributes, which is only achieved through sight. However, the author returns to the established Hanafi position that the seller has no such option. The proof is the hadith: 'Whoever buys something and does not see it has the option when he sees it' — this only mentions the buyer. Further, the story of Talhah and 'Uthman, where the judge ruled that the option belongs to Talhah (the buyer) and not to 'Uthman (the seller), is cited. The difference is explained: the buyer's option is due to a missing desirable attribute, while the seller's reason is that the object is more than expected, which by consensus does not give him a right to reject. The footnote clarifies that if the object becomes defective while with the buyer, the option of sight is invalidated to prevent harm to the seller, as the object left his ownership sound.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): An option valid for the buyer to confirm or rescind the sale after seeing the object if bought unseen.
تعيب (ta'ayyub): The sold object becoming defective while in the possession of the buyer, which invalidates the option of sight to protect the seller.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated by either party for a specified period during which the contract is suspended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Both the buyer and the seller
B) Only the buyer
C) Only the seller
D) Neither party

Key Takeaway
The option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) is exclusive to the buyer; the seller, even if he sold unseen, cannot rescind based on not seeing the item.

Acts That Invalidate Khiyar al-Ru'ya
Ruling statement classifying invalidating acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify acts that invalidate khiyar al-ru'ya and apply the rules to determine when the option is lost.

Core Idea
Acts creating irrevocable rights invalidate khiyar al-ru'ya at any time; acts merely indicating consent only invalidate after sight.

Khiyar al-ru'ya gives the buyer the right to confirm or reject a contract after seeing the object. However, certain actions by the buyer can forfeit this right. This lesson examines the two categories of invalidating acts and the reasoning behind them, including a key intra-Hanafi dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Look at the matn: it first mentions acts that are 'irrevocable or create rights for others' (emancipation, absolute sale, pledge, lease). Then acts that 'do not create rights for others' (sale with option, musawamah, gift without delivery).
Irrevocable acts cannot be undone once performed.
2
Contrast the timing of invalidation
The first category invalidates the option whether done before or after sight. The second category only invalidates after sight. Understand why: the first category indicates final consent because it creates binding rights that cannot be rescinded.
3
Analyze the rationale
The commentary explains that irrevocable acts (like freeing a slave) make rescission of the sale impossible because they are valid and ownership had to be present. Acts that do not create third-party rights (like offering for sale) only indicate consent when done after sight.
Before sight, such acts are ambiguous and do not waive the option.
4
Examine the defect and virtual defect case
The text notes that if the object becomes defective (real or virtual) while with the buyer, the option is lost. The example: if the seller brings the object to the buyer's home and the buyer doesn't see it, returning it would require transport, counted as a virtual defect.
This shows khiyar al-ru'ya is tied to physical inspection.
5
Assess the intra-Hanafi dispute
According to al-Kafi, a sale with a stipulated option for the seller invalidates khiyar al-ru'ya. However, al-Zahiriyya (and a narration of al-Hasan) hold that it does not, except when the buyer's option is stipulated. The text prefers al-Kafi's view.
The practical difference: if the buyer sells with seller's option, does khiyar remain? Al-Kafi says no.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية
وتصرف لا يُفْسَخُ كالإعتاقِ والتَّذبير، أو يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع المطلق، والرهن والإجارة قبل الرؤية أو بعدها، وما لا يُوجِبُ حقا لغيره كالبيع بالخيار والمساومة، والهبة بلا تسليم يُبْطِلُ بعدها لا قبلها [١] وتصرف لا يُفْسَخُ كالإعتاق والتدبير، أو يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع المطلق) : أي بدون شرط الخيار ) ، (والرَّهْنُ والإجارة قبل الرُّؤية أو بعدها ) : أي هذه التَّصَرُّفات تبطل خيار الرؤية سواء كانت قبل الرؤية أو بعدها. (1)[8] (وما لا يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع بالخيار" ، والمساومة ، والهبة بلا تسليم يُبْطِلُ بعدها لا قبلها : لأنَّ هذه التصرفات لا تدل على صريح الرضاء سليماً، فلا يعود معيباً، والتعيُّب سواءً كان بفعله أو بفعل غيره، وسواء تعيب بعض المبيع أو تمامه. حتى لو اشترى عدل زطي فحدث بثوب منه عيب ليس له أن يردّه، سواء كان التعيُّبُ حقيقة أو حكماً ، كما إذا حمل البائع المبيع إلى منزل يراه المشتري، ولم يره المشتري فليس له أن يردّه، إذ اختاره الفقيه أبو الليث الله ؛ لأنه لو رد لاحتاج إلى الحمل، فيصير بمنزلة عيب حادث عند المشتري. كذا ذكره البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً عن «الفصول العمادية». [١] قوله : بدون شرط الخيار ؛ أي خيار البائع، فإنه لو باعه على أن المشتري بالخيار يبطل الخيار كما صرحوا به. [٢] قوله : تبطل خيار الرؤية ؛ أما قبل الرؤية فلتعدُّرِ الفسخ في هذهِ التصرُّفاتِ لوقوعها صحيحة ؛ لابتناء صحتها على قيام الملك ، وقد وُجِدَ وبعد صحتها لا يمكنُ رفعها ، وأما بعد الرؤية ؛ فلانها دلائل الرّضاء. (٤) قوله : والمساومة ؛ أي عرض المبيع ليُباع ، وأما عرضه ليقوم فلا يبطل خياره. (٠)قوله : لأن...الخ ؛ أي لأنّ هذه التصرفاتِ المذكورة لا تزيد على صريح (1) المساومة : العرض على البيع . ينظر : «مجمع الأنهر»(٢ : ٣٥).
Book of Sales: Option of Sight. And a disposition that cannot be rescinded — like emancipation and tadbir — or one that creates a right for another — like an absolute sale, pledge, and lease before or after sight — invalidates it. And that which does not create a right for another — like a sale with an option, musawamah, and a gift without delivery — invalidates after it but not before it. [1] The disposition that cannot be rescinded like emancipation and tadbir, or that creates a right for another like absolute sale (i.e., without a condition of option), and pledge and lease before or after sight: these dispositions invalidate the option of sight whether before or after sight. (1)[8] And what does not create a right for another, like a sale with an option, musawamah, and gift without delivery, invalidates after but not before, because these dispositions do not indicate explicit consent. Soundly, it should not return defective. And becoming defective applies whether by his action or another's action, whether part or all of the sold object becomes defective. Even if he bought a bale of cloth and a garment in it becomes defective, he may not return it, whether the defect is real or virtual, as when the seller carries the sold object to the buyer's home to show him and the buyer does not see it, then he may not return it, because if he were to return it, he would need to carry it back, making it like a defect arising with the buyer. Thus mentioned by al-Barjandi quoting al-Fusul al-'Imadiyya. [1] His statement: without a condition of option means the seller's option, so if he sells it on the condition that the buyer has an option, it invalidates the option as they have stated. [2] His statement: invalidates the option of sight means that before sight, these dispositions invalidate because rescinding them after they have been validly concluded is impossible, as their validity depends on ownership existing, and after validity it cannot be undone. After sight, they invalidate because they indicate consent. [3] His statement: with an option means with a stipulated option for the seller, so it invalidates the option of sight. This is what is mentioned in al-Kafi. In al-Zahiriyya it is mentioned: 'If he sells with the seller's option, the option of sight is not invalidated except in the narration of al-Hasan, and with the buyer's option it is invalidated.' Thus in Sharh al-Niqaya by al-Barjandi. (4) His statement: and musawamah means offering the sold object for sale. As for offering it for appraisal, it does not invalidate his option. (0) His statement: because... etc. means that these aforementioned dispositions do not exceed explicit... (1) Musawamah: offering for sale. See: Majma' al-Anhur (2:35).
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the Kitab al-Bay' on khiyar al-ru'ya, explaining which dispositions invalidate the option of sight. The matn divides acts into two categories: (1) those that cannot be rescinded (like freeing a slave or tadbir) or create rights for others (like an absolute sale, pledge, or lease) invalidate the option whether done before or after seeing the object. (2) Those that do not create rights for others (like a sale with a stipulated option, musawamah, or gift without delivery) only invalidate the option if done after seeing the object, not before. The commentary explains that the former invalidate because they are irrevocable or create third-party rights, making rescission impossible. The latter, lacking such rights, only indicate consent after sight. The passage also discusses the effect of a defect, clarifying that if a defect occurs while the object is with the buyer, the option is lost. A special case: if the seller brings the object to the buyer's home and the buyer does not see it, returning it would require transport, which counts as a virtual defect. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to al-Kafi, a sale with seller's option invalidates khiyar al-ru'ya, but according to al-Zahiriyya, it does not except in a narration of al-Hasan; the buyer's option always invalidates.

Key Terms
تصرّف لا يفسخ (tasarruf la yufsakh): A legal disposition that cannot be revoked or annulled, such as emancipation or tadbir, because it creates an irrevocable status.
بيع مطلق (bai' mutlaq): An absolute sale without any condition of option for either party; it creates a completed transfer of ownership.
مساومة (musawamah): Offering goods for sale without specifying a price or negotiating; it does not create any binding right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following invalidates khiyar al-ru'ya even before the buyer sees the object?
A) Absolute sale (bai' mutlaq)
B) Sale with a stipulated option for the seller
C) Offering for musawamah
D) Gift without delivery

Key Takeaway
The timing of invalidation of khiyar al-ru'ya depends on whether the buyer's act creates an irrevocable right or merely indicates consent after sight.

Transactions That Nullify the Option of Inspection
Classification of transactions affecting the option of inspection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify transactions into those that nullify the option of inspection before and after sight, and explain the rationale for each classification using Hanafi talil.

Core Idea
Transactions indicating final satisfaction nullify the option of inspection; ambiguous ones only nullify after sight.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) allows a buyer to rescind a sale after seeing the item if it does not match the description. However, certain actions by the buyer or seller can nullify this right. This lesson examines how different transactions—such as sale, mortgage, gift, and negotiation—affect the option, drawing on the Hanafi classification in 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
From the passage, list the three types of transactions: irrevocable acts (e.g., freeing a slave), those creating a right for another (e.g., absolute sale), and those not creating a right (e.g., sale with option).
Irrevocable acts and rights-creating transactions nullify both before and after sight; the third category nullifies only after sight.
2
Understand the rationale
For each category, explain why nullification occurs or not. Irrevocable acts cannot be undone; rights-creating transactions indicate final satisfaction; ambiguous transactions lack clear satisfaction until sight.
The reasoning (talil) is based on the transaction's implication of consent and possibility of annulment.
3
Apply to examples
Given scenarios like a buyer mortgaging an unseen item or negotiating price, determine if khiyar al-ru'yah is nullified and when.
Mortgage nullifies before sight; negotiation for valuation does not; negotiation for sale nullifies after sight.
4
Analyze the intra-madhhab dispute
Compare the positions in al-Kafi and al-Zahiriyya regarding a sale with a stipulated option for the seller. Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) for the buyer's right of inspection.
Al-Kafi says the option is nullified; al-Zahiriyya says it is not, except in one narration from al-Hasan.

Source Text

كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية
وتصرف لا يُفْسَخُ كالإعتاقِ والتَّذبير، أو يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع المطلق، والرهن والإجارة قبل الرؤية أو بعدها، وما لا يُوجب حقا لغيره كالبيع بالخيار والمساومة، والهبة بلا تسليم يُبْطِلُ بعدها لا قبلها
وتصرف لا يُفْسَخُ كالإعتاق والتدبير، أو يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع المطلق: أي بدون شرط الخيار، والرَّهْنُ والإجارة قبل الرُّؤية أو بعدها: أي هذه التَّصَرُّفات تبطل خيار الرؤية سواء كانت قبل الرؤية أو بعدها. وما لا يُوجِبُ حقاً لغيره كالبيع بالخيار، والمساومة، والهبة بلا تسليم يُبْطِلُ بعدها لا قبلها: لأنَّ هذه التصرفات لا تدل على صريح الرضاء سليماً، فلا يعود معيباً، والتعيُّب سواءً كان بفعله أو بفعل غيره، وسواء تعيب بعض المبيع أو تمامه. حتى لو اشترى عدل زطي فحدث بثوب منه عيب ليس له أن يردّه، سواء كان التعيُّبُ حقيقة أو حكماً، كما إذا حمل البائع المبيع إلى منزل يراه المشتري، ولم يره المشتري فليس له أن يردّه، إذ اختاره الفقيه أبو الليث الله ؛ لأنه لو رد لاحتاج إلى الحمل، فيصير بمنزلة عيب حادث عند المشتري. كذا ذكره البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً عن «الفصول العمادية». قوله: بدون شرط الخيار؛ أي خيار البائع، فإنه لو باعه على أن المشتري بالخيار يبطل الخيار كما صرحوا به. قوله: تبطل خيار الرؤية؛ أما قبل الرؤية فلتعدُّرِ الفسخ في هذهِ التصرُّفاتِ لوقوعها صحيحة؛ لابتناء صحتها على قيام الملك، وقد وُجِدَ وبعد صحتها لا يمكنُ رفعها، وأما بعد الرؤية؛ فلانها دلائل الرّضاء. قوله: بالخيار؛ أي بخيار الشَّرط للبائع، فإنّه يبطل به خيار الرؤية، هذا هو المذكور في «الكافي»، وذكر في «الظهيرية»: «إنّه إذا باع بخيار البائع لا يبطل خيارُ الرّؤيةِ إلا في رواية الحسن الله، وبخيار المشتري يبطل». كذا في «شرح النّقاية» للعلامة البرْجَنْدِي. قوله: والمساومة؛ أي عرض المبيع ليُباع، وأما عرضه ليقوم فلا يبطل خياره. قوله: لأن...الخ؛ أي لأنّ هذه التصرفاتِ المذكورة لا تزيد على صريح
Book of Sale: Option of Inspection. And a transaction that cannot be annulled, like freeing a slave or tadbir, or that obligates a right for another, like an absolute sale, mortgage, and lease before or after sight, and that which does not obligate a right for another, like a sale with an option (khiyar), negotiation, and a gift without delivery: it nullifies after sight, not before. And a transaction that cannot be annulled like freeing a slave and tadbir, or that obligates a right for another like an absolute sale: i.e., without a condition of option, and mortgage and lease before or after sight: i.e., these transactions nullify the option of inspection whether before or after sight. And that which does not obligate a right for another like a sale with an option, negotiation, and a gift without delivery: it nullifies after them, not before: because these transactions do not indicate clear satisfaction without defect, so it does not become defective, and defect whether by his action or another's, and whether some or all of the sold item is defective. Even if he bought a load of clothes and a defect occurs in a garment thereof, he does not have the right to return it, whether the defect is real or legal, like if the seller carries the sold item to a house where the buyer sees it, and the buyer has not seen it, he does not have the right to return it. As chosen by Abu al-Layth, because if he returns it, he would need to carry it, so it becomes like a defect occurring with the buyer. This is mentioned by al-Barjandi quoting al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. He said: Without a condition of option: i.e., the seller's option, for if he sells it with the condition that the buyer has an option, the option is nullified as they state. He said: It nullifies the option of inspection: before sight due to impossibility of annulment in these transactions because they occur validly, being based on existence of ownership, and after their validity it cannot be lifted; after sight, because they are indicators of satisfaction. He said: With an option: i.e., with a stipulated option for the seller, for it nullifies the option of inspection. This is mentioned in al-Kafi, and in al-Zahiriyya: 'If he sells with an option for the seller, the option of inspection is not nullified except in the narration of al-Hasan, and with an option for the buyer it is nullified.' Likewise in Sharh al-Niqayah by al-Barjandi. He said: Negotiation: i.e., offering the item for sale; as for offering it for valuation, it does not nullify his option. He said: Because...etc.: i.e., because these mentioned transactions are not more than explicit satisfaction.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with reasoning from the Hanafi school on how various transactions affect the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). The author classifies transactions into three categories. First, transactions that cannot be annulled (like freeing a slave or tadbir) nullify the option of inspection both before and after sight because they are irrevocable. Second, transactions that create a right for another party (like an absolute sale, mortgage, or lease) also nullify the option both before and after sight because they are conclusive and indicate satisfaction. Third, transactions that do not create a right for another (like a sale with a stipulated option, mere negotiation, or a gift without delivery) only nullify the option after sight, not before, because they do not express clear and final satisfaction. The commentary elaborates that the nullification after sight is due to the transaction being an indication of approval. It also provides an example: if a buyer purchases a bundle of cloth and a defect occurs in one garment, he cannot return it even if the defect is legal (e.g., the seller brings the item to the buyer's house for viewing) because the act of carrying it becomes like a defect occurring with the buyer. The commentary then discusses a difference within the Hanafi school: according to al-Kafi, a sale with a stipulated option for the seller nullifies the option of inspection, but according to al-Zahiriyya it does not, except in a narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated here, but the text gives weight to the view from al-Kafi. Finally, negotiation (musawamah) means offering the item for sale, not for valuation; only the former nullifies the option.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the right of the buyer to annul a sale after seeing the purchased item if it does not meet the description or expectation.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The stipulated option: a condition in the sale contract allowing one or both parties to annul the sale within a specified period.
مساومة (musawamah): Negotiation: offering the item for sale to potential buyers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following transactions nullifies the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) both before and after sight?
A) A sale with a stipulated option (khiyar al-shart)
B) A gift without delivery
C) A mortgage (rahn)
D) Negotiation (musawamah) for sale

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection is nullified by transactions that indicate clear satisfaction, such as those creating a right for another, and is only nullified after sight by ambiguous transactions like negotiation.

Sufficient Inspection for Different Types of Goods
Definition of what constitutes sufficient inspection (ru'yah) for various items under the option of inspection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific body part or aspect that must be inspected for each type of good (slave, heap, riding beast, garment) to validly exercise khiyar al-ru'yah, and explain the rationale behind each requirement.

Core Idea
Sufficient inspection under khiyar al-ru'yah targets the most value-determining features: face for slaves, face and rump for riding beasts, whole heap for grain, and mark for garments.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) gives a buyer the right to cancel a purchase after seeing the item if it does not meet expectations. However, what counts as a sufficient 'seeing' varies by the nature of the good. Hanafi jurists have specified the required extent of inspection for different items, ensuring that the buyer gains genuine knowledge of the item's value. This lesson breaks down these rules as explained in the classical commentaries.

Guided Path
1
Classify the good
Identify which category the purchased item falls into: slave, heap (of grain), riding beast (horse, mule, donkey), or garment (with or without a design mark).
Each category has a different required focus for inspection.
2
Identify the primary value indicator
For each good, determine the part or aspect that most influences its market value. For slaves it is the face; for riding beasts, the face and rump; for a heap, the entire mass; for a garment, the visible outer side or the design mark.
The rules are built on the principle that the buyer must see what determines the price.
3
Apply the rule for homogeneous vs. differentiated goods
If the good is uniform (like a heap of grain or a plain garment), seeing the outer part suffices. If the good has a feature that varies (like a marked garment), the mark must be seen. If the inside may differ, both sides require inspection.
This accounts for the possibility of hidden defects affecting value.
4
Note scholarly variations within the Hanafi school
Be aware of differing opinions, such as Imam Muhammad's view that seeing the face of a riding beast alone is sufficient by qiyas to humans, and the view requiring seeing the legs. Understand which view is relied upon (mufta bihi).
The relied-upon position requires seeing both face and rump for riding beasts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنظر إلى وجه الأمة، والصبرة، ووجه الدَّابَةِ وكَفَلِها، وظاهر ثوب مطوي غير معلم، وإلى موضع علمهُ مُعَلِّماً
وهو إِنَّما يُبْطِلُهُ بعد الرُّؤيةِ ، أمَّا التَّصرُّفات الأولُ فهي أقوى ؛ لأَنَّ بعضها لا يقبلُ الفسخ ، وبعضُها أوجب حق الغير فلا يبطل.
والنظر إلى وجه الأمة ، والصبرة ، ووجه الدَّابَةِ وكَفَلِها ، وظاهر ثوب مطوي غير معلم" ، وإلى موضع علمه (۳) معلماً الرضاء، وهو لا يبطل خيار الرؤية قبلها ، إلا أنه إذا تعلق به حق الغير صار ذلك الحق مانعا من الفسخ ولا حق بهما.
[١]قوله : إلى وجه الأمة ؛ وكذا وجه العبد، صرّح به في «الكافي»، وخصص الوجه ؛ لأنَّ سائر الأعضاء في الرقيق تبع لوجهه، فإن القيمة فيه تتفاوت بتفاوته، والتساوي في سائر الأعضاء صرَّحَ به غير واحدٍ من الفقهاء. [٢] قوله : ووجه الدابة وكفلها ؛ فلا يسقط برؤية وجهها حتى ينظر إلى كَفَلِها ؛ لانه موضع مقصود منه كالوجه، هو الصحيح. كما في «المحيط»، واكتفى محمد الله بالنَّظر إلى وجهها اعتباراً بالآدمي، وشَرَطَ بعضُهم رؤية القوائم.
وعن الإمام له في البردون والبغل والحمار يكفي أن يرى شيئاً منه إلا الحافر والذنب والناصية. كما في «البحر» (۳) ، والدابة لغة: كل ما يمشي على الأرض، ثمّ خُصت في العرف بالفرس والبغل والحمار.
(۳) قوله : غير مُعلم ؛ لأنّ برؤية ظاهره يُعْلَمُ حال البقيَّة ؛ إذ لا تتفاوت أطراف الثوب الواحد إلا يسيراً. (٤) قوله : مُعلِّماً ؛ لأنَّ ماليَّته تتفاوت بحسب علمه، أطلق في هذا، لكن في «المحيط» (٤) مقيَّداً بما إذا كان مطويا، هذا إذ لم يخالف باطنُ الثَّوبِ ظاهره، أما إذا اختلفا فلا بد من رؤية الباطن قبل هذا في عرفهم.
And looking at the face of a slave girl, a heap, and the face of a riding beast and its rump, and the apparent part of a folded garment without a mark, and to the place of its mark if marked. And it only nullifies after sight; as for the first transactions, they are stronger: because some of them do not accept annulment, and some obligate a right for another, so it does not nullify. And looking at the face of a slave girl, and a heap, and the face of a riding beast and its rump, and the apparent part of a folded garment without a mark, and to the place of its mark if marked: satisfaction, and it does not nullify the option of inspection before it, except that if a right of another is attached to it, that right becomes a barrier to annulment, and there is no right for them. He said: 'To the face of a slave girl' and likewise the face of a male slave, stated in al-Kafi, and he specified the face because the other limbs of the slave are subordinate to the face, for value varies with it, and equality in other limbs is stated by many jurists. He said: 'And the face of the riding beast and its rump': so it is not waived by seeing its face until he looks at its rump, because it is an intended part like the face; this is the correct view. As in al-Muhit, and Muhammad was satisfied with looking at its face by analogy to human, and some stipulated seeing the legs. And from Imam Abu Hanifa: regarding a mule, donkey, and himar, it is sufficient to see something from it except the hoof, tail, and forelock. As in al-Bahr. And riding beast (dabba) linguistically: everything that walks on the earth, then it was specified in custom to horse, mule, and donkey. He said: 'Without a mark': because by seeing its apparent part, the state of the rest is known, since the sides of a single garment do not differ except slightly. He said: 'Marked': because its value varies according to its mark; he spoke generally in this, but in al-Muhit it is restricted to when it is folded; this is when the inner of the garment does not differ from its outer; if they differ, then it is necessary to see the inner before this in their custom.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines what constitutes sufficient inspection (ru'yah) for different types of goods under the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). The matn lists: for a slave (male or female), looking at the face is sufficient; for a heap (of grain, etc.), seeing the whole heap; for a riding beast (horse, mule, donkey), seeing the face and the rump (croup); for a folded garment without a mark, seeing the apparent outer part; for a garment with a mark (design), seeing the place of the mark. The commentary elaborates: The face of a slave is specified because the value is mainly determined by the face; other limbs are subordinate. For a riding beast, both face and rump are necessary because the rump is also an intended part, though Imam Muhammad sufficed with the face by analogy to humans. There is also a view that legs must be seen. For mules and donkeys, seeing any part except the hoof, tail, and forelock is sufficient. The passage also clarifies that the transaction only nullifies the option after sight, and the first category of transactions (those that are irrevocable) are stronger and prevent annulment. The commentary adds that for a garment without a mark, the outer appearance is assumed uniform; for a marked garment, the mark is the most value-determining part, but if the inside differs from the outside, the inside must also be seen.

Key Terms
وجه الأمة (wajh al-amah): The face of a female slave; in the context of inspection, seeing the face suffices for khiyar al-ru'yah.
صبرة (subrah): A heap (of grain or similar); sufficient inspection requires seeing the entire heap.
كفل (kafl): The rump or croup of a riding beast; along with the face, it must be seen for sufficient inspection.
علم الثوب (alam al-thawb): The mark or design on a garment; for a marked garment, seeing the mark suffices for inspection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) A slave girl
C) A folded garment without a mark
D) A heap of grain

Key Takeaway
The required extent of inspection under khiyar al-ru'yah is tailored to each good's primary value indicator, ensuring the buyer has seen what truly determines the price.

Sufficiency of Inspection for the Option of Inspection
Determining sufficient inspection for khiyar al-ru'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific parts of slaves, animals, and garments that must be inspected to drop the option of inspection, and distinguish the relied-upon Hanafi position from intra-school variations.

Core Idea
For khiyar al-ru'yah, inspection must cover primary value indicators: face for slaves, face and croup for animals, and the design area for marked garments.

When you buy an unseen item, Islamic law grants you the right to inspect it and rescind if dissatisfied. But how much inspection is enough? This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah clarifies the Hanafi criteria for sufficient inspection across different commodities, and introduces important nuances regarding prior transactions and third-party rights that can affect the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commodity type
Determine whether the purchased item is a slave, a riding animal (dābbah), a folded garment without a design, or a garment with a design. The ruling differs for each.
The text also considers a heap of grain (ṣubrah) – face of the heap suffices.
2
Apply the rule for sufficient inspection
For a slave, seeing the face suffices. For a riding animal, both face and croup (rump) must be seen. For a plain folded garment, the exterior suffices. For a designed garment, the marked area must be seen.
The rationale is that value primarily depends on these parts.
3
Note the intra-Hanafi variation
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani held that seeing the face of an animal alone suffices by analogy with humans. However, the correct (muftā bihī) view requires both face and croup.
The text declares 'هو الصحيح' (it is correct) for the croup requirement.
4
Understand exceptions for certain animals
For mules, mares, and donkeys, seeing any part except the hoof, tail, and forelock suffices, according to a narration from Abu Hanifa.
This is a more lenient rule for these animals.
5
Consider the effect of prior transactions
If the buyer has already performed an act that cannot be rescinded (e.g., resale) or if a third-party right is attached, the option of inspection is not voided but the right to rescind is blocked.
The text states: 'initial transactions are stronger' and third-party rights prevent rescission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنظر إلى وجه الأمة، والصبرة، ووجه الدَّابَةِ وكَفَلِها، وظاهر ثوب مطوي غير معلم، وإلى موضع علمهُ مُعَلِّماً وهو إِنَّما يُبْطِلُهُ بعد الرُّؤيةِ، أمَّا التَّصرُّفات الأولُ فهي أقوى؛ لأَنَّ بعضها لا يقبلُ الفسخ، وبعضُها أوجب حق الغير فلا يبطل. والنظر إلى وجه الأمة، والصبرة، ووجه الدَّابَةِ وكَفَلِها، وظاهر ثوب مطوي غير معلم، وإلى موضع علمه معلماً الرضاء، وهو لا يبطل خيار الرؤية قبلها، إلا أنه إذا تعلق به حق الغير صار ذلك الحق مانعا من الفسخ ولا حق بهما.
[١]قوله: إلى وجه الأمة؛ وكذا وجه العبد، صرّح به في «الكافي»، وخصص الوجه؛ لأنَّ سائر الأعضاء في الرقيق تبع لوجهه، فإن القيمة فيه تتفاوت بتفاوته، والتساوي في سائر الأعضاء صرَّحَ به غير واحدٍ من الفقهاء.
[٢] قوله: ووجه الدابة وكفلها؛ فلا يسقط برؤية وجهها حتى ينظر إلى كَفَلِها؛ لانه موضع مقصود منه كالوجه، هو الصحيح. كما في «المحيط»، واكتفى محمد الله بالنَّظر إلى وجهها اعتباراً بالآدمي، وشَرَطَ بعضُهم رؤية القوائم.
وعن الإمام له في البردون والبغل والحمار يكفي أن يرى شيئاً منه إلا الحافر والذنب والناصية. كما في «البحر»، والدابة لغة: كل ما يمشي على الأرض، ثمّ خُصت في العرف بالفرس والبغل والحمار.
(۳) قوله: غير مُعلم؛ لأنّ برؤية ظاهره يُعْلَمُ حال البقيَّة؛ إذ لا تتفاوت أطراف الثوب الواحد إلا يسيراً.
(٤) قوله: مُعلِّماً؛ لأنَّ ماليَّته تتفاوت بحسب علمه، أطلق في هذا، لكن في «المحيط» مقيَّداً بما إذا كان مطويا، هذا إذ لم يخالف باطنُ الثَّوبِ ظاهره، أما إذا اختلفا فلا بد من رؤية الباطن قبل هذا في عرفهم.
And looking at the face of a female slave, a heap [of grain], the face of a riding animal and its croup, the exterior of a folded garment without a mark [i.e., without a decorative design], and [looking] at the place of its mark if it is marked [i.e., if it has a design]. And it [i.e., such partial inspection] only invalidates [the option] after the sighting. As for the initial transactions [done before sighting], they are stronger [i.e., valid] because some of them do not accept rescission, and some have established a right of a third party, so they are not voided. And looking at the face of a female slave, the heap, the face of a riding animal and its croup, the exterior of a folded garment without a mark, and at the place of its mark if marked [constitutes] satisfaction; and it does not void the option of inspection before it, except that if a right of a third party becomes attached to it, that right prevents rescission, and there is no [option] with them.
[1] His saying: 'At the face of a female slave'; likewise the face of a male slave, as stated in al-Kafi; and he specified the face because the other limbs of a slave are subordinate to the face, since the value varies with its variation, and equality in other limbs has been stated by more than one jurist.
[2] His saying: 'The face of the riding animal and its croup'; so [the option] is not dropped by seeing its face until one looks at its croup, because it is an intended part like the face; this is the correct [view]. As in al-Muhit, and Muhammad [al-Shaybani] sufficed with looking at its face by analogy with a human, and some conditioned seeing the legs.
And from the Imam [Abu Hanifa] regarding the mule, the mare, and the donkey, it suffices to see something of it except the hoof, the tail, and the forelock. As in al-Bahr. And 'dabbah' linguistically means everything that walks on the earth, then it was specified in custom to [mean] the horse, the mule, and the donkey.
(3) His saying: 'Without a mark' because by seeing its exterior the condition of the rest is known, since the ends of a single garment do not differ except slightly.
(4) His saying: 'If marked' because its monetary value varies according to its mark. This was left unrestricted here, but in al-Muhit it is restricted to when it is folded, this [applies] as long as the inside of the garment does not differ from its outside; but if they differ, then it is necessary to see the inside before this in their custom.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah explains the ruling of 'khiyar al-ru'yah' (option of inspection) regarding what constitutes sufficient inspection of various commodities to waive the right to rescind the contract upon seeing the item. The ruling states that looking at the face of a female slave (and analogously a male slave) suffices because the face is the primary indicator of value; other limbs are subordinate. For a riding animal (horse, mule, donkey), one must look at both the face and the croup (rump/backside) as each is a primary part of value. However, Imam Muhammad is reported to have sufficed with the face alone by analogy with humans, while some required seeing the legs. For a mule, donkey, or mare, Abu Hanifa said seeing any part except the hoof, tail, and forelock suffices. For a folded garment without a decorative design (علامة), seeing the outside suffices because the appearance of the whole is uniform. For a garment with a design, one must see the place of the design because the value depends on it. The passage also notes that if a transaction has already been performed (e.g., the buyer resold the item) which cannot be rescinded, or if a third party right is involved, the option is not voided but the right of the third party prevents rescission. The commentator expands on these rulings by citing authorities like al-Muhit and al-Bahr, and provides rationales (ta'lil) based on value variation and custom.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to rescind a contract after seeing the purchased item if it does not meet expectations.
كفل (kafal): Croup or rump of an animal; considered a primary part indicating value.
صبرة (subrah): A heap of grain; a commodity that is sold in bulk; inspection of the face of the heap suffices.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For a riding animal (dabbah), which parts must be inspected to drop the option of inspection according to the correct Hanafi view?
A) Only the face
B) Only the croup
C) Both the face and the croup
D) Any part except the hoof, tail, and forelock

Key Takeaway
Sufficient inspection for khiyar al-ru'yah varies by commodity: face for slaves, face and croup for animals, exterior for plain garment, design area for marked garment; prior irreversible transactions or third-party rights may block rescission.

Wakil vs. Rasul: Who Can Drop the Option of Inspection?
Distinguishing inspection by agent vs. messenger

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a wakil bil-shira', a wakil bil-qabd, and a rasul, and state which inspections drop the option of inspection according to Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Only a wakil in purchase or conditionally a wakil in receipt can drop the option; a messenger never does.

This lesson unpacks a subtle but crucial distinction in sale transactions: who can waive the buyer's right to inspect and return goods (khiyar al-ru'yah)? The Hanafi school has an internal disagreement regarding an agent authorized to receive the item (wakil bil-qabd). Understanding the difference between a wakil and a mere messenger (rasul) is essential for correctly applying the option of inspection in agency arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three roles
Locate the three distinct roles defined in the passage: wakil bil-shira' (agent in purchase), wakil bil-qabd (agent in receipt), and rasul (messenger). Note the scope of authority for each.
The wakil bil-qabd has the power to take possession; the rasul only conveys.
2
State the consensus on wakil bil-shira'
Recognize that by consensus, inspection by a wakil bil-shira' drops the option. The reason: all rights of the contract revert to him.
This is agreed upon within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the dispute on wakil bil-qabd
Contrast Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions. Abu Hanifa: inspection drops option if the item is seen before taking possession. Sahibayn: even inspection by wakil bil-qabd does not drop the option.
The root of the dispute is whether the wakil bil-qabd's authority extends to waiving personal rights of the buyer.
4
Distinguish the rasul
Note that a rasul's inspection never drops the option, even if he sees the item before receiving it. The buyer retains full khiyar al-ru'yah.
The rasul is a pure carrier with no independent authority.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, if the wakil bil-qabd sees and then takes possession, the buyer cannot return the item except for a hidden defect. Under the Sahibayn, the buyer can always return the item after seeing it, even if the agent had inspected it.
Practical consequence: reliance on which view is operative affects parties' rights.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونظرُ وَكِيلِهِ بالشراء أو بالقبض كاف لا نظر رسوله) : الوكيلُ بالقَبْضِ : هو الذي مَلكَهُ بالقَبْضِ بخلافِ الرَّسول، فإِنَّهُ الذي أمره بأداء الرسالةِ بالتسليم، فالبائع إذا لم يُسَلِّمْ إليه لا يملك الخصومة بخلاف الوكيل وأما في عرفنا فما لم يرَ الباطن لا يسقط خيارُه؛ لأنه ليس بمِثْلِي، فلا يُعْرَفُ كلَّه بدونِ نشره، ولا بُدَّ منه، وهو قول زفر، وفي «المبسوط») : الجواب على ما قال زفر، وهو المختار كما في أكثر المعتبرات (). ذكره في «مجمع الأنهر»، وغيره.
[ ] قوله: وكيله...الخ؛ ينبغي أن يُعْلَمَ أنّ هاهنا وكيلاً بالشراء، ووكيلاً بالقبض، ورسولاً. فصورة التوكيل بالشراء أن يقول الموكل: كن وكيلاً عني بشراء كذا. وصورة التوكيل بالقبض أن يقول: كن وكيلاً عنِّي بقبض ما اشتريته وما رأيته. وصورة الرسالة أن يقول: كن رسولاً عنِّي بقبضه.
فرؤية الوكيل بالشّراءِ تُسْقِط الخيار بالإجماع؛ لأنَّ حقوق العقدِ ترجع إليه. ورؤية الوكيل بالقبض تسقط الخيار عند الإمام له إذا قبضه بالنَّظر إليه، فحينئذ ليس له ولا للموكل أن يرده إلا بعيب وأما إذا قبضه مستوراً، ثم رآه فأسقط الخيار فإنه لا يسقط؛ لأنه إذا قبض مستوراً انتهى التوكيل بالقبض الناقص فلا يملك إسقاطه قصداً الصيرورته أجنبياً، بل للموكل الخيار.
وإن أرسل رسولاً لقبضه فقبضه بعدما رآه، فللمشتري أن يرده، وقالا: الوكيل بالقبض والرسول سواء في أن قبضهما بعد الرؤية لا يسقط خيار المشتري، كذا في «الدرر»، وغيره.
(٢) قوله: كافي؛ اعلم أنَّ الأصل في إسقاط خيار الرؤية أن رؤية جميع المبيع غير مشروط؛ لأنه متعذر.
And the look of his agent (wakil) in purchase or in receipt is sufficient, not the look of his messenger (rasul). The agent in receipt is the one who has ownership of receipt, unlike the messenger, for he is the one who is commanded to perform the message of delivery. So if the seller does not deliver to him, he does not have the right of litigation, unlike the agent. And in our custom, as long as he has not seen the inside, his option does not drop, because it is not [a commodity] of like type, so it is not known in its entirety without unfolding, and it is necessary. This is the statement of Zufar. And in al-Mabsut: 'The answer is according to what Zufar said, and it is the chosen [view] as in most of the authoritative works.' Mentioned in Majma' al-Anhur and others.
[ ] His saying: 'His agent...' etc.; it should be known that here there is an agent in purchase, an agent in receipt, and a messenger. The form of agency in purchase is that the principal says: 'Be an agent for me in purchasing such-and-such.' The form of agency in receipt is: 'Be an agent for me in receiving what I purchased and what I saw.' The form of the message is: 'Be a messenger for me in receiving it.'
Thus the sighting of the agent in purchase drops the option by consensus, because the rights of the contract revert to him. And the sighting of the agent in receipt drops the option according to the Imam [Abu Hanifa] if he receives it after seeing it; then neither he nor the principal can return it except for a defect. But if he receives it concealed and then sees it and drops the option, it does not drop, because when he received it concealed, the agency of receipt ended incomplete, so he does not have the power to drop it intentionally, as he becomes a stranger; rather the principal has the option.
And if he sends a messenger to receive it, and he receives it after seeing it, then the buyer has the right to return it. And the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] said: The agent in receipt and the messenger are equal in that their receipt after sighting does not drop the option of the buyer. Such is in al-Durar and others.
(2) His saying: 'Sufficient'; know that the principle in dropping the option of inspection is that seeing all of the sold item is not conditioned because it is impossible.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage addresses the subtle distinction between a wakil (agent) and a rasul (messenger) regarding the effect of their inspection on khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection). The author explains that there are three types: (1) agent in purchase (wakil bil-shira'), whose inspection drops the option by consensus because all contract rights revert to him. (2) Agent in receipt (wakil bil-qabd), who is empowered to receive the item after seeing it; if he sees it and takes possession, the option drops according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but if he takes possession without seeing it and then later sees it and tries to drop the option, it does not drop because his agency has ended. (3) Messenger (rasul), who is merely a carrier; his inspection does not drop the option, and the buyer retains the right to return the item. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the wakil bil-qabd and the rasul are alike in that their inspection after receipt does not drop the option; the buyer keeps the right. The commentator notes that the relied-upon view (al-mukhtar) is that of Zufar, who required unfolding the garment to see its inside for it to drop the option, due to the custom of the time. The core legal principle is that the option is not necessarily dropped by partial inspection; it depends on the type of commodity and the authority of the viewer.

Key Terms
وكيل بالشراء (wakil bil-shira'): An agent authorized to purchase an item on behalf of the buyer.
وكيل بالقبض (wakil bil-qabd): An agent authorized to take possession of a purchased item on behalf of the buyer.
رسول (rasul): A messenger sent to receive physical delivery of an item, but with no authority beyond conveyance.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection – the buyer's right to return an item after seeing it if it does not meet expectations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does the inspection of a wakil bil-qabd (agent in receipt) drop the option of inspection?
A) Always, regardless of when he sees the item
B) Only if he takes possession after seeing the item
C) Only if he takes possession before seeing the item
D) Never; only the principal's sighting matters

Key Takeaway
The inspection of a wakil bil-qabd drops the option only if he sees the item before taking possession (Abu Hanifa); a rasul's inspection never drops it.
