Sight by Agent vs Messenger in Option of Inspection
Explains the effect of sight by an agent or messenger on the option of inspection, plus rules for sufficient sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how sight by an agent for purchase, agent for receipt, and messenger affects the option of inspection, and distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Only an authorized agent's sight waives the option of inspection; a messenger's sight does not.

When a buyer appoints someone to purchase or receive an item, the legal effect of that person's sight on the option of inspection depends on the nature of the appointment. This lesson clarifies the distinctions between an agent for purchase, an agent for receipt, and a mere messenger, explores the intra-Hanafi dispute, and explains what constitutes sufficient sight to waive the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of representatives
Define wakīl bi al-shirā' (agent for purchase), wakīl bi al-qabḍ (agent for receipt), and rasūl (messenger). Understand the authority of each.
The agent has authority (wilāyah); the messenger merely carries out a task.
2
Determine the effect of their sight on khiyār
State the ruling for each: agent for purchase waives option by consensus; agent for receipt waives option under Imam Abu Hanifa if he receives with sight; messenger's sight never waives option.
The Sahibayn object on the agent for receipt.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilāf
Compare Imam Abu Hanifa's view (agent for receipt's sight waives option) with the Sahibayn's view (agent for receipt is like messenger).
The thamarah al-khilāf: under Abu Hanifa the buyer cannot return after agent's sight; under Sahibayn he can.
4
Understand the principle for sufficient sight
Explain that seeing the entire sold item is not required; only the intended part (e.g., face of a slave, outer part of a heap) must be seen to waive the option.
The text gives examples for slaves, animals, cloth, food, perfumes, and drums.
5
Apply the principle to different items
Work through the specific rules: for a slave, seeing the face suffices; for an animal, face and hindquarters; for a heap, outer part; for cloth, outer part unless embroidery inside; for a sheep, feeling or seeing udder as needed.
The ruling accommodates practicality and custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية
ونظرُ وَكِيلِهِ بالشراء أو بالقبض كاف لا نظر رسوله
الوكيلُ بالقَبْضِ : هو الذي مَلكَهُ بالقَبْضِ بخلافِ الرَّسول ، فإِنَّهُ الذي أمره بأداء الرسالةِ بالتسليم ، فالبائع إذا لم يُسَلِّمْ إليه لا يملك الخصومة بخلاف الوكيل وأما في عرفنا فما لم يرَ الباطن لا يسقط خيارُه ؛ لأنه ليس بمِثْلِي ، فلا يُعْرَفُ كلَّه بدونِ نشره، ولا بُدَّ منه، وهو قول زفر ، وفي «المبسوط»: الجواب على ما قال زفر ،، وهو المختار كما في أكثر المعتبرات . ذكره في «مجمع الأنهر» ، وغيره.
قوله : وكيله...الخ ؛ ينبغي أن يُعْلَمَ أنّ هاهنا وكيلاً بالشراء، ووكيلاً بالقبض، ورسولاً. فصورة التوكيل بالشراء أن يقول الموكل : كن وكيلاً عني بشراء كذا. وصورة التوكيل بالقبض أن يقول : كن وكيلاً عنِّي بقبض ما اشتريته وما رأيته. وصورة الرسالة أن يقول : كن رسولاً عنِّي بقبضه.
فرؤية الوكيل بالشّراءِ تُسْقِط الخيار بالإجماع ؛ لأنَّ حقوق العقدِ ترجع إليه. ورؤية الوكيل بالقبض تسقط الخيار عند الإمام له إذا قبضه بالنَّظر إليه، فحينئذ ليس له ولا للموكل أن يرده إلا بعيب وأما إذا قبضه مستوراً، ثم رآه فأسقط الخيار فإنه لا يسقط ؛ لأنه إذا قبض مستوراً انتهى التوكيل بالقبض الناقص فلا يملك إسقاطه قصداً الصيرورته أجنبياً، بل للموكل الخيار.
وإن أرسل رسولاً لقبضه فقبضه بعدما رآه ، فللمشتري أن يرده ، وقالا : الوكيل بالقبض والرسول سواء في أن قبضهما بعد الرؤية لا يسقط خيار المشتري، كذا في «الدرر»، وغيره.
(٢) قوله : كافي؛ اعلم أنَّ الأصل في إسقاط خيار الرؤية أن رؤية جميع المبيع غير مشروط ؛ ؛ لأنه متعذر :
(۱) «المبسوط» (۱۳ : ۳۷).
(۲) قال ابن عابدين في رد المحتار (٤ : ٦٧) : (والمختار هو نشره كله كما في أكثر المعتبرات، وعليه الفتوى ؛ لاختلاف الزمان إذ كان هذا بناءً على عادتهم في الكوفة أو بغداد». 
(۳) «مجمع الأنهر» (٢ : ٣٥).
(٤) «درر الحكام» (٢ : ١٥٨).
أما في العبد والأمة ؛ فلأنَّ في رؤيةِ جميع بدنهما رؤية مواضع عورتهما، ففي العبد لا يجوز أصلاً، سواء فسخ العقد أو لم يفسخ ، وفي الأمة فإنه لو فسخ البيع بخيار الرؤية بعد رؤية عورتها، كأن نظره إلى عورتها وقع في غير ملكه ؛ لأنَّ الفسخ رفع العقد من الأصل، فصار كأنَّ العقد لم يوجد ، فكأنَّ نظره وقع حراماً في أصله. وفي الثوب المطوي ؛ فيتضرَّرُ البائع بانكسار ثوبه بالنشر والطي على تقديرِ اشتراط رؤية جميع أجزائه.
وأما في الصبرة ؛ فلا يمكن اشتراط رؤية كلِّ حَبَّةٍ من حبَّاتِ الحنطة مثلاً، وهذا ملخص ما في «الكفاية » ، وقد مرَّ الكلام في رؤية ثوب مطوي سابقاً، فتذكر. وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لَمّا تعدَّر رؤية جميع المبيع فلا محال أن يعتبر رؤية ما هو المقصود ؛ لأنَّ بها يحصل العلم بالمبيع، ولا يشترط رؤية غير المقصود، ولا يكتفي بها وحدها، حتى لو رأى سائر أعضاء الرقيق دون ما هو المقصود كان باقياً على خياره. فالنَّظر إلى وجه الأمة وكذا إلى وجه العبدِ كافٍ لما مرَّ من أنَّ المقصود في الرَّقيقِ وجهه.
والنَّظر إلى وجهِ الدَّابة وكَفَلها كافٍ ؛ لأنهما مقصودان فيها ؛ ولهذا تتفاوت قيمتها بتفاوتهما.
والنَّظر إلى وجه الصبرة كافٍ؛ لأنه يعرفُ وصف البقية ؛ لأنه مكيلٌ يُعْرَفُ بالنموذج.
وكذا النَّظر إلى ظاهرِ الثَّوبِ ما يُعْلَمُ البقيَّةُ إلا إذا كان في طيه ما يكون مقصوداً، كمواضع العلم. نعم؛ لا بد من الحبس في الشَّاةِ التي تشترى في الذَّبح لأجل اللحم ؛ لأنَّ المقصود وهو اللحم يُعْرَفُ بالحبس وفي الشَّاةِ التي تُحْبَس في البيت لأجل الشاج، لا بد من رؤية الضرع، وفي المطعومات لا بدَّ من الدَّوق وعلى هذا في المشموماتِ لا بد من الشم، وفي دفوف الغازي لا بدَّ من سماع صوتها ، وقد مرَّ بحثه في بيان المراد بالرؤية.
(۱) «الكفاية» ( ٥ : ٥٣٦ - ٥٣٧).
Book of Sale: Option of Inspection. And the sight of his agent for purchase or for receipt is sufficient; not the sight of his messenger. The agent for receipt is the one who has been given authority over receipt, unlike the messenger, for he is the one ordered to perform the task of delivering. So if the seller does not deliver to him, he does not have the right to litigation, unlike the agent. But in our custom, if he does not see the inside, his option does not lapse, because it is not like-kind, so it cannot be fully known without unfolding it, and it is necessary, and this is the saying of Zufar. And in al-Mabsut: the answer is according to what Zufar said, and it is the chosen view as in most reliable works. Mentioned in Majma' al-Anhur and others. His saying: 'his agent, etc.'; it should be known that here there is an agent for purchase, an agent for receipt, and a messenger. The form of agency for purchase is that the principal says: be an agent for me in buying such-and-such. The form of agency for receipt is that he says: be an agent for me in receiving what I have bought and what I have seen. The form of message is that he says: be a messenger for me in receiving it. So the sight of the agent for purchase drops the option by consensus, because the rights of the contract revert to him. And the sight of the agent for receipt drops the option according to Imam Abu Hanifa if he receives it with sight, so then neither he nor the principal can return it except for a defect. But if he received it covered, then saw it and dropped the option, it does not drop because when he received it covered, the agency for receipt ended incomplete, so he cannot drop it intentionally because he becomes a stranger; rather the principal has the option. And if he sent a messenger to receive it and he received it after having seen it, the buyer has the right to return it. And they (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: the agent for receipt and the messenger are equal in that their receipt after sight does not drop the option of the buyer. Thus in al-Durar and others. (2) His saying: 'sufficient'; know that the basis in dropping the option of inspection is that sight of all the sold item is not a condition, because it is impossible: as for the male slave and female slave, because seeing their entire body involves seeing their private parts; for the male slave it is not permissible at all, whether the contract is rescinded or not; and for the female slave, if the sale is rescinded due to option of inspection after seeing her private parts, it is as if his look at her private parts occurred outside his ownership, because rescission lifts the contract from its origin, so as if the contract never existed, so as if his look occurred sinfully in its origin. And for folded cloth, the seller would be harmed by the breaking of his cloth due to unfolding and folding if sight of all its parts were required. And for a heap [of grain], it is impossible to require sight of every grain of wheat, for example. This is a summary of what is in al-Kifayah, and the discussion of seeing a folded cloth has passed previously, so remember. And when you know this, we say: since seeing the entire sold item is impossible, so inevitably what is intended is considered, because through it knowledge of the sold item is obtained; and seeing what is not intended is not a condition, nor is it sufficient alone. So if he saw the other limbs of the slave without what is intended, he remains with his option. So looking at the face of the female slave and likewise at the face of the male slave is sufficient, because the intended part in a slave is his face. And looking at the face and hindquarters of the animal is sufficient, because they are the intended parts in it; hence their value varies with their variation. And looking at the face of the heap is sufficient, because the description of the rest is known, because it is measured and known by sample. Likewise, looking at the apparent part of the cloth, the rest is known, unless there is in its fold something that is intended, like places of embroidery. Yes, it is necessary to feel the sheep that is bought for slaughter for the sake of meat, because the intended part, which is the meat, is known by feeling. And for the sheep that is kept in the house for the sake of milk, it is necessary to see the udder. And for edibles, tasting is necessary. Likewise, for things smelled, smelling is necessary. And for the drums of the warrior, hearing their sound is necessary. And its discussion has passed in the explanation of what is meant by sight. (1) al-Kifayah (5:536-537).
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage from the book 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, addresses the chapter on the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) in sales. The author begins by quoting the matn: 'And the sight of his agent for purchase or for receipt is sufficient; not the sight of his messenger.' The commentary then elaborates on the types of representatives: the agent for purchase (wakil bi al-shira'), the agent for receipt (wakil bi al-qabd), and the messenger (rasul). The key distinction is that the agent has authority (wilayah) over the transaction, while the messenger merely carries out a task. The sight of the agent for purchase drops the option by consensus because all rights of the contract revert to him. For the agent for receipt, Imam Abu Hanifa rules that if he receives the item after having seen it, his sight drops the option; thereafter, neither he nor the principal may return the item except for a hidden defect. However, if he receives it without seeing it (covered), then later sees it and attempts to drop the option, his sight has no effect because his agency ended upon receipt, and he becomes a stranger. The messenger's sight, even before receipt, does not drop the option; the buyer retains the right to return the item. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) hold that the agent for receipt and the messenger are alike: their sight after receipt does not drop the option. The author then cites Zufar's opinion that the option does not lapse until the inside is seen, which is the chosen view in later works. The commentary then shifts to explaining what constitutes sufficient sight (ru'yah) to drop the option. The principle is that seeing the entire object is not required because it is impossible or harmful. For slaves, seeing the face suffices because it is the intended part; seeing the private parts would be unlawful. For animals, the face and hindquarters are sufficient. For a heap of grain, seeing the face (outer part) suffices because the rest is known by sample. For cloth, seeing the outer part is enough unless there are important parts like embroidery inside. For a sheep bought for meat, feeling (al-habs) is necessary; for a sheep bought for milk, seeing the udder is necessary. For edibles, tasting is required; for perfumes, smelling; for warrior drums, hearing the sound. The author refers to earlier discussions in the commentary. The overall ruling is that the option of inspection is waived by sight of the intended part, and this is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
وكيل بالشراء (wakīl bi al-shirā'): An agent appointed specifically to purchase an item on behalf of the principal.
وكيل بالقبض (wakīl bi al-qabḍ): An agent appointed to receive (take delivery of) an item that has been purchased.
رسول (rasūl): A messenger who is merely sent to physically transport the item; he has no authority over the contract or receipt.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru'yah): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to return the purchased item if it does not match the description or if he has not seen it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the effect of a messenger seeing the item before he receives it on the buyer's option of inspection?
A) The messenger's sight waives the option, and the buyer cannot return the item.
B) The messenger's sight does not waive the option; the buyer retains the right to return the item.
C) The option is waived only if the principal also sees it later.
D) The option becomes void only if the item is damaged during transport.

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection is waived by sight of an authorized agent (for purchase or receipt) of the intended part of the item, but not by a messenger's sight.

Limitations of Khiyar al-Ru'ya: Viewing Slaves, Folded Garment, and Pile
Explaining why viewing all parts of certain items is impractical or unlawful for khiyar al-ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya for slaves, folded garments, and piles, and explain the rational proofs (dalil aqli) for each limitation.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-ru'ya does not require seeing every part when it leads to haram or undue harm.

This lesson examines three scenarios where the full viewing required by khiyar al-ru'ya is restricted: viewing slaves (male and female), a folded garment, and a pile of grain. The Hanafi school provides rational proofs based on avoiding the sin of looking at private parts and preventing harm to the seller. Understanding these limitations is essential for correctly applying the option of sight in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue
What is the scope of khiyar al-ru'ya? Does it require viewing the entire item? The text addresses cases where full viewing is problematic.
Khiyar al-ru'ya gives the buyer the right to rescind after seeing the item, but the extent of required viewing is not absolute.
2
Understand the Proof for Male Slave
Why is full viewing not allowed for a male slave? Because it necessarily involves seeing his private parts, which is absolutely unlawful (haram) regardless of contract status.
The ruling applies whether the buyer cancels or keeps the contract.
3
Distinguish Female Slave Ruling
For a female slave, the ruling differs if the buyer cancels after seeing her private part. The cancellation retroactively invalidates the contract, making the look occur outside ownership, thus haram.
If he does not cancel, the look was within ownership (as she was his property) and thus permissible? The text implies it is permissible only until cancellation, but careful: looking at private parts of one's own slave is permitted? The passage focuses on khiyar scenario.
4
Analyze the Folded Garment Proof
Requiring the buyer to unfold and see every part harms the seller through potential damage to the cloth. Therefore, full viewing is not required.
The buyer may inspect the garment as it is, without demanding unfolding.
5
Analyze the Pile Proof
It is physically impossible to require seeing every grain in a pile. Thus, khiyar al-ru'ya is satisfied by inspecting a representative sample.
The same principle applies to other bulk goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما في العبد والأمة ; فلأنَّ في رؤيةِ جميع بدنهما رؤية مواضع عورتهما، ففي العبد لا يجوز أصلاً، سواء فسخ العقد أو لم يفسخ , وفي الأمة فإنه لو فسخ البيع بخيار الرؤية بعد رؤية عورتها، كأن نظره إلى عورتها وقع في غير ملكه ; لأنَّ الفسخ رفع العقد من الأصل، فصار كأنَّ العقد لم يوجد , فكأنَّ نظره وقع حراماً في أصله. وفي الثوب المطوي ; فيتضرَّرُ البائع بانكسار ثوبه بالنشر والطي على تقديرِ اشتراط رؤية جميع أجزائه. وأما في الصبرة ; فلا يمكن اشتراط رؤية كلِّ حَبَّةٍ من حبَّاتِ الحنطة مثلاً، وهذا ملخص ما في «الكفاية ) , وقد مرَّ الكلام في رؤية ثوب مطوي سابقاً، فتذكر.
As for the male slave and the female slave: because seeing all of their bodies involves seeing the places of their private parts. So in the male slave, it is not permissible at all, whether he cancels the contract or not. And in the female slave, if he cancels the sale by the option of sight after seeing her private parts, it is as if his look at her private part occurred outside his ownership; because the cancellation raises the contract from the root, so it becomes as if the contract never existed, so his look occurred as forbidden in its origin. And in the folded garment: the seller is harmed by the breaking of his garment through unfolding and folding on the assumption of requiring seeing all its parts. And as for the pile (sabrah): it is not possible to require seeing every grain of wheat for example. This is the summary of what is in al-Kifayah. And the discussion on seeing a folded garment has passed earlier, so remember.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a ruling explaining why the option of sight does not require seeing every part of the sold item in certain cases. The text addresses three examples: slaves (male and female), a folded garment, and a pile (such as grain). For slaves, requiring seeing the entire body would necessarily involve seeing the private parts, which is unlawful non-mahram looking. This ruling distinguishes between a male slave and a female slave: for the male slave, such viewing is absolutely impermissible regardless of whether the contract is rescinded. For the female slave, if he rescinds the sale after seeing her private part, the look becomes forbidden because the cancellation retroactively nullifies the contract, meaning the look occurred when she was not his property. For the folded garment, requiring the buyer to unfold it to see all parts would harm the seller because repeated folding and unfolding may damage the cloth. For a pile of grain, it is physically impossible to inspect every single grain. The passage concludes by citing al-Kifayah and reminding the reader that the discussion on a folded garment has already been covered. This establishes the foundational principle that khiyar al-ru'ya is based on practicality and avoiding harm and unlawfulness.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of sight: the right of the buyer to rescind a contract after seeing the item if it does not match the description.
العبد (al-'abd): Male slave; a human being owned as property.
الأمة (al-amah): Female slave.
الثوب المطوي (al-thawb al-matwi): A folded garment; the text discusses the harm of unfolding it for inspection.
الصبرة (al-sabrah): A pile (e.g., of grain); the text says it is impossible to inspect every grain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible to require seeing all parts of a male slave when exercising khiyar al-ru'ya?
A) Because it would cause harm to the slave.
B) Because it would involve seeing his private parts, which is unlawful.
C) Because the seller would be inconvenienced.
D) Because the contract is automatically void.

Key Takeaway
When khiyar al-ru'ya would require a haram act, cause harm, or be impossible, full viewing is not required; the buyer may inspect what is reasonably accessible.

Specific Applications of Khiyar al-Ru'ya: Viewing the Intended Part
Listing rulings for specific items under khiyar al-ru'ya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on what suffices for khiyar al-ru'ya for various sold items and apply the principle of al-maqsud to new cases.

Core Idea
In khiyar al-ru'ya, viewing the intended part (maqsud) suffices; the required inspection varies by the nature of the item.

You've learned that khiyar al-ru'ya requires seeing the item before sale, but what if the item is large or has many parts? The Hanafi school answers: you only need to see the part that is the primary intended element (al-maqsud). This lesson applies that principle to slaves, animals, grains, garments, sheep for slaughter or milk, food, perfume, and drums.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item and its intended part
For each sold item, determine what is the maqsud—the part that determines value or quality. The text lists examples: for slaves, the face; for animals, face and croup; for grain, the surface; for garments, the exterior except embroidery; for sheep for slaughter, the meat (assessed by holding); for sheep for milk, the udder; for food, taste; for perfume, smell; for drums, sound.
The maqsud is not always visual; it can be accessed through other senses.
2
State the ruling
Once the maqsud is identified, the ruling is that seeing (or sensing) that part suffices to satisfy khiyar al-ru'ya. If the buyer later discovers a defect in a non-intended part, he cannot rescind the sale based on that defect alone.
The ruling is specific to what suffices; it does not affect other rights of the buyer.
3
Explain the reasoning
The reasoning (talil) is that since seeing the entire item is impossible or impractical, the law considers what is reasonable. The maqsud gives sufficient knowledge of the item's quality and value. For example, a slave's face indicates beauty; an animal's face and croup indicate strength; grain's surface indicates the whole pile if it is uniform.
The principle is based on necessity and general custom (urf).
4
Note exceptions and special cases
Some items require additional sensory checks beyond visual: sheep for slaughter require holding (al-habs) to assess meat; sheep for milk require seeing the udder; edibles require tasting; perfumes require smelling; drums require hearing. These exceptions arise because the maqsud is not fully appreciated by sight alone.
These special cases override the general rule of sufficiency of sight.
5
Apply to similar cases
Using the principle of al-maqsud, you can reason about new items. For example, a car: the engine might be the maqsud for performance, but looking at the exterior might suffice if the car is sold for appearance. The buyer should determine the intended use.
Modern applications require identifying the maqsud based on the nature of the item and the parties' intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت هذا فنقول : لَمّا تعدَّر رؤية جميع المبيع فلا محال أن يعتبر رؤية ما هو المقصود ; لأنَّ بها يحصل العلم بالمبيع، ولا يشترط رؤية غير المقصود، ولا يكتفي بها وحدها، حتى لو رأى سائر أعضاء الرقيق دون ما هو المقصود كان باقياً على خياره. فالنَّظر إلى وجه الأمة وكذا إلى وجه العبدِ كافٍ لما مرَّ من أنَّ المقصود في الرَّقيقِ وجهه. والنَّظر إلى وجهِ الدَّابة وكَفَلها كافٍ ; لأنهما مقصودان فيها ; ولهذا تتفاوت قيمتها بتفاوتهما. والنَّظر إلى وجه الصبرة كافٍ; لأنه يعرفُ وصف البقية ; لأنه مكيلٌ يُعْرَفُ بالنموذج. وكذا النَّظر إلى ظاهرِ الثَّوبِ ما يُعْلَمُ البقيَّةُ إلا إذا كان في طيه ما يكون مقصوداً، كمواضع العلم. نعم; لا بد من الحبس في الشَّاةِ التي تشترى في الذَّبح لأجل اللحم ; لأنَّ المقصود وهو اللحم يُعْرَفُ بالحبس وفي الشَّاةِ التي تُحْبَس في البيت لأجل الشاج، لا بد من رؤية الضرع، وفي المطعومات لا بدَّ من الدَّوق وعلى هذا في المشموماتِ لا بد من الشم، وفي دفوف الغازي لا بدَّ من سماع صوتها , وقد مرَّ بحثه في بيان المراد بالرؤية.
And if you know this, then we say: Since seeing all of the sold item is not possible, then necessarily seeing what is intended is considered; because through it knowledge of the sold item is obtained, and seeing what is not intended is not required, nor is it sufficient by itself, such that if he saw the other limbs of the slave without what is intended, he remains with his option. So looking at the face of the female slave and likewise at the face of the male slave is sufficient, because what is intended in slaves is their face. And looking at the face and croup of an animal is sufficient, because both are intended in it; and for this reason its value varies with their variation. And looking at the face of the pile is sufficient, because it knows the description of the rest; because it is a measured item known by sample. Likewise, looking at the exterior of the garment makes known the remainder, unless there is in its fold something intended, such as places of embroidery. Yes, it is necessary to hold (lit. restrain) the sheep that is bought for slaughter for the meat, because what is intended, namely the meat, is known by holding. And in the sheep that is kept in the house for milk, it is necessary to see the udder. And in edibles, tasting is necessary. And similarly in perfumes, smelling is necessary. And in the gazelle's drums [or: drums of the warrior], hearing their sound is necessary. And its discussion has passed in clarifying the meaning of sight.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage applies the general principle that khiyar al-ru'ya is satisfied by viewing the part of the item that is the primary intended element (maqsud). The text lists several examples: For slaves (both male and female), looking at the face is sufficient because the face is the determinant of value and beauty. For animals, both the face and the croup (hindquarters) are intended, as they affect the animal's strength and appearance. For a pile of grain, looking at the surface is sufficient because grain is measured and a sample suffices. For a garment, viewing the outside is sufficient unless there are embroidered areas in the folds that are explicitly intended. Special cases are then mentioned: a sheep bought for slaughter must be held (felt) to assess its meat; a sheep bought for milking requires seeing the udder; food must be tasted; perfumes must be smelled; and drums (or the drums of a warrior) must be heard. This is a detailed expansion of the fiqh of khiyar al-ru'ya, showing that the required viewing varies by the nature of the item.

Key Terms
المقصود (al-maqsud): The intended part of the sold item that is determinative of its value or quality.
الحبس (al-habs): Holding or handling an animal to assess its meat quantity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Looking at its face.
B) Hearing its sound.
C) Seeing its udder.
D) Tasting its milk.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, khiyar al-ru'ya is satisfied by viewing the intended part (maqsud) of the item; the required inspection is tailored to the item's nature.

Dispute on Whether Agent's Viewing Terminates Khiyar al-Ru'ya
Dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on agent's viewing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether an agent's viewing terminates khiyar al-ru'ya and explain the reasoning behind each position.

Core Idea
An agent's viewing only voids khiyar al-ru'ya if possession is taken with sight (Abu Hanifa).

The option of sight (khiyar al-ru'ya) allows a buyer to rescind a contract if the item does not match expectations. But can an agent appointed to take possession nullify this option by looking at the item? This lesson explores a classical Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two leading students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the scope of an agent's authority and the nature of complete possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and parties
What is the legal question? Which scholars disagree? The dispute is about whether an agent (wakil bil-qabd) can terminate the option of sight by viewing the item.
Intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn.
2
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the agent's viewing does not suffice because the agent was authorized only for possession, not for nullifying options. Their proof: nullifying the option is not part of possession.
The agent's authority is strictly limited to what was delegated.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa argues that possession is of two types: complete (with sight) and incomplete (without sight). If the agent takes possession while seeing, it is complete possession and the option drops. He analogizes to the principal's own action.
Complete possession integrates sight; the agent can choose either type.
4
Analyze the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the agent takes possession while seeing, according to Abu Hanifa the principal loses the option; according to the Sahibayn, the principal retains it because the agent's sight is irrelevant.
The dispute affects whether the principal can still rescind.
5
Note the tarjih (preferred view)
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but later Hanafi fatwa literature may prefer the Sahibayn's view as more cautious.
Check later sources like al-Hidayah or Radd al-Muhtar for the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعندهما نظر الوكيل بالقَبْضِ غِيرُ كافٍ ; لأَنَّهُ) وَكَلَهُ بالقَبْضِ لا بالنَّظر، ولأبي حنيفة ) أَنَّ القَبْضِ الكاملَ بالنَّظَرِ ; لِيُعْلَمَ أنَّ هذا هو الذي أمر بقبضه. [١] قوله : لأنه ... الخ ; حاصله : أنَّ الوكيل بالقبض وكله الموكل بالقبض، وإبطال خيار الرؤية ليس بقبض، فلا يملكه ; ولهذا لا يملك إبطاله قصداً بأنَّ قبضه مستوراً ثمَّ نظر إليه. (٢) قوله : ولأبي حنيفة ... الخ ; حاصله : أنَّ الموكل وكله بالقبض، وهو على نوعين : تام، وناقص. والقبض التام : هو أن يقبضه وهو يراه. والقبض الناقص : هو أن يقبضه مستوراً. وللموكل أن يقبضه بنوعيه فكذا الوكيل، فإذا قبضه وهو ينظر إليه بطل الخيار، بمقتضى تمام القبض، كما لو قبضه الموكل كذلك , وإذا قبضه مستوراً فقد اختار الناقص , وانتهت الوكالة به وتعيّن ذلك للموكل , فلا يملك إسقاطه بعد ذلك ; لكونه أجنبياً.
And according to them (the two companions), the agent's looking at the time of taking possession is not sufficient, because he was authorized to take possession, not to look. And according to Abu Hanifa, complete taking possession occurs by looking, in order to know that this is what he was ordered to take possession of. [1] The summary of his statement 'because...' is that the agent for taking possession is authorized by the principal to take possession, and nullifying the option of sight is not taking possession, so he does not have the authority to do so; hence he cannot nullify it intentionally by taking possession while it is covered then looking at it. [2] The summary of his statement 'and according to Abu Hanifa...' is that the principal authorized him for taking possession, which is of two types: complete and incomplete. Complete taking possession is that he takes possession while seeing it. Incomplete taking possession is that he takes possession while it is covered. The principal may exercise either type, and so may the agent. If he takes possession while looking at it, the option is nullified by virtue of complete possession, just as if the principal had done so. And if he takes possession while it is covered, he has chosen the incomplete type, the agency ends with it, and that is determined for the principal, so he cannot drop it thereafter because he is a stranger.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether an agent appointed to take possession (wakil bil-qabd) can terminate the khiyar al-ru'ya by viewing the item. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the agent's looking is not sufficient because he was only authorized to take physical possession, not to exercise the option. Abu Hanifa holds that complete possession includes seeing, so if the agent takes possession while looking, the option is voided. The commentary explains the reasoning: nullifying the option is not part of possession, so the agent cannot intentionally cancel it. However, Abu Hanifa argues that possession has two types: complete (with sight) and incomplete (without sight). The agent, like the principal, can choose either. If he takes possession with sight, the option drops. If he takes possession without sight, the agency ends with incomplete possession, and the agent cannot later drop the option because he is now a stranger to the contract. The relied-upon position is not stated here, but the commentary describes both views.

Key Terms
الوكيل بالقبض (al-wakil bil-qabd): An agent appointed to take possession of a sold item on behalf of the buyer.
القبض التام (al-qabd al-tamm): Complete possession that includes seeing the item.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): Option of sight: the buyer's right to rescind a contract for an item bought without seeing it, if it does not meet expectations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what condition must be met for an agent's viewing to terminate khiyar al-ru'ya?
A) The agent must view the item at the time of contract.
B) The agent must take possession while viewing the item.
C) The principal must authorize the agent to inspect.
D) The agent's viewing never terminates the option.

Key Takeaway
An agent's authority is limited to the delegated task; whether viewing terminates the option depends on whether sight is considered part of complete possession—a point of legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Khiyar al-Ru'ya for Houses: Custom Changes the Ruling
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya requiring interior viewing due to changed custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi fatwa on khiyar al-ru'ya for houses shifted from exterior-only viewing to requiring interior viewing, and identify the role of custom ('urf) in this change.

Core Idea
Custom can change a ruling: when houses varied little, exterior viewing sufficed; now variation demands interior inspection.

This lesson examines a fascinating example of how custom ('urf) can affect the application of a fiqh ruling. The case of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) for buying a house shows that the Hanafi school did not treat all times and places identically. The earlier ruling from the three imams sufficed with viewing the exterior because houses were similar. However, due to increased variation in modern homes, the fatwa shifted to require interior viewing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the historical ruling
The passage states that originally, seeing the exterior walls of a house or trees of an orchard was sufficient for khiyar al-ru'ya. This was the ruling from the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad).
This was based on the custom in Kufa/Baghdad where houses did not vary much.
2
Recognize the change in custom
The text says 'today' because there is now great variation (tafawut) in houses: summer/winter rooms, upper/lower floors, kitchens, roofs, etc.
The factor 'tafawut' is the key driver of the change.
3
Apply the principle that rulings can change with custom
Since the original ruling was based on a particular custom, when that custom changes, the ruling may also change. Here, the fatwa adopted Imam Zufar's view that interior viewing is now necessary.
This is an example of istihsan or maslaha based on change in 'urf.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The three imams held one view; Zufar held the opposing view. The commentator states Zufar's view is correct and the fatwa is upon it.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference) is clear: today, a buyer must see the interior or the option remains.
5
Understand the practical consequence
In practice, a buyer today must enter and see the entire interior (rooms, kitchen, roof, etc.) for khiyar al-ru'ya to be satisfied. If they do not, they retain the right to return the house.
This ruling is relied upon in Egypt, Syria, and Iraq according to the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط رؤية " داخلِ الدَّارِ اليوم : إنما قال اليوم ; لأنَّ الرِّوايةَ أَنَّهُ إِذا رَأَى حيطانَ الدَّارِ أو أشجار البستان من خارج كان كافياً ; وذلك لأن دورهــم وبساتينهم لم تكن متفاوتةً , فرؤية الخارج كانت مغنية عن رؤيةِ الداخل, أما الآن فالتفاوت فاحش فلا بد من رؤيةِ الداخل [٣] قوله : وشرط رؤية ... الخ ; قيل هذا قول زفر ه : وهو الصحيح وعليه الفتوى، واكتفى الثلاثة برؤية خارجها , وكذا برؤية صحنها، والأصح أنَّ هذا بناءً على عادتهم في الكوفة أو بغداد , فإنَّ دورهم لم تكن متفاوتة إلا في الكبر والصغر، وكونها جديدة أو لا , فأما في ديارنا فهي متفاوتة. قال الشَّارِحُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ , لأن بيوت الشتوية والصيفية والعلوية والسفلية مرافقها ومطابخها وسطوحها مختلفة، فلا بد من رؤية ذلك كله في الأظهر». وفي (۲) : «وهذا هو المعتبر في ديار مصر والشَّام والعراق». كذا في «النهر». «الفتح»
And the condition of seeing the interior of the house today: He said 'today' only because the narration is that if he saw the walls of the house or the trees of the orchard from outside, it was sufficient; and that because their houses and orchards were not varying, so seeing the exterior sufficed for seeing the interior. But now, variation is great, so seeing the interior is necessary. [3] His statement: 'and the condition of seeing...' etc. It is said that this is the view of Zufar, and it is the correct view and upon it is the fatwa. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) were content with seeing its exterior, and likewise with seeing its courtyard. The most correct is that this is based on their custom in Kufa or Baghdad, for their houses did not vary except in size and whether new or not. As for our lands, they vary greatly. Al-Zayla'i said: 'Because winter and summer houses, upper and lower, their amenities, kitchens, and roofs are different, so it is necessary to see all of that in the apparent view.' And in Fath al-Qadir: 'And this is what is relied upon in the lands of Egypt, Syria, and Iraq.' So it is in al-Nahr and al-Fath.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage addresses a change in the ruling regarding khiyar al-ru'ya for houses. The text states that currently, it is required to see the inside of the house, because houses now vary greatly in their internal features. The original ruling (from earlier texts) was that seeing the exterior walls or trees of an orchard sufficed, because houses and orchards in earlier times did not differ much in layout and quality. However, due to increased variation, the fatwa has shifted. The commentary explains that this is the view of Imam Zufar, and it is the relied-upon fatwa. The three (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad) originally sufficed with seeing the exterior or the courtyard. The commentator notes that the original ruling was based on the custom in Kufa or Baghdad, where houses only differed in size and age. In later times and other lands, houses have many different features (winter/summer rooms, upper/lower floors, amenities, kitchens, roofs), so viewing all of that is necessary in the strongest view. This is the standard in Egypt, Syria, and Iraq according to Fath al-Qadir.

Key Terms
الرِّواية (al-riwayah): The transmitted ruling from the early authorities (the three imams).
التفاوت (al-tafawut): Variation or difference in quality and features.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did the fatwa on khiyar al-ru'ya for houses change?
A) Because the early authorities retracted their opinions.
B) Because modern houses vary greatly in internal features.
C) Because the Quran commands interior inspection.
D) Because buyers demanded a change.

Key Takeaway
Custom can cause a fiqh ruling to change; in khiyar al-ru'ya, interior viewing of houses is now required due to increased variation.

Condition of Seeing Inside a House for the Sight Option
Ruling and commentary on required sight for khiyar al-ru'ya in house purchases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school shifted from requiring only seeing the outside of a house to requiring seeing the inside for the option of inspection, and identify the 'illa (effective cause) for the change.

Core Idea
Seeing inside a house is now required because custom changed; variation is the 'illa.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) allows a buyer to rescind a sale if the item is not as expected. A key question is what counts as sufficient sight to drop this option for a house. The Hanafi school's answer has evolved based on custom, and this passage explains the reasoning and the operative ruling today.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
State the type of option and the relied-upon condition for it to drop when purchasing a house.
The option is khiyar al-ru'ya; the condition is sight of the item.
2
Identify the 'illa (effective cause)
The ruling is based on custom regarding variation of houses. Determine how the 'illa changes the sufficiency of inside vs. outside sight.
'Illa is the variation in internal features.
3
Trace the difference between the three imams and Zufar
The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) held outside sight sufficient in their time; Zufar required inside sight. Understand the basis for each.
Custom in Kufa vs. later custom.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of adopting Zufar's view?
Today, a buyer must see inside to lose the option; otherwise, he retains it.
5
Note the tarjih and mufta bihi
Which position is operative for fatwa today and why?
Zufar's view is mufta bihi due to change in custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

و شرط رؤية داخل الدار اليوم

وعندهما نظر الوكيل بالقَبْضِ غِيرُ كافٍ ؛ لأَنَّهُ) وَكَلَهُ بالقَبْضِ لا بالنَّظر، ولأبي
حنيفة ) أَنَّ القَبْضِ الكاملَ بالنَّظَرِ ؛ لِيُعْلَمَ أنَّ هذا هو الذي أمر بقبضه. وشرط رؤية " داخلِ الدَّارِ اليوم : إنما قال اليوم ؛ لأنَّ الرِّوايةَ أَنَّهُ إِذا رَأَى حيطانَ الدَّارِ أو أشجار البستان من خارج كان كافياً ؛ وذلك لأن دورهــم وبساتينهم لم تكن متفاوتةً ، فرؤية الخارج كانت مغنية عن رؤيةِ الداخل، أما الآن فالتفاوت فاحش فلا بد من رؤيةِ الداخل
[١] قوله : لأنه ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنَّ الوكيل بالقبض وكله الموكل بالقبض، وإبطال خيار الرؤية ليس بقبض، فلا يملكه ؛ ولهذا لا يملك إبطاله قصداً بأنَّ قبضه مستوراً ثمَّ

والقبض التام : هو أن يقبضه وهو يراه.
والقبض الناقص : هو أن يقبضه مستوراً.
وللموكل أن يقبضه بنوعيه فكذا الوكيل، فإذا قبضه وهو ينظر إليه بطل الخيار، بمقتضى تمام القبض، كما لو قبضه الموكل كذلك ، وإذا قبضه مستوراً فقد اختار الناقص ، وانتهت الوكالة به وتعيّن ذلك للموكل ، فلا يملك إسقاطه بعد ذلك ؛ لكونه

واعلم أنَّ زفره لما قال باشتراط رؤيةِ داخل الدارِ فظنَّ بعضُهم أنَّ هذا اختلاف . وزمان، والحال أنّ أئمتنا الثلاثة اكتفوا برؤية خارج الدَّارِ ولم يشترطوا رؤية داخلها ؛ لكون الدور في زمانهم غير متفاوتة ، و زفر الله كان في زمانهم، فقد خالفهم، فعُلِمَ أنه قائل باشتراط رؤية داخلها وإن لم يتفاوت فكان هذا اختلاف حجة وبرهان.
The book of sale: The option of inspection. And the condition of seeing inside the house today. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the agent's sight upon taking possession is not sufficient, because he was appointed for taking possession, not for sighting. According to Abu Hanifa, complete taking possession is by sighting, so that it is known that this is what he was ordered to take possession of. And the condition of seeing inside the house today: He said 'today' because the narration is that if he saw the walls of the house or the trees of the garden from outside, it was sufficient; because their houses and gardens were not varied, so seeing the outside sufficed for seeing the inside. But now the variation is great, so seeing the inside is necessary. [1] His saying: 'because...' etc.; conclusion: that the agent appointed for taking possession is appointed by the principal for taking possession, and nullifying the option of inspection is not taking possession, so he does not have the power to do so; hence he cannot intentionally nullify it by taking possession while it is concealed and then looking at it. (2) His saying: 'And according to Abu Hanifa...' etc.; conclusion: that the principal appointed him for taking possession, which is of two types: complete and incomplete. Complete taking possession is that he takes possession while seeing it. Incomplete taking possession is that he takes possession while it is concealed. The principal may take possession in either way, so too the agent. So if he takes possession while looking at it, the option is nullified by virtue of complete taking possession, just as if the principal had taken possession like that. And if he takes possession while it is concealed, he has chosen the incomplete, and the agency ends with it, and that is determined for the principal, so he cannot drop it afterwards because he is a stranger. [3] His saying: 'And the condition of seeing...' etc.; it is said that this is the statement of Zufar, and it is correct and on it is the fatwa. The three imams were content with seeing its outside, and likewise seeing its courtyard. The most correct is that this is based on their custom in Kufa or Baghdad, for their houses were not varied except in size and newness or not. But in our lands they are varied. Al-Zayla'i said: 'Because winter and summer houses, upper and lower, their annexes, kitchens, and roofs are different, so it is necessary to see all of that in the most apparent view.' And in (2): 'This is the considered view in the lands of Egypt, Syria, and Iraq.' Thus in al-Nahr. And it is conditioned to see inside the house today. And know that Zufar, when he said it is required to see inside the house, some thought that this is a difference of time. But the fact is that our three imams were content with seeing outside the house and did not require seeing inside, because the houses in their time were not varied, and Zufar was in their time, so he disagreed with them. Thus it is known that he is the one saying it is required to see inside even if there is no variation, so this is a difference of proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on the condition of seeing inside a house for the sight option (khiyar al-ru'ya) in sales. The author explains that today, it is required to see the inside of a house because houses are now greatly varied. In earlier times, seeing the outside was sufficient because houses were uniform. The passage also discusses the ruling regarding an agent appointed to take possession of a purchased item: if the agent sees the item while taking possession, the option of inspection is nullified; if he takes possession without seeing, the agency ends but the option remains. There is a dispute between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the sufficiency of the agent's sight. Abu Hanifa argues that complete taking possession requires sight, so the agent's sight suffices. The companions say the agent was appointed for taking possession, not sighting, so his sight does not nullify the option. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa. The condition of seeing inside the house is attributed to Imam Zufar, and it is the correct position for fatwa today. The three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) were content with seeing the outside in their time, but due to change in custom, Zufar's view is now followed.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to rescind the sale if he sees the item and finds it not to his satisfaction, provided he had not seen it at the time of contract.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession: physical or constructive control of the purchased item, which may be complete (with sight) or incomplete (without sight).
وكيل (wakil): Agent: a person appointed by the principal to perform a specific task, here taking possession of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because Zufar had a stronger proof from the Qur'an.
D) Because the three imams later changed their ruling.

Key Takeaway
The sufficiency of outside sight for khiyar al-ru'ya depends on custom; today, inside sight is required due to variation in houses.

Sale and Purchase by a Blind Person
Ruling that a blind person's sale and purchase are valid with an option.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of a blind person's sale and purchase and explain why the option of inspection drops through tactile, olfactory, or gustatory examination.

Core Idea
A blind person's sale and purchase are valid, and the option of inspection drops by touch, smell, or taste.

Many assume that a blind person cannot engage in sales because he cannot see the item. The Hanafi school, however, affirms full legal capacity for the blind, granting them an option of inspection that functions similarly to the sighted buyer's option. This lesson examines the ruling, its proof, and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the opening sentence: the sale and purchase of a blind person are valid (sahih). This is the core Hanafi position.
The ruling applies to both congenital and acquired blindness.
2
Understand the Option
The blind buyer has an option (khiyar) because he buys an unseen item. This is analogous to a sighted person buying sight unseen.
The option is khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection).
3
Examine How the Option Drops
The option drops when the blind person touches, smells, or tastes the item. These senses substitute for sight in providing knowledge.
Touching before purchase drops the option; after purchase, the option remains until satisfaction is shown.
4
Consider the Shafi‘i Objection
Al-Shafi‘i argued that a blind person's purchase is invalid because he cannot see the item. He distinguishes between acquired and congenital blindness.
The opposing view is based on the principle that buying unseen is impermissible.
5
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response cites the established practice of the blind (without denial) and necessity: if invalid, the blind would starve. Also, qiyas treats the blind like a sighted person buying unseen.
The Hanafi view is considered stronger due to necessity and ijma' 'amali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع الأعمى وشراؤه صح، وله الخيار مشترياً، ويسقط بجسه المبيع، وشمه، وذوقه

[١] قوله : صح ؛ وقال الشافعي الله : لا يصح شراؤه ؛ لأنه لا يجوز شراء ما لم يره، وقيل: إن كان بصيراً فعمي فهو كالبصير عند الشَّافِعِي له، وإن كان أكمه فلا يجوز بيعه وشراؤه أصلاً ؛ لأنه لا يعرف ألوان الأشياء وصفتها. كذا في «الكفاية). وهذا القول محجوج بمعاملة العميان من غير نكير، وبأنَّ مِن أصله أن مَن لا يملك الشراء لا يملك الأمر به، فإذا احتاج الأعمى إلى ما يأكل، ولا يتمكن من شراء
(۲)
المأكول، ولا التوكيل به مات جوعاً، وفيه من القبح ما لا يخفي. كذا في «العناية ) [٢) قوله : وله الخيار مشترياً ؛ لأنَّ شراء ما لم يره جائز، وللمشتري الخيار والأعمى كالبصير الذي يشتري ما لم يره فيجوز شراؤه مع ثبوت الخيار له كالبصير.
"
[٣] قوله : بجسه ؛ وهذا إذا كان قبل الشراء، وأما إذا كان بعده فإن خياره لا يسقط باتفاق الروايات بل يمتد إلى أن يوجد ما يدلُّ على الرضاء من قول أو فعل على
الصحيح . كذا في كمال الدراية () والجس: بسودن وبراسيدن. كذا في «الصراح»، وفي «المصباح»: «جسه بيدِهِ جساً من باب قتل، واجتسه ليتَعَرَّفُه ، وجس الأخبار وتجسسها : تتبعها، ومنه الجاسوس ؛ لأنه يتتبع الأخبار ويفحص عن بواطن الأمور، ثمّ استعير لنظر العين». انتهى (٤) قوله : أي بجسه فيما... الخ ؛ لأنّ هذه تُفيد العلم كالبصير فتقوم مقام الرؤية.
And the sale and purchase of a blind person are valid, and he has the option as a buyer, and it drops by his touching the sold item, smelling it, and tasting it. [1] His saying: 'valid'; and al-Shafi'i said: his purchase is not valid because it is not permissible to buy what one has not seen. And it is said: if he was sighted then became blind, he is like a sighted person according to al-Shafi'i, but if he was born blind, his sale and purchase are not valid at all because he does not know the colors and attributes of things. Thus in al-Kifayah. And this statement is refuted by the dealing of the blind without denial, and because from his principle, the one who cannot purchase cannot command it. So if a blind person needs food and cannot purchase it, nor appoint an agent, he would die of hunger, and there is ugliness in that which is not hidden. Thus in al-Inayah. [2] His saying: 'and he has the option as a buyer' because buying what one has not seen is permissible, and the buyer has the option, and the blind person is like a sighted person who buys what he has not seen, so his purchase is valid with the option established for him like the sighted person. [3] His saying: 'by his touching'; and this is if it is before the purchase. But if it is after, then his option does not drop by agreement of the narrations, rather it extends until something indicating satisfaction appears from speech or action according to the correct view. Thus in Kamal al-Dirayah. And 'al-jass' means 'to stroke and touch' in Persian. Thus in al-Surah, and in al-Misbah: 'He touched it with his hand, jassan, from the verb qatala, and ijtassahu to recognize it, and jass al-akhbar wa tajassasuha: to investigate, from which is al-jasus (spy) because he investigates news and examines hidden matters, then it is metaphoric for sight.' End. (4) His saying: 'i.e. by his touching it in what...' etc., because these senses provide knowledge like a sighted person, so they stand in place of sight.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on the validity of sale and purchase by a blind person. The Hanafi position is that the sale and purchase of a blind person are valid, and the blind buyer has the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya). This option drops if the blind person touches, smells, or tastes the item, as these senses provide knowledge equivalent to sight for the blind. The passage addresses the objection of Imam al-Shafi'i, who held that a blind person's purchase is invalid because he cannot see the item. The Hanafi response is based on the practical necessity: the blind deal with others and do not die of hunger. Furthermore, if the blind cannot purchase, they would suffer harm. The option for the blind buyer is analogous to the option for a sighted buyer who purchases an unseen item. The passage also explains that touching before purchase drops the option, but after purchase, the option remains until some indication of satisfaction is shown.

Key Terms
أعمى (a'ma): A blind person, specifically one who cannot see. The text discusses the legal capacity of such a person in sales.
جس (jass): Touching with the hand to examine an object. For the blind, this serves as a means of recognition equivalent to sight.
خيار (khiyar): Option; specifically khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) which allows the buyer to rescind upon seeing the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how does the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) drop for a blind buyer?
A) By seeing the item with his eyes.
B) By touching, smelling, or tasting the item.
C) By verbal acceptance only.
D) It never drops.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates the sale and purchase of a blind person, granting an option of inspection that drops through tactile, olfactory, or gustatory examination, based on necessity and established practice.

Dispute on Seeing Inside the House When Buying
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-ru'yah in house purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on seeing the interior of a house for sale and explain why Zufar's contrary view is a difference of legal reasoning, not a change in custom.

Core Idea
The three imams sufficed with exterior view; Zufar's dissent is a matter of proof, not time.

In the Hanafi school, the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) allows a buyer to cancel a sale after seeing the object. A common question is whether seeing the interior of a house is required. This passage resolves a misconception: Zufar's view requiring interior inspection is not due to changing building styles but to a legitimate difference in legal reasoning. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the relied-upon position in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is purchasing a house. The ruling concerns what level of inspection satisfies the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). According to the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani), seeing the exterior alone is sufficient.
The ruling is that the sale is valid and no option remains for the interior after exterior view.
2
Locate the dispute and the parties
Zufar, a Hanafi jurist, held that the buyer must also see the interior. The passage clarifies that he was a contemporary of the three imams, so the difference is not due to changing times.
Both sides are within the Hanafi school — this is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the basis for the majority view
The three imams reasoned that houses in their time were built uniformly, so the exterior gives sufficient knowledge of the interior. Thus, requiring interior inspection would be unnecessary hardship.
This is a talil (legal reasoning) based on custom (urf) and practicality.
4
Recognize the nature of the ikhtilaf
The commentator states this is 'ikhtilaf hujja wa burhan' — a difference of proof and evidence. Zufar's view is valid but counterbalanced by the stronger proof of the majority. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The student should note that ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams is a mercy and both positions are part of the school's heritage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشُرِط رؤية داخلِ الدَّارِ اليوم. واعلم أنَّ زفره لما قال باشتراط رؤيةِ داخل الدارِ فظنَّ بعضُهم أنَّ هذا اختلاف زمان، والحال أنّ أئمتنا الثلاثة اكتفوا برؤية خارج الدَّارِ ولم يشترطوا رؤية داخلها ؛ لكون الدور في زمانهم غير متفاوتة، و زفر الله كان في زمانهم، فقد خالفهم، فعُلِمَ أنه قائل باشتراط رؤية داخلها وإن لم يتفاوت فكان هذا اختلاف حجة وبرهان.
And it is conditioned to see the inside of the house today. And know that Zufar, when he said requiring seeing the inside of the house, some thought this was a difference of time. However, our three imams sufficed with seeing the outside of the house and did not require seeing its inside because houses in their time were not variable, and Zufar was in their time, so he opposed them. Thus it is known that he is a proponent of requiring seeing the inside even if it is not variable, so this was a difference of proof and evidence.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement of seeing the interior of a house before purchase. The text notes that some thought Zufar's position was due to a difference in the types of houses in his era, but the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) ruled that seeing the outside suffices because houses in their time were uniform in construction. Zufar, however, required seeing inside regardless. The commentator clarifies that this is a genuine disagreement based on legal reasoning, not a change in custom.

Key Terms
رؤية (ru'yah): Visual inspection required for khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Seeing both the interior and exterior
B) Seeing only the exterior
C) Seeing only the interior
D) Neither, description is enough

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, seeing the outside of a house suffices for the option of inspection; Zufar's dissent is a matter of legal proof, not custom.

Sale by a Blind Person: Validity and Option
Ruling with proof on blind person's sale and khiyar al-ru'yah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a blind person's sale and purchase, and explain the Shafi'i disagreement and the Hanafi rebuttal.

Core Idea
A blind person's sale is valid with the option of inspection, which falls by other senses.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: can a person who cannot see engage in trade? The Hanafi school affirms its validity, while al-Shafi'i disagrees. The commentator refutes the Shafi'i position with evidence from practice and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State the Hanafi ruling on the sale/purchase of a blind person: it is valid (sahih). The matn and commentary affirm this clearly.
2
Locate the proof
The Hanafi proof is the continuous practice of dealing with blind people without disapproval (sukut al-sahabah), and the principle of necessity (darurah) to avoid starvation.
3
Note the ikhtilaf
Al-Shafi'i holds that a blind person's purchase is invalid because he cannot see the item, citing the prohibition of selling unseen goods.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal
The commentator refutes al-Shafi'i by pointing out that blindness does not prevent knowledge via other senses (touch, smell, taste), and that prohibiting such transactions causes undue hardship.
5
Understand the khiyar detail
The blind buyer has the option (khiyar al-ru'yah). Before purchase, it falls by touching, smelling, or tasting the item. After purchase, the option remains until he indicates satisfaction by word or action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيع الأعمى وشراؤه صح، وله الخيار مشترياً، ويسقط بجسه المبيع، وشمه، وذوقه. قوله : صح؛ وقال الشافعي الله : لا يصح شراؤه ؛ لأنه لا يجوز شراء ما لم يره، وقيل: إن كان بصيراً فعمي فهو كالبصير عند الشَّافِعِي له، وإن كان أكمه فلا يجوز بيعه وشراؤه أصلاً ؛ لأنه لا يعرف ألوان الأشياء وصفتها. كذا في «الكفاية». وهذا القول محجوج بمعاملة العميان من غير نكير، وبأنَّ مِن أصله أن مَن لا يملك الشراء لا يملك الأمر به، فإذا احتاج الأعمى إلى ما يأكل، ولا يتمكن من شراء المأكول، ولا التوكيل به مات جوعاً، وفيه من القبح ما لا يخفي. كذا في «العناية» قوله : وله الخيار مشترياً ؛ لأنَّ شراء ما لم يره جائز، وللمشتري الخيار والأعمى كالبصير الذي يشتري ما لم يره فيجوز شراؤه مع ثبوت الخيار له كالبصير. قوله : بجسه؛ وهذا إذا كان قبل الشراء، وأما إذا كان بعده فإن خياره لا يسقط باتفاق الروايات بل يمتد إلى أن يوجد ما يدلُّ على الرضاء من قول أو فعل على الصحيح. كذا في كمال الدراية () والجس: بسودن وبراسيدن. كذا في «الصراح»، وفي «المصباح»: «جسه بيدِهِ جساً من باب قتل، واجتسه ليتَعَرَّفُه ، وجس الأخبار وتجسسها : تتبعها، ومنه الجاسوس ؛ لأنه يتتبع الأخبار ويفحص عن بواطن الأمور، ثمّ استعير لنظر العين». انتهى قوله : أي بجسه فيما... الخ ؛ لأنَّ هذه تُفيد العلم كالبصير فتقوم مقام الرؤية.
And the sale and purchase of a blind person is valid, and he has the option as a buyer, and it falls by his touching the sold item, smelling it, and tasting it. His saying: 'valid'; and al-Shafi'i said: his purchase is not valid because it is not permissible to purchase what one has not seen. And it is said: if he was sighted and became blind, he is like a sighted person according to al-Shafi'i; but if he is blind from birth, his sale and purchase are not valid at all because he does not know colors and qualities of things. This is in al-Kifayah. And this statement is refuted by the dealing of blind people without disapproval, and by the principle that one who cannot purchase cannot command it, so if a blind person needs to eat and cannot buy food or appoint an agent, he would die of hunger, and there is obvious ugliness in that. This is in al-Inayah. His saying: 'and he has the option as a buyer' because purchasing what one has not seen is permissible, and the buyer has the option, and the blind person is like a sighted person who buys what he has not seen: his purchase is valid with the option established for him like the sighted person. His saying: 'by his touching' – this applies if it is before the purchase; but if after, his option does not fall by consensus of the narrations, rather it continues until something indicating satisfaction appears by word or action, according to the correct view. This is in Kamal al-Dirayah. And 'al-jass': in Persian 'bāsūdan wa barāsīdan'. This is in al-Sirah, and in al-Misbah: 'he touched it with his hand, jassan, from the verb qatala, and ijtassahu to know it; and touching news and spying: following them; from it is the spy because he follows news and investigates hidden matters; then it was metaphorically used for the sight of the eye.' End. His saying: 'i.e. by his touching in what... etc.' because these senses give knowledge like the sighted, so they stand in place of sight.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the validity of sale by a blind person. The matn states that the sale and purchase of a blind person is valid, he has the option of inspection as a buyer, and that option falls by touching, smelling, or tasting the item before purchase. The commentator then explains the disagreement with al-Shafi'i, who invalidates the blind person's purchase because he cannot see. The Hanafi rebuttal cites the continuous practice of dealing with blind people without objection, and the logical consequence that prohibiting it would lead to starvation. The commentator also clarifies that if the blind person touches after purchase, the option remains until he expresses satisfaction. The passage ends with lexical notes on 'jass' (touching). This is a clear instance of inter-madhhab dispute and intra-Hanafi clarification.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): Option to rescind the contract upon seeing the item.
جس (jass): Touching with the hand to ascertain the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only by seeing the item
B) By hearing a description
C) By touching, smelling, or tasting
D) It never falls

Key Takeaway
A blind person's sale is valid with the option of inspection, which can be exercised by other senses; necessity supports this ruling.

Describing Immovable Property for a Blind Buyer
Ruling on describing real estate to a blind buyer to remove option

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on describing real estate to a blind buyer and differentiate between the views of Abu Yusuf and Hasan ibn Ziyad regarding its implementation.

Core Idea
Description suffices for the blind buyer's option to fall; sight is replaced by accurate description when seeing is impossible.

This lesson examines the ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) when the buyer is blind and the item is real estate. The Hanafi school holds that description of location and boundaries removes the option, as seeing is physically impossible. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf rejects the need to place the blind person as if seeing, while a narration from Abu Hanifa (via Hasan ibn Ziyad) suggests appointing an agent. Understanding these positions clarifies how the school accommodates disability in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
From the matn, determine the primary method for a blind buyer to lose his khiyar al-ru'yah: description of the property (location, boundaries, etc.).
2
Distinguish the intra-Hanafi positions
Contrast Abu Yusuf's view (no need for hypothetical standing) with Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration from Abu Hanifa (appoint an agent to see and take possession).
Both are within the school; the mufta bihi is the simpler description method.
3
Analyze the analogical justification
The commentator uses analogies: moving lips for the mute in prayer, passing a razor for the bald pilgrim. Understand how these substitutes function when the original act is impossible.
This shows the principle of 'necessity allows substitution' in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply edge cases
Consider what happens if the blind buyer regains sight (no option) or if a sighted buyer becomes blind (option transfers to description).
These cases reinforce that the option is tied to the ability to see, and description is a permanent substitute for the blind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ويوصف العقار) : ولا اعتبار لوقوفه في مكان لو كان بصيراً لراه، كما هو قول أبي يوسف . قوله : ويوصف العقار ؛ فيما إذا كان المبيع عقاراً ؛ وذلك بأن تبيَّن محلته وحدوده وغير ذلك مما يحتاجُ في معرفته ؛ لأنه لا سبيل إلى معرفته إلا به، حتى يسقط خياره بعد ذلك. قوله : كما هو... الخ ؛ فقد رُوي عنه أنه اشترط في وصف العقار أن يوقف الأعمى في مكان لو كان بصيراً لرآه منه ، ثم يذكر له أوصافه ؛ لأنَّ التشبيه يقامُ كمقام الحقيقة عند العجز عنها : كتحريكك الشَّفتين أقيم في حق الأخرس مقام القراءة في الصلوات وإجراء الموسَى على رأس المحرم في الحج أو العمرة أقيم للذي لا شعر له مقام الحلق عند التحلل. وقال الحسن بن زياد الله وهو رواية عن أبي حنيفة : يوكل وكيلاً بقبضه له ويراه، وهو أشبه بقول أبي حنيفة الله ؛ لأنَّ رؤية الوكيل بالقبض كرؤية الموكل عنده ، ولو وصف له ثمَّ أَبْصَرَ فلا خيار له ؛ لتمام العقد وسقوط الخيار، ولو اشترى ما لم يره ثمَّ عَمِي ينتقل الخيار إلى الوصف ؛ لأنَّ النَّقل من الرؤية إلى الوصف للعجز، وقد تحقق، كذا في كمال الدراية).
And immovable property is described: And his standing in a place from which he would see if sighted is not considered, as is the statement of Abu Yusuf. His saying: 'and immovable property is described' – when the sold item is real estate; that is by clarifying its location, boundaries, and other necessary information, because there is no way to know it except by that, so that his option falls after that. His saying: 'as is... etc.' – it is transmitted from him that he required in describing real estate to stop the blind person in a place from which he would see if sighted, then mention its characteristics to him, because the simile takes the place of reality when unable: like moving the lips takes the place of recitation for the mute in prayers, and passing a razor over the head of a pilgrim substitutes for shaving for one who has no hair during ihram. And al-Hasan ibn Ziyad said, which is a narration from Abu Hanifa: he should appoint an agent to take possession for him and see it, and this is closer to the statement of Abu Hanifa because the sight of the agent for the purpose of taking possession is like the sight of the principal. And if described to him and then he became sighted, he has no option because the contract is complete and the option falls. And if he bought what he had not seen and then became blind, the option transfers to the description because the transfer from sight to description is due to inability, which has occurred. This is in Kamal al-Dirayah.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage discusses the method of describing immovable property for a blind buyer to satisfy the requirement of khiyar al-ru'yah. The matn states that the property is described (by specifying location, boundaries, etc.) and that it is not necessary for the blind person to stand in a place where he would see it if sighted, according to Abu Yusuf. The commentator explains that description is sufficient to remove the option. He also mentions a stricter view from another transmission requiring the blind person to be placed as if seeing, analogizing to other legal substitutes in prayer and Hajj. He then cites Hasan ibn Ziyad's narration that the blind person should appoint an agent to see and take possession, which is closer to Abu Hanifa's view. Finally, he addresses edge cases: if the blind buyer later regains sight, no option remains; if a sighted buyer becomes blind, the option transfers to description.

Key Terms
عقار (aqar): Immovable property (real estate).
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of inspection, allowing the buyer to cancel the contract upon seeing the purchased item if it is not as expected.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Returning Both Items When One Seen in a Bundle
Ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah in multi-item sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on returning items in a bundle when only one was seen before purchase, and explain why returning only the unseen item is not permitted.

Core Idea
In a single transaction, you must return the entire bundle—not just the unseen item—to avoid splitting the contract.

This lesson examines a common sale scenario: a buyer sees one of two garments, purchases both, and later sees the second. Should the buyer be allowed to return only the one he did not see? The Hanafi school answers with a clear prohibition based on the principle of 'tafriq al-safqah' (splitting the transaction). Let's explore the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer sees one of two garments in a bundle, then purchases both without seeing the other.
This is a case of khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection) for the unseen item.
2
State the primary ruling
The buyer may return both garments if he wishes, because seeing one does not give knowledge of the other.
The option continues for the unseen item.
3
Identify the restriction
The buyer cannot return only the unseen garment; he must return both or keep both.
4
Understand the legal basis
Returning only one would split the single transaction (tafriq al-safqah), which is prohibited by a hadith.
The transaction is considered one unit until full acceptance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ومن رأى أحدَ الثَّوبين ثمَّ شراهما ، ثُمَّ رأى الآخر، فلهُ ردُّهُما لا رَدّ الآخر وحده ، لئلا يلزم تفريق الصفقة قبل التمام.) قوله : فله ردُّهما ... الخ ; أي له أن يردهما إن شاء ؛ لأنَّ رؤية أحدهما لا تُغْنِي عن رؤية الآخر؛ لأنَّ الثياب تكون متفاوتة، فبقي خياره فيما لم يره ، وليس له أن يرده وحده ، لما نهى رسول الله الله عن تفريق الصفقة ) ، وفي رد أحدهما دون الآخرِ تفريقُ الصفقة، فيردهما جميعاً ضرورة. قوله : لئلا يلزم ... الخ ; وتقرير اللزوم : أنَّ الصفقة لا تتم إلا بتمام الرضاء، وقد أشار إلى حديث النهي عن تفريق الصفقة.
And (one who saw one of two garments, then bought both, then saw the other, he may return both, not only the other, so as to avoid splitting the transaction before completion.) His saying: 'he may return both...' i.e., he may return them if he wishes, because seeing one does not suffice for the other, since garments may vary; so his option remains for what he did not see. And he may not return only the one, because the Prophet forbade splitting the transaction; and returning one without the other splits the transaction, so he must return both necessarily. His saying: 'so as to avoid...' and the explanation of necessity: the transaction is not complete except with complete satisfaction, and he indicated the hadith forbidding splitting the transaction.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on khiyar al-ru'yah in a multi-item sale. If a buyer sees one of two garments, then buys both, and later sees the second garment, he is not allowed to return only the unseen one. Instead, he must return both if he wishes to exercise his option. The reason is that the contract is a single transaction (safqah); returning part of it before full acceptance amounts to splitting the transaction, which is prohibited by a hadith. The commentary emphasizes that seeing one garment does not give knowledge of the other, so the option remains for the unseen item, but to avoid partial rescission of a single contract, the entire bundle must be returned.

Key Terms
صفقة (safqah): A single transaction or bargain involving multiple items.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting or dividing a single transaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the seller requires it
B) To avoid splitting the transaction without full consent
C) Because both garments are identical
D) Because the option applies to each item separately

Key Takeaway
A buyer who sees only part of a bundle must return the entire bundle if he wishes to exercise the option of inspection, to avoid splitting the contract.
