Khiyar al-Ru'ya: The Option of Inspection and Its Proofs
Expansion of khiyar al-ru'ya ruling with conditions and proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for khiyar al-ru'ya and apply the burden of proof rules when the buyer and seller dispute change of the item.

Core Idea
A buyer who saw an item has the option to return it only if he finds it changed — and the burden of proof shifts with time.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya expands on the Hanafi ruling of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection). It clarifies the conditions under which the buyer may return goods after seeing them, the evidentiary rules when the seller denies change, and the textual proof from the hadith. The commentary also resolves a potential conflict with the prohibition of separating a transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling and its proof
Locate the main statement: the buyer has the option if he finds the item changed, otherwise not. Then find the hadith cited as evidence.
The hadith: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it.'
2
Determine the conditions for the option to apply
From the commentary, extract the two conditions: (1) the buyer must have seen the item with the intention to purchase at that time, and (2) he must be aware at the time of contract that he had seen it previously.
If the buyer saw without purchase intent, the option remains because his examination was incomplete.
3
Analyze the burden of proof regarding change
Note the two scenarios based on time: if the period between sight and purchase is short, the seller's denial of change is accepted with his oath; if long, the buyer's claim is accepted.
The mufta bihi position: buyer's word if change is likely, as per al-Sarakhsi and Qadi Khan.
4
Understand the nature of the return
The return under khiyar al-ru'ya is a rescission from the beginning (faskh min al-ibtida'), not a new sale, so no need for court order or seller's consent.
This contrasts with other options where consent or court may be needed.
5
Resolve the potential conflict with the hadith on separating a transaction
The author argues that the prohibition of separating a sale is overridden by the specific hadith granting the option, since prohibition (haram) takes precedence over permission.
Thus returning one of two items purchased together under khiyar al-ru'ya is allowed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن رأى شيئاً ثمَّ شراهُ خَيْرَ إن وجَدَهُ مُتَغَيِّراً وإلا لا، والقول للبائع في عدم تغيره، وللمشتري في عدم رؤيته) وبعده والرضاء لا يتم قبل الرؤية، فالصفقة لا تتم مع خيار الرؤية قبل القبض مطلقاً، إذا قبضه مستوراً ؛ وبهذا يمكن للمشتري من رد المبيع بغير قضاء القاضي ولا رضاء البائع. ويكون الردّ بصورة خيار الرؤيةِ فسخ من الابتداء ؛ لأنَّ الرضاء لم يتحقق بجهل صفاتِ المعقود عليه فلا حاجة إلى القضاء ولا إلى الرّضاء، وإن اختلج في قلبك أنَّ النهي عن تفريق الصفقة كما ورد في الحديث كذلك ولاية رد أحد الثوبين الذي لم يره ثابت بالحديث الآخر وهو قوله : «من اشترى شيئاً لم يره فله الخيار إذا رآه» لأنّ النهي محرّم، والمحرّم راجح على المبيح. [1] قوله : ومن رأى شيئاً ؛ أي قاصداً لشرائه عند رؤيته عالماً بأنه مرتيه وقت الشراء، ثم شراء بعد مُدَّةٍ خُيّر إن وجده متغيّراً عن الصفة التي رآه عليها قبل ذلك ؛ لأنَّ العقد وقع على مجهول الوصف ؛ لأنَّ تلك الرؤيةِ لم تقع معلمة بأوصافه فكأنه لم يره وإنما قيدنا قاصداً لشرائه عند رؤيته ؛ لأنه لو رآه لا لقصدِ الشراء ثم اشتراه فله الخيار؛ لأنه إذا رأى لا لقصد الشراء لا يتأمل كل التأمل فلم يقع معرفته. كذا في «البحر» (1) وإنما قيّدنا : عالماً بأنه مرئيّة وقت الشراء ؛ لأنه لو لم يعلم عند العقد أنه رآه من قبل، فحينئذ ثبت له الخيار بعدم الرضاء. كذا في «الرمز» (" ). ٢١) قوله : وإلا ؛ وإن لم يتغيّر ذلك الشيء عن الصفقة التي رآه عليها لا ؛ أي لا يُخَيَّر ؛ لأنَّ العلم بالمبيع قد حصل بالرؤية السابقة وقد رَضي به ما دام على تلك الصفة. أي إذا اشترى شيئا قد رآه ، فقال البائع : إنَّه لم يتغير حتى لا يكون لك الخيار ، فالقولُ للبائع " مع حلفه ، ولو قال المشتري لم أره ، ولي الخيار، فالقولُ للمشتري" مع الحلف. [١] قوله : حتى لا يكون ... الخ ؛ يعني إن التغير لم يقع في المبيع، ويثبت الخيار لك إذا تغير فلا يكون لك الخيار. [٢] قوله : فالقول ؛ لأنَّ التغيُّرَ عارض وحادث ، فإنّه إنما يكون بعيب، وكل منهما عارض والمشتري يدعيه، والبائعُ يُنْكِرُه ويتمسك بالأصل ؛ فإنَّ سبب لزوم العقد وهو رؤية جزء من المعقود عليه أو الرؤية السابقة، أو البيع البات الخالي عن الشروط المفسدة على اختلاف الأقوال ظاهر، والأصل لزوم العقد، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه ، وهذا إذا كانت المدة قريبة. أما إذا كانت بعيدة فالقولُ للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر شاهد له، ألا ترى أن الجارية الشَّابة لا تبقى شابة بعد عشرين سنة، وفي «الذخيرة»: إنَّه لا يُصَدَّق المشتري في دعوى التغيير إلا بحجة إلا إذا طالت المدة. وقال الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له : إذا رأى ثم اشترى بعد زمان يتغير فيه ذلك الشيء غالباً، كان القــول للمشتري ، قال قاضي خان : عليه الفتوى. كذا ذكره العلامة البرْجَنْدِي في «شرح النُّقاية». (۳) قوله : فالقولُ للمشتري مع حلف ؛ لأنَّ البائع يدعي أمراً حادثاً، وهو العلم بالصفة، والمشتري ينكره ، فالقولُ له ، وقال في «البحر» (۱) : لو أراد المشتري أن يرده فأنكر البائع كون المردّ معيباً، فالقول للمشتري، وكذلك في خيار الشرط ؛ لأنه انفسخ العقد برده ، وبقي ملك البائع في يده، فيكون القولُ قول القابض في تعيين ملكه أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالمودع والغاصب، ولو اختلفا في الرد بالعيب فالقولُ للبائع.
And whoever saw a thing then purchased it, he has the option if he finds it changed, otherwise not. And the word is the seller's regarding its not changing, and the buyer's regarding his not seeing it. And after that, consent is not completed before sight, so the transaction is not completed with the option of inspection before possession absolutely, if he takes possession of it hidden. And by this, the buyer can return the sold item without a judge's order or the seller's consent. And the return in the form of the option of inspection is a rescission from the beginning, because consent was not achieved due to ignorance of the attributes of the object of contract, so there is no need for a judge or consent. And if it occurs to your heart that the prohibition of separating a transaction as mentioned in the hadith, likewise the authority to return one of the two garments that he did not see is established by another hadith, which is his statement: 'Whoever buys something he has not seen has the option when he sees it' because the prohibition is haram, and haram takes precedence over what is permissible. [1] His statement: 'And whoever saw a thing' i.e. intending to purchase it at the time of seeing, knowing that he had seen it at the time of purchase, then purchased after a period, he has the option if he finds it changed from the description he saw it upon before that, because the contract occurred on an unknown description, because that sight did not inform of its attributes, so it is as if he did not see it. And we restricted it to intending to purchase at the time of seeing, because if he saw it not intending to purchase then bought it, he has the option, because if he saw not intending to purchase, he does not examine fully, so his knowledge did not occur. Thus in al-Bahr. And we restricted it to knowing that he had seen it at the time of purchase, because if he did not know at the contract that he had seen it before, then the option is established for him due to lack of consent. Thus in al-Ramz. [2] His statement: 'And if not' i.e. if that thing did not change from the description he saw it upon, then no option, because knowledge of the sold item was obtained by the prior sight and he consented as long as it is on that description. (From page 73) i.e. if he bought something he had seen, and the seller says: 'It did not change, so you have no option,' then the word is the seller's with his oath. And if the buyer says: 'I did not see it, and I have the option,' then the word is the buyer's with his oath. [1] His statement: 'so that there is no...' etc. means that the change did not occur in the sold item, and the option is established for you if it changes, so you have no option. [2] His statement: 'the word is the seller's' because change is an incidental and new occurrence, for it only occurs due to a defect, and each of them is incidental, and the buyer claims it, the seller denies it and adheres to the original. For the cause of the contract's binding is the sight of a part of the object or prior sight, or an absolute sale free from corrupt conditions according to varying opinions, and the original is the binding of the contract, and the word is the word of the denier with his oath. And this is if the period is short. But if it is long, then the word is the buyer's, because the apparent is evidence for him. Do you not see that a young female slave does not remain young after twenty years? And in al-Dhakhira, the buyer is not believed in claiming change without proof unless the period is long. And Imam al-Sarakhsi said: if he saw then bought after a time in which that thing usually changes, the word is the buyer's. Qadi Khan said: the fatwa is on this. Thus mentioned by al-Barjandi in Sharh al-Nuqaya. (3) His statement: 'the word is the buyer's with his oath' because the seller claims an incidental matter, which is knowledge of the attribute, and the buyer denies it, so the word is his. And it is said in al-Bahr: if the buyer wants to return it and the seller denies that the returned item is defective, the word is the buyer's, and similarly in the option of condition, because the contract is rescinded by his return, and the seller's ownership remains in his hand, so the word is the word of the possessor in specifying his ownership, whether trustworthy or liable, like the depositary and the usurper. But if they disagree on return due to defect, the word is the seller's.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection). The author begins by stating the basic rule: a buyer who has seen an item and later purchases it has the option to return if he finds it changed from the state in which he saw it. If it is unchanged, he has no option. The author then explains the burden of proof: the seller's word is taken regarding no change, while the buyer's word is taken regarding not having seen it. The author then elaborates on the nature of this option, explaining that consent based on sight is essential, and the contract is not fully concluded until the buyer sees the item. The option allows the buyer to return the item without a court order or the seller's consent, as the return constitutes a rescission of the contract ab initio. The author addresses a potential objection: the prohibition of separating a transaction (e.g., selling two items together and then returning one) is overridden by the hadith granting the option upon sight, as prohibition (haram) takes precedence over permission. The author then provides detailed commentary on the conditions for khiyar al-ru'ya: the buyer must have seen the item with the intention to purchase at that time, and he must be aware at the time of contract that he had seen it previously. If he saw it without purchase intention, he retains the option because his examination may have been incomplete. The commentary further discusses the burden of proof regarding change: if the period between sight and purchase is short, the seller's denial of change is accepted with his oath; if the period is long, the buyer's claim of change is accepted because the apparent indicator (zahir) supports him. The author cites al-Sarakhsi and Qadi Khan for the relied-upon position that if the time is such that change is likely, the buyer's word is taken. Finally, the author extends the same principle to other scenarios, such as return due to defect or option of condition.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of inspection: the buyer's right to return goods after seeing them if they are not as expected.
القول للبائع / للمشتري (al-qawlu lil-bā'i'i / lil-mushtarī): The burden of proof: whose sworn statement is taken in a dispute.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when does the seller's word regarding no change prevail?
A) Always, regardless of time
B) When the period between sight and purchase is short
C) When the period is long
D) Never

Key Takeaway
The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'ya) is a buyer's right to return goods if they have changed from what was seen, with the burden of proof shifting based on the time elapsed.

Selling Part of a Bale Before Possession
Ruling on selling part of a purchased bale before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling part of a purchased bale and identify the prerequisite of possession for valid disposal.

Core Idea
You cannot sell what you do not possess – possession of the whole is required before part can be sold.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the sale of goods in bulk. The text presents a case where a buyer purchases a bale of cloth and then wishes to sell a single garment from it. The key condition is that the buyer must have taken possession of the entire bale before any part can be validly sold. This reinforces the fundamental Hanafi principle that one cannot dispose of goods before taking possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its category
The act is selling a garment from a bale that was previously purchased. This falls under the chapter of sales (buyu).
2
Identify the hukm level
The text implies that such a sale is sahih (valid) when the condition is met.
3
Identify the condition
The condition is possession (qabd) of the entire bale by the buyer.
4
Note the consequence of missing the condition
Without possession, the sale or gift would be invalid (batil).
5
Relate to the general principle
This is an application of the rule that one cannot sell what one does not possess (al-mas'alah al-mashhurah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومَن شرى عِدْلَ زُلِّي وَقَبَضَهُ فباعَ منه ثوباً [٤]قوله : وقبضه ؛ إنّما قيَّد القبض ؛ لأنَّ ذلك العدل لو لم يكن مقبوضاً لم يصح تصرُّف المشتري فيه ببيع أو هبة مع التسليم.
And whoever buys a bale of zulli and takes possession of it, then sells a garment from it. [4] His statement: 'and takes possession of it' – indeed he restricted it to possession because if that bale were not possessed, the buyer's disposal of it by sale or gift with delivery would not be valid.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents a specific ruling regarding khiyar al-ru'ya. The matn states that if someone buys a bale of cloth (zulli) and takes possession of it, he may then sell a garment from that bale. The commentary clarifies the reason for the condition of possession: without prior possession of the entire bale, the buyer cannot validly dispose of any part of it by sale or gift because he does not have legal control over the goods. This aligns with the general Hanafi principle that a person cannot sell what he does not possess (the prohibition of selling before possession). The ruling underscores that possession is a prerequisite for valid transfer of ownership.

Key Terms
عدل زلي (ʿidl zullī): A bale of a type of cloth (zulli).
قبض (qabḍ): Physical or constructive possession; taking control of the purchased goods.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why must the buyer take possession of the entire bale before selling a garment from it?
A) Because the seller owns it until possession
B) Because the buyer cannot validly sell what he does not possess
C) Because the bale is not specified
D) Because the price is not paid

Key Takeaway
Possession of the entire purchased bale is a prerequisite for validly selling any part of it.

Burden of Proof in Khiyar al-Ru'yah: Who is Believed When Change is Claimed?
Ruling with proof: burden of proof in khiyar al-ru'yah change claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who bears the burden of proof when the buyer claims the goods have changed after inspection, and apply the distinction between short and long periods.

Core Idea
Seller believed on oath for short periods; buyer believed for long periods due to apparent change.

The option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) protects a buyer who purchases unseen goods by allowing return upon sight. But what happens when the buyer claims the goods have changed since inspection? The Hanafi school provides detailed rules on whose word is accepted. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the default principle and its exception when enough time has passed for natural change to occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
When the seller denies change, his word is accepted with oath. This is because the original principle is that the contract is binding, and change is an accidental claim.
The default applies when the period since inspection is short.
2
Understand the rationale
Change is a new occurrence (عارض). The seller denies it and adheres to the original binding that resulted from sight. The denier's word is generally preferred.
This is based on the maxim 'al-asl baqa' ma kana'.
3
Note the exception for long periods
If the period is long enough that goods typically change, the buyer's word is accepted because the apparent circumstance (الظاهر) supports his claim.
The text uses the example of a young slave girl not remaining young after twenty years as an analogy.
4
Examine the qiyas (analogy)
The slave girl analogy shows that after a long time, change is probable. This shifts the burden of proof to the seller, who must prove no change occurred.
The qiyas is not a strict legal analogy but a common-sense inference.
5
Recognize the fatwa authority
Qadi Khan and al-Sarakhsi support the buyer's word for long periods, and this is the relied-upon fatwa.
Al-Barjandi cites this in Sharh al-Nuqayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية ومَن شرى عِدْلَ زُلِّي وَقَبَضَهُ فباعَ منه ثوباً أي إذا اشترى شيئا قد رآه ، فقال البائع : إنَّه لم يتغير حتى لا يكون لك الخيار ، فالقولُ للبائع مع حلفه ، ولو قال المشتري لم أره ، ولي الخيار، فالقولُ للمشتري مع الحلف. (ومن شرى عِدْلَ زُلِّي وَقَبَضَهُ فباعَ منه ثوباً [١] قوله : حتى لا يكون ... الخ ؛ يعني إن التغير لم يقع في المبيع، ويثبت الخيار لك إذا تغير فلا يكون لك الخيار. [٢] قوله : فالقول ؛ لأنَّ التغيُّرَ عارض وحادث ، فإنّه إنما يكون بعيب، وكل منهما عارض والمشتري يدعيه، والبائعُ يُنْكِرُه ويتمسك بالأصل ؛ فإنَّ سبب لزوم العقد وهو رؤية جزء من المعقود عليه أو الرؤية السابقة، أو البيع البات الخالي عن الشروط المفسدة على اختلاف الأقوال ظاهر، والأصل لزوم العقد، والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه ، وهذا إذا كانت المدة قريبة. أما إذا كانت بعيدة فالقولُ للمشتري ؛ لأنَّ الظاهر شاهد له، ألا ترى أن الجارية الشَّابة لا تبقى شابة بعد عشرين سنة، وفي «الذخيرة»: إنَّه لا يُصَدَّق المشتري في دعوى التغيير إلا بحجة إلا إذا طالت المدة. وقال الإمامُ السَّرَخْسِي له : إذا رأى ثم اشترى بعد زمان يتغير فيه ذلك الشيء غالباً، كان القــول للمشتري ، قال قاضي خان : عليه الفتوى. كذا ذكره العلامة البرْجَنْدِي في «شرح النُّقاية». (۳) قوله : فالقولُ للمشتري مع حلف ؛ لأنَّ البائع يدعي أمراً حادثاً، وهو العلم بالصفة، والمشتري ينكره ، فالقولُ له ، وقال في «البحر» (۱) : لو أراد المشتري أن يرده فأنكر البائع كون المردّ معيباً، فالقول للمشتري، وكذلك في خيار الشرط ؛ لأنه انفسخ العقد برده ، وبقي ملك البائع في يده، فيكون القولُ قول القابض في تعيين ملكه أميناً كان أو ضميناً كالمودع والغاصب، ولو اختلفا في الرد بالعيب فالقولُ للبائع. [٤]قوله : وقبضه ؛ إنّما قيَّد القبض ؛ لأنَّ ذلك العدل لو لم يكن مقبوضاً لم يصح تصرُّف المشتري فيه ببيع أو هبة مع التسليم. أو وهبَ ثوباً وسَلَّم لم يَرُدَّهُ بخيار رؤية أو شرط، بل يعيبو) : الرُّط : جيل من النَّاسِ في سَوادِ العراق، والثَّوبُ الزُّطْيِّ يُنسب إليهم، والأصل فيه أن رَدَّ البعض يُوجِبُ تفريق الصفقة للملك. [١] قوله : وسلم ؛ قيّد بالتسليم ؛ لأنَّ الهبة بدون التسليم غير لازمةٍ وغير مفيدة [٢] قوله : لم يرده بخيار رؤية أو شرط ؛ لأنَّ الردّ تعذر فيما أخرجه عن ملكه ، ولا يمكنه أن يردّ الباقي بخيار الرؤية والشرط ، سواء كان قبل القبض أو بعده، لما فيه من تفريق الصفقة قبل التمام وفي خيار العيب يملك ؛ لأنّ التفريق بعد القبض ، فلو عاد إليه بسبب هو الفسخُ فهو على خيار الرؤية ، لارتفاع المانع من الردّ ، وهو تفريق الصفقة، وعن أبي يوسف ه : إنه لا يعود، وعليه اعتمدَ القَدُوري، كذا في «الهداية» (۱) ، و«الرمز» [٣] قوله : الزط ؛ في «الصراح»: كرو هي از مردم زطي يکي از ايشان، وفي «المنتخب»: يك صنف آدمي ست ، وفي منتهى الأرب»: كرو هي از هند معرب : جَتْ (٤) (۳) بالفتح، والقياس يقتضي فتح معربه أيضا زطي يكي ، وهكذا في «القاموس» وقال في «الكفاية ) : الرُّط : جيلُ من الهندِ ينسب إليهم الثياب الزُّطَيَّة. كذا في «المغرب») ، وقيل : جيل من النَّاسِ بسوادِ العراق. انتهى. فما قال الشارح له في تفسيره لم أجده في كتاب من كتب اللغة، والله أعلم. Book of Sale: Option of Inspection. Whoever buys a bundle of Zutti cloth and takes possession, then sells a garment from it: meaning if he bought something he had seen, and the seller says: 'It has not changed, so you have no option', then the seller's word is accepted with his oath. And if the buyer says: 'I did not see it, and I have the option', then the buyer's word is accepted with his oath. (Whoever buys a bundle of Zutti cloth and takes possession, then sells a garment from it: [1] His saying 'so that you have no option' means that the change did not occur in the sold item, and the option is established for you if it changed, so you have no option. [2] His saying 'the word' because change is an accidental and new occurrence, for it only happens due to a defect, and each [claim] is accidental and the buyer claims it while the seller denies it and adheres to the original [principle]; for the cause of the contract's binding—which is seeing a part of the object of sale or prior sight, or an outright sale free of invalidating conditions according to differing views—is apparent, and the original principle is the binding of the contract, and the word is that of the denier with his oath, and this is when the period is short. As for when it is long, then the buyer's word is accepted because the apparent circumstance witnesses for him; do you not see that a young slave girl does not remain young after twenty years. In al-Dhakhira: the buyer is not believed in his claim of change except with evidence unless the period is long. Imam al-Sarakhsi said: if he saw then bought after a time in which that thing usually changes, the word is the buyer's. Qadi Khan said: upon it is the fatwa. Thus mentioned by al-Allama al-Barjandi in Sharh al-Nuqayah. (3) His saying 'the buyer's word with his oath' because the seller claims a new matter, which is knowledge of the attribute, and the buyer denies it, so his word is accepted. And it is stated in al-Bahr: if the buyer wants to return it and the seller denies that the returned item is defective, the buyer's word is accepted, and likewise in khiyar al-shart because the contract is rescinded by the return and the seller's ownership remains in his hand, so the word is that of the possessor in determining his ownership, whether he is a trustee or a guarantor, like the depositary and the usurper. And if they differ regarding return due to defect, the seller's word is accepted. [4] His saying 'and he took possession': he restricted with possession because if that bundle were not taken possession, the buyer's disposal of it by sale or gift with delivery would not be valid. Or he gave a garment as a gift and delivered it, he does not return it by option of inspection or condition, but rather defects? The Zutt: a group of people in the Sawad of Iraq, and Zutti cloth is attributed to them. The principle is that returning part necessitates splitting the transaction regarding ownership. [1] His saying 'and delivered': he restricted with delivery because a gift without delivery is not binding and not beneficial. [2] His saying 'he does not return it by option of inspection or condition' because return is prevented regarding what he has removed from his ownership, and he cannot return the remainder by option of inspection or condition, whether before or after possession, due to the splitting of the transaction before completion. And in option of defect he can, because the splitting is after possession. So if it returns to him due to rescission, then he is upon the option of inspection, due to the removal of the impediment to return, which is splitting the transaction. And from Abu Yusuf: it does not return, and al-Quduri relied upon it, as in al-Hidayah and al-Ramz. [3] His saying 'al-Zutt': in al-Sirah: it is a people from Zutt, one of them. In al-Muntakhab: it is a kind of people. In Muntaha al-Arab: it is from Hind, arabicized from 'jat' with fatha, and the analogy requires that its arabicization also be 'zutti yaki'. And thus in al-Qamus. And in al-Kifayah: al-Zutt is a group from Hind to whom Zutti garments are attributed. Thus in al-Mughrib. And it is said: a group of people in the Sawad of Iraq. End. So what the commentator said in his interpretation I did not find in any book of language, and Allah knows best. [4] The saying 'group' (jayl) with kasr: a group of people. It is said: the Arabs are a jayl, the Turks are a jayl, the Chinese are a jayl. And it is said: every nation that specializes in a language is a jayl, its plural is ajyal. Thus in Muntaha al-Arab. [0] His saying 'al-safqah': in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'I struck his head with a slap' from the chapter of ضرب, from al-Hidayah (3:35), Ramz al-Haqa'iq (2:15), al-Qamus al-Muhit (2:375), al-Kifayah (5:545), al-Mughrib (p. 208).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the Hidayah, discussing rulings related to khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection). The passage begins by addressing a dispute between buyer and seller: if the seller claims the goods have not changed since inspection, his word is accepted with an oath, because the original principle is that the contract is binding. However, if the buyer claims he never saw the goods, his word is accepted. The commentary then provides the reasoning: change is an accidental occurrence, so the denier (seller) is favored when the period since inspection is short. If the period is long enough that goods typically change, the buyer's word is accepted because the apparent circumstance indicates change. This is supported by al-Sarakhsi and others, with Qadi Khan stating this is the relied-upon position. The passage then moves to the case of a bundle (adl) of Zutti cloth: if the buyer takes possession and sells a garment from it, he cannot return the remainder via khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart because selling part splits the transaction, which is an impediment to return. However, if the sold item later returns to the buyer through rescission (faskh), two views are reported: according to Abu Hanifa, the option returns because the impediment is removed; according to Abu Yusuf, it does not. Al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view, as stated in al-Hidayah. The passage also includes lexical notes on the term 'Zutt' (a people known for weaving cloth) and 'safqah' (transaction).

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The right of the buyer to rescind the contract upon actually seeing the item if it was not seen at the time of contract, provided no change has occurred.
عدل (adl): A bundle or bale of cloth, typically of Zutti fabric.
زط (zutt): A group of people originally from India, settled in the Sawad of Iraq, known for weaving a distinctive type of cloth (زطي).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario is the buyer's word accepted regarding change of goods?
A) When the seller admits the change.
B) When the period since inspection is short.
C) When the period since inspection is long.
D) When the buyer brings witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule balances contract binding with fairness: seller believed for short periods, buyer for long periods, using the presumption of natural change.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
10 min
Selling Part of the Goods and Its Effect on Khiyar al-Ru'yah: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling with proof: intra-Hanafi dispute on effect of selling part on khiyar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why selling part of a bundle prevents return of the remainder under khiyar al-ru'yah, and state the two Hanafi views on whether the option revives if the sold part is returned.

Core Idea
Selling part splits the transaction, blocking return; if the sold part returns, Abu Hanifa revives the option, Abu Yusuf does not.

When a buyer purchases a bundle of cloth unseen, he has the option to return the whole upon sight. But what if he sells one piece from the bundle before inspecting the rest? The Hanafi school holds that by selling part, he splits the transaction and loses the option for the remainder. A further dispute arises: if the sold piece later returns to the buyer through rescission, does the option revive? This passage presents the views of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, with the later fatwa following Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer buys a bundle (adl) of Zutti cloth, takes possession, then sells a garment from it. The question is whether he can return the remaining bundle via khiyar al-ru'yah.
The key element is that possession was taken before the sale.
2
Understand the impediment
Selling part splits the transaction (tafriq al-safqah), which prevents return of the remainder because the return would split the contract after partial completion.
This applies whether the sale was before or after possession.
3
Note the exception for khiyar al-'ayb
In khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect), splitting after possession does not block return because the defect option is based on a right that arose after possession.
The distinction shows that khiyar al-ru'yah is more sensitive to splitting.
4
Examine the dispute on return of option
If the sold piece returns to the buyer through rescission (faskh), Abu Hanifa says the option returns because the impediment (splitting) is removed. Abu Yusuf says it does not because the disposal was valid and its effect is permanent.
The text notes that al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is Abu Yusuf's: the option does not revive. This is stated in al-Hidayah and al-Ramz.
Thus, a buyer who sells part cannot later return the remainder even if the sold part comes back.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the Hidayah, discussing rulings related to khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection). The passage begins by addressing a dispute between buyer and seller: if the seller claims the goods have not changed since inspection, his word is accepted with an oath, because the original principle is that the contract is binding. However, if the buyer claims he never saw the goods, his word is accepted. The commentary then provides the reasoning: change is an accidental occurrence, so the denier (seller) is favored when the period since inspection is short. If the period is long enough that goods typically change, the buyer's word is accepted because the apparent circumstance indicates change. This is supported by al-Sarakhsi and others, with Qadi Khan stating this is the relied-upon position. The passage then moves to the case of a bundle (adl) of Zutti cloth: if the buyer takes possession and sells a garment from it, he cannot return the remainder via khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart because selling part splits the transaction, which is an impediment to return. However, if the sold item later returns to the buyer through rescission (faskh), two views are reported: according to Abu Hanifa, the option returns because the impediment is removed; according to Abu Yusuf, it does not. Al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view, as stated in al-Hidayah. The passage also includes lexical notes on the term 'Zutt' (a people known for weaving cloth) and 'safqah' (transaction).

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The right of the buyer to rescind the contract upon actually seeing the item if it was not seen at the time of contract, provided no change has occurred.
عدل (adl): A bundle or bale of cloth, typically of Zutti fabric.
زط (zutt): A group of people originally from India, settled in the Sawad of Iraq, known for weaving a distinctive type of cloth (زطي).
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting the transaction; refers to the act of separating the subject matter of a contract into parts, which can affect options and rights.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi view in the Hanafi school, if a buyer sells a garment from a bundle he bought on inspection, and then that garment returns to him through rescission, can he return the remaining bundle under khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) Yes, because the impediment is removed.
B) No, because the disposal was valid and its effect is permanent.
C) Yes, but only if the return was due to a defect.
D) No, unless the original seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Selling part of the goods extinguishes the option of inspection for the remainder, and the option does not revive per the relied-upon view of Abu Yusuf.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate

Ruling on Returning Part of a Gift Under Khiyar and the Ikhtilaf of Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-ru'yah after gift rescission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on returning a gifted item under khiyar and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
When a gift is rescinded, the right to khiyar al-ru'yah returns per Abu Hanifa but not per Abu Yusuf—mufta bihi follows Abu Yusuf.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether the donor's right to khiyar al-ru'yah revives when a gift is rescinded after the recipient found a defect. The key issue is the principle of tafriq al-safqah (splitting the contract) and how it applies before and after delivery. We will analyze the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and understand the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The donor cannot return part of a gifted item under khiyar al-ru'yah or khiyar al-shart before or after delivery because it would split the contract before completion (tafriq al-safqah). However, under khiyar al-'ayb, return is allowed because splitting occurs after possession.
The matn explicitly says 'lam yaruddahu' (does not return).
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf holds that even after the gift is rescinded (e.g., due to a defect), the right to khiyar al-ru'yah does not return. The text does not detail his reasoning but implies that once lost, the right does not revive.
al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The commentary states that if the gift returns to the donor due to rescission, the impediment (tafriq al-safqah) is removed, so the right to khiyar al-ru'yah reasserts (Abu Hanifa's position). However, the mufta bihi is that it does not return.
This is recorded in al-Hidayah and al-Ramz.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if a donor rescinds a gift after the recipient returns it due to a defect, the donor cannot reject the returned item based on khiyar al-ru'yah (following the mufta bihi). The donor must accept the item even if he hasn't seen it properly.
This affects how gifts with defects are handled.
5
Identify the tarjih
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Yusuf, as al-Quduri relied on it, and it is recorded in al-Hidayah. Therefore, the operative ruling for fatwa is that the right to khiyar al-ru'yah does not revive.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.

أو وهب ثوباً وسَلمَ لم يَرُدَّهُ بخيار رؤية أو شرط، بل يعيبو : الرُّط : جيل من النَّاسِ في سَوادِ العراق، والثَّوبُ الزُّطْيِّ يُنسب إليهم، والأصل فيه أن رَدَّ البعض يُوجِبُ تفريق الصفقة للملك.

[0]قوله : الصفقة ; قال في «المصباح المنير»: «صفقته على رأسه صفقاً من باب ضرب باليد، وصفقت له بالسعة صفقاً أيضاً : ضربت بيدي على يده ، وكان العرب إذا أوجب البيع ضرب أحدهما يده على يد صاحبه، ثم استعملت الصفقة في العقد، فقيل : بارك الله لك في صفقة يمينك، قال الأَزْهَرِيُّ : تكون الصفقة للبائع والمشتري». انتهى
Or if he gave a garment as a gift and delivered it, he did not return it due to an option of sight or condition, but it becomes defective: al-Zutt: a group of people in the Sawad of Iraq, and al-Zutti cloth is attributed to them. The basis in this is that returning part necessitates splitting the contract regarding ownership. [1] His saying: 'and delivered' is qualified by delivery because a gift without delivery is not binding nor beneficial. [2] His saying: 'he did not return it due to an option of sight or condition' because return is prevented in what he has removed from his ownership, and he cannot return the remainder due to option of sight or condition, whether before or after possession, due to splitting the contract before completion. And in the option of defect he can, because splitting after possession. So if it returns to him due to a cause that is rescission, then he is upon the option of sight because the impediment to return is removed, which is splitting the contract. And from Abu Yusuf: that it does not return, and al-Quduri relied on it, as in al-Hidayah and al-Ramz. [3] His saying: al-Zutt: in al-Sirah: like a group of people, one of them. In al-Muntakhab: a kind of people. In Muntaha al-Arab: like a people from India, arabicized 'jat' with fatha, and analogy requires fatha on its arabicized form also. And so in al-Qamus. And in al-Kifayah: al-Zutt: a people from India to whom al-Zutti garments are attributed. So in al-Mughrib. And it is said: a group of people in the Sawad of Iraq. End. What the commentator said in its explanation I did not find in any book of language, and Allah knows best. [4] His saying: jīl (with kasr): a group of people. It is said: Arabs are a jil, Turks are a jil, Chinese are a jil. And it is said: every nation specialized in a language is a jil, jīl is plural. So in Muntaha al-Arab. [0] His saying: al-safqah: in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'he struck his hand on his head, a strike from the category of striking with the hand, and I struck for him the sale, a strike also: I struck my hand on his hand. The Arabs, when concluding a sale, one would strike his hand on the hand of his companion, then safqah was used for the contract. It was said: May Allah bless you in the sale of your right hand. Al-Azhari said: al-safqah applies to both seller and buyer.' End.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins by quoting a matn line: 'Or if he gave a garment as a gift and delivered it, he did not return it due to an option of sight or condition, but it becomes defective.' The commentary first explains that the word 'al-Zutt' refers to a group of people in Iraq, and that the principle is that returning part of a sold item necessitates splitting the contract (tafriq al-safqah). The commentator then clarifies the matn: the condition 'delivered' is mentioned because a gift without delivery is not binding. The statement 'did not return it due to an option of sight or condition' means that the recipient cannot return the gifted item using these options because he has already taken ownership; returning part would split the contract before completion. However, under the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), he can return because splitting occurs after possession. If the gift is rescinded and comes back to the donor, the option of sight returns because the impediment (tafriq al-safqah) is removed. This is the position attributed to Abu Hanifa. The commentator then notes a difference: Abu Yusuf holds that the option of sight does not return in this case, and al-Quduri relied on Abu Yusuf's view. This is recorded in al-Hidayah and al-Ramz. The passage then provides lexical explanations of 'al-Zutt', 'jīl', and 'al-safqah' for clarification.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option for the buyer to cancel the sale after seeing the item if it does not meet expectations.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): The option for either party to cancel the sale within a stipulated period.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting a single contract by returning part of the sold item, which is generally disallowed before completion of the contract.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option for the buyer to return a defective item after discovery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for not allowing return of part of a gifted item under khiyar al-ru'yah?
A) It would cause splitting the contract before completion.
B) The gift is not binding without delivery.
C) The option of sight does not apply to gifts.
D) Abu Yusuf forbids it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mufta bihi is that khiyar al-ru'yah does not revive after a gift is rescinded, following Abu Yusuf.

The Effect of Khiyar Options on Contract Completion
Hanafi ta'lil on khiyar effects

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the effects of khiyar al-shart, khiyar al-ru'yah, and khiyar al-'ayb on the completion of a sales contract and apply the rule regarding returning part of a multi-item sale.

Core Idea
Khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah prevent complete consent, thus blocking completion of the contract entirely.

In Hanafi sales law, not all khiyar options are equal. Some options, like the option of condition (khiyar al-shart) and option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah), prevent the contract from becoming fully complete (tamam al-safqah) because they undermine complete mutual satisfaction. In contrast, the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) blocks completion only before delivery, after which the contract is considered final and the buyer's remedies are limited. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind these distinctions and their practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule and its scope
First, locate the matn line: 'وهو قبل التمام لا يجوز، وبعد التمام يجوز' which rules on splitting a multi-item sale (tafriq al-safqah). Note that the permissibility of returning only part depends on whether the contract is complete or not.
The rule applies when a buyer purchases multiple items together.
2
Distinguish the three khiyar types
The commentator lists three options: khiyar al-shart (stipulated option), khiyar al-ru'yah (option of sight), and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect). Observe how each affects completion: the first two prevent it entirely; the third prevents it only before possession.
Memorize the categories: 'man' vs 'la yamna' after qabd.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Examine the basis: 'al-safqah innama tatimmu bi-al-rida' – completion requires full satisfaction. An option introduces uncertainty, so complete consent is not realized. For khiyar al-'ayb, before delivery the contract is not yet finalized due to pending delivery and ownership transfer.
The key concept is 'al-rida' al-kamil' (complete consent).
4
Apply to splitting a multi-item sale
If the contract is incomplete (due to an ongoing option), the buyer cannot return only part of the items because that would harm the seller, who may have bundled good and bad items. After completion, returning part is permissible.
The harm is based on custom (urf): sellers bundle to promote inferior goods.
5
Note the post-possession limitation
After delivery, the contract is complete. The buyer cannot return a defective item unilaterally; he needs the seller's consent or a judge's ruling. This is because he consented to the contract on the apparent basis of safety.
This differs from the pre-possession scenario where the contract is not yet complete.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو قبل التّمام لا يجوز، وبعد التمام يجوز.
[١]قوله : لا يجوز ; اعتباراً بابتداء الصفقة، فإنّه إذا أوجب البيع في الشيئين لا يملك المشتري القبول في أحدهما لما فيه من الإضرار بالبائع ; لأنَّ من العادة ضم الجيد إلى الرديء ترويجاً للرديء بالجيد، كذا قيل.
ثمَّ خِيارُ الشَّرط والرُّؤيةِ يمنعان تمام الصفقة، وخيار العيب يمنعُهُ قبل القبض " لا بعده ، وهذا لأنه إذا شُرِطَ الخِيارُ لأحدهما لم يتحقق الرضاء الكامل، وكذا إذا لم يرَ الْمُشْتَرِي مُشْتَرَاهُ
[٢] قوله : يمنعان ; لأنَّ الصفقة إنما تتم بالرّضاء، ولا يتحقق الرّضاء مع خيار الشرط أو الرؤية. [٣] قوله : يمنعه قبل القبض ; لأنَّ تمام الصفقة لا يحصل إلا بانتهاء الأحكام والمقصود، وذلك لا يكون قبل التسليم وثبوتِ الملك، فلو وجد المشتري عيباً في ثوب منها، فله أن يردها إن شاء، وليس له أن يردّ المعيب خاصة بعدم تمام الصفقة قبل القبض.
[٤]قوله : لا بعده ; لأنّ المشتري رضي بالعقدِ على اعتبار السلامة، والسلامة عن العيب ثابتة ظاهراً، وكانت الصفقة تامة ; ولهذا لا يملك الردّ بحكم العيب بعد القبض إلا برضاء البائع أو قضاء القاضي. [0]قوله : وهذا ; إلى كون خيارِ الشَّرطِ والرؤية مانعاً .
وكون خيار العيب مانعاً قبل القبض لا بعده.
عن تمام الصفقة مطلقاً،

And it is not permissible before completion, and permissible after completion. [1] His saying: 'it is not permissible' considering the beginning of the contract, for if he effects a sale for two items, the buyer does not have the right to accept one of them because it would harm the seller; since it is customary to combine good with bad to promote the bad with the good, as it is said. Then the option of condition and option of sight prevent the completion of the contract, and the option of defect prevents it before possession, not after. And this is because if the option is stipulated for one of them, complete satisfaction is not realized, and similarly if the buyer does not see his purchase. [2] His saying: 'They prevent' because the contract is only completed with satisfaction, and satisfaction is not realized with an option of condition or sight. [3] His saying: 'It prevents it before possession' because completion of the contract is only achieved by the conclusion of rulings and objectives, and that does not occur before delivery and confirmation of ownership. So if the buyer finds a defect in one of the garments, he may return it if he wishes, but he does not have the right to return only the defective one due to the incompleteness of the contract before possession. [4] His saying: 'not after' because the buyer consented to the contract on the basis of safety, and safety from defect is apparent, and the contract was complete. Hence he does not have the right to return due to a defect after possession except with the seller's consent or a judge's ruling. [0] His saying: 'And this' refers to the fact that the option of condition and option of sight are preventive, and the option of defect is preventive before possession, not after, regarding the completion of the contract absolutely. [6] His saying: 'complete' is not absolute, because if he did not consent at all he would not have made this contract. [7] His saying: 'so too' means complete satisfaction is not realized if the buyer does not see his purchase, because he...
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It begins with a matn line: 'And it is not permissible before completion, and permissible after completion.' The commentator explains that this refers to splitting the contract (tafriq al-safqah) by returning part of a multi-item sale: it is not allowed before the contract is complete, but after completion it is allowed. Then the text introduces a general rule: 'Then the option of condition and option of sight prevent the completion of the contract, and the option of defect prevents it before possession, not after.' The commentator explains the reasoning: the contract is completed only with full satisfaction (rida). When an option is stipulated, satisfaction is not complete because the party may still cancel. Similarly, when the buyer has not seen the item, his satisfaction is not complete. For the option of defect, before delivery the contract is not fully complete because ownership and delivery have not occurred; thus the buyer may return the entire item but not just the defective part. After delivery, the contract is considered complete, so the buyer cannot return due to a defect without the seller's consent or a judge's ruling. The commentator also clarifies that 'complete satisfaction' does not mean absolute satisfaction (as the buyer did consent to the contract), but rather the full and final satisfaction that leaves no room for cancellation.

Key Terms
تمام الصفقة (tamam al-safqah): The completion of a sales contract when all rights and obligations are finalized, and neither party has a right to unilaterally rescind.
الرضاء الكامل (al-rida' al-kamil): Complete and free consent of both parties, which is essential for the validity and completion of the contract.
خيار الشرط (khiyar al-shart): A stipulated option allowing one or both parties to cancel the sale within a specified period.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): An option for the buyer to cancel the contract upon seeing the purchased item if it does not meet expectations.
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): An option to return a defective item if the defect existed at the time of sale.
تفريق الصفقة (tafriq al-safqah): Splitting a multi-item sale by accepting part and rejecting part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does khiyar al-shart prevent tamam al-safqah?
A) Because the price is uncertain.
B) Because complete satisfaction is not realized.
C) Because delivery has not occurred.
D) Because the seller has a right to cancel.

Key Takeaway
Khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah prevent contract completion entirely due to incomplete consent, while khiyar al-'ayb only prevents completion before delivery.

Effects of Options on Sale Completion
Commentary on options preventing sale completion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how the three options (stipulation, inspection, defect) affect the completion of a sale and explain the rationale based on satisfaction (rida).

Core Idea
Options of stipulation and inspection prevent sale completion entirely; option of defect prevents only before receipt.

The Hanafi school recognizes that a sale's finality (tamam al-safqah) depends on the parties' complete satisfaction. This commentary from al-Wiqayah clarifies when different options suspend that finality. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for determining when a buyer or seller can cancel a contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a sale contract with an option. The ruling is that the sale is valid (sahih) but its completion (tamam) is suspended by certain options.
The passage distinguishes between different types of options.
2
Distinguish the three options
The passage discusses khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation), khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection), and khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect).
3
State effect on tamam for each option
Khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah prevent tamam unconditionally. Khiyar al-'ayb prevents tamam only before the buyer takes possession (qabd).
After receipt, the sale is complete for the purpose of defect return.
4
Trace the rationale
The underlying principle is that a transaction requires complete satisfaction (rida). An option or lack of inspection indicates incomplete satisfaction. For defect after receipt, the buyer's consent is presumed based on apparent soundness.
This is a rational reasoning (ta'lil) rooted in custom and consent.
5
Note practical consequence
The practical effect is that after receipt, a buyer cannot unilaterally return a defective item but needs the seller's consent or a judge's ruling.
This differs from some other schools.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار الرؤية
[1].
وهو قبل التّمام لا يجوز، وبعد التمام يجوز.
ثمَّ خِيارُ الشَّرط والرُّؤيةِ يمنعان تمام الصفقة، وخيار العيب يمنعُهُ قبل القبض " لا بعده ، وهذا لأنه إذا شُرِطَ الخِيارُ لأحدهما لم يتحقق الرضاء الكامل، وكذا إذا لم يرَ الْمُشْتَرِي مُشْتَرَاهُ
ضَرَبَ ضربة باليد، وصفقت له بالسعة صفقاً أيضاً : ضربت بيدي على يده ، وكان العرب إذا أوجب البيع ضرب أحدهما يده على يد صاحبه، ثم استعملت الصفقة في العقد، فقيل : بارك الله لك في صفقة يمينك، قال الأَزْهَرِيُّ : تكون الصفقة للبائع والمشتري». انتهى
(1)
[١]قوله : لا يجوز ؛ اعتباراً بابتداء الصفقة، فإنّه إذا أوجب البيع في الشيئين لا يملك المشتري القبول في أحدهما لما فيه من الإضرار بالبائع ؛ لأنَّ من العادة ضم الجيد إلى الرديء ترويجاً للرديء بالجيد، كذا قيل.
(٢) قوله : يمنعان ؛ لأنَّ الصفقة إنما تتم بالرّضاء، ولا يتحقق الرّضاء مع خيار الشرط أو الرؤية. [٣] قوله : يمنعه قبل القبض ؛ لأنَّ تمام الصفقة لا يحصل إلا بانتهاء الأحكام والمقصود، وذلك لا يكون قبل التسليم وثبوتِ الملك، فلو وجد المشتري عيباً في ثوب منها، فله أن يردها إن شاء، وليس له أن يردّ المعيب خاصة بعدم تمام الصفقة قبل القبض.
[٤]قوله : لا بعده ؛ لأنّ المشتري رضي بالعقدِ على اعتبار السلامة، والسلامة عن العيب ثابتة ظاهراً، وكانت الصفقة تامة ؛ ولهذا لا يملك الردّ بحكم العيب بعد القبض إلا برضاء البائع أو قضاء القاضي. [0]قوله : وهذا ؛ إلى كون خيارِ الشَّرطِ والرؤية مانعاً . وكون خيار العيب مانعاً قبل القبض لا بعده. عن تمام الصفقة مطلقاً،
[٦]قوله : الكامل؛ لا مطلقاً ؛ لأنه لو لم يرض به أصلاً لم يوقع هذا العقد. [٧]قوله : فكذا ؛ لا يتحقَّقُ الرّضاء الكامل إذا لم يردّ المشتري مشتراه ؛ لأنه إن
أما إذا لم يشترط الخيار ، أو شَرَطَ فأجازَ مَن له الخيار، أو المُشتَرِي قد رأى المبيع فرضي به فبعد ذلك إن قبض فقد تَمَّ الصَّفقةُ بحصولِ الرِّضى الكامل. لكن مع ذلك يمكن أن يكون المبيع معيباً، والمشتري لا يرضى به ، فيُفْسَخُ العقد، فذلك أمرٌ مُتَوَهَّم ، فلا يمنع تمام الصفقة، وإن لم يقبض المبيع فالبيع في معرض الفسخ ؛ بأن هلك في يد البائع فيرتفع العقد، فإذا اجتمع الأمران أي عدم القبض ووجود العيب فيتقوى أحدُهُما بالآخر، فلا يَتِمُّ الصفقة، ويظهر هذا في المسألة التي تأتي، وهي قوله : ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة وقبض أحدهما ووَجَدَ به أو بالآخر عيبا.
وجده عند الرؤية معيباً وهو لم يرض به فله اختيار الردّ ، فهو ما لم يره متردّد بين الأخذ والرد.
موميامي
Book of Sale: Option of Inspection. Before completion it is not permissible, and after completion it is permissible. Then, option of stipulation and option of inspection prevent the completion of the transaction, and option of defect prevents it before receipt, not after. This is because if the option is stipulated for one of them, complete satisfaction is not realized, and likewise if the buyer has not seen what he purchased. [He] struck a blow with his hand, and I clapped for him with spaciousness also: he struck his hand on his hand. The Arabs, when they concluded a sale, one of them struck his hand on the hand of his companion. Then the word 'safqah' was used for the contract, so it was said: 'May Allah bless you in the transaction of your right hand.' Al-Azhari said: The transaction is for the seller and the buyer. [Commentary:] [1] His saying: 'it is not permissible': considering the beginning of the transaction, for if he offers to sell two things, the buyer does not have the right to accept one of them because that harms the seller, since it is customary to join good with bad to promote the bad by good, as it is said. [2] His saying: 'they prevent': because the transaction is only completed by consent, and consent is not realized with option of stipulation or inspection. [3] His saying: 'it prevents before receipt': because completion of the transaction only occurs with the conclusion of rulings and purpose, and that does not happen before delivery and establishment of ownership. So if the buyer finds a defect in a garment from them, he has the right to return it if he wishes, but he cannot return only the defective one due to the incompleteness of the transaction before receipt. [4] His saying: 'not after': because the buyer consented to the contract on the basis of presumed soundness, and freedom from defect is ostensibly established, and the transaction was complete. For this reason, he does not have the right to return due to defect after receipt except with the seller's consent or a judge's ruling. [0] His saying: 'This' refers to that option of stipulation and inspection prevent absolutely, and option of defect prevents before receipt not after. [6] His saying: 'complete' not absolutely, because if he had not consented at all, he would not have entered into this contract. [7] His saying: 'likewise', complete satisfaction is not realized if the buyer has not seen what he purchased. If no option is stipulated, or it was stipulated and the one with the option ratified, or the buyer saw the merchandise and was satisfied with it, then after that if he has received it, the transaction is completed by the attainment of complete satisfaction. However, it is still possible that the merchandise is defective and the buyer is not satisfied with it, so the contract is rescinded; that is a potential matter, so it does not prevent the completion. And if he has not received the merchandise, the sale is subject to rescission, such as if it perishes in the seller's hand, the contract is dissolved. When the two matters combine, i.e., non-receipt and existence of a defect, each strengthens the other, so the transaction is not completed. This appears in the coming issue, which is his saying: 'If he bought two slaves in one transaction and received one and found a defect in it or the other.' He finds it at inspection defective and he is not satisfied with it, so he has the choice to return; so as long as he has not seen it, he is hesitant between taking and returning.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah concerning the 'option of inspection' and related options in sales. It begins by stating the basic ruling: an option of inspection before completion of the sale is not valid, but after completion it is valid. The commentator then explains how different types of options affect the completion (tamām) of the transaction. Khiyār al-sharṭ (option of stipulation) and khiyār al-ru’yah (option of inspection) both prevent the transaction from being fully concluded because they indicate that the parties' satisfaction (riḍā) is not complete. As long as an option is pending, the contract is not final. On the other hand, khiyār al-‘ayb (option of defect) prevents completion only before the buyer takes possession (qabḍ). Once the buyer has taken delivery, the transaction is considered complete because the buyer implicitly agreed to accept the goods assuming they were free from defect; if a defect appears later, he cannot return it unilaterally but needs the seller's consent or a judge's order. The commentator provides the underlying rationale (ta‘līl): the transaction is built on mutual satisfaction, and an option or lack of inspection undermines complete satisfaction. After receipt, the transaction is deemed final regarding the option of defect, but the possibility of hidden defect remains a potential concern; however, this potential does not prevent the immediate completion of the transaction. When both non-receipt and defect coexist, they strengthen each other to prevent completion. The commentary also touches on the linguistic origin of the term 'ṣafqah' from the practice of hand-clapping in contracts. This passage is essential for understanding how the Hanafi school categorizes options and their effect on the finality of a sale.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): The buyer's option to cancel a sale after seeing the purchased item if it was not seen at the time of contract.
خيار الشرط (khiyār al-sharṭ): An option stipulated by one party to cancel the sale within a specified period.
خيار العيب (khiyār al-‘ayb): The buyer's option to return a defective item due to a hidden defect that existed at the time of sale.
تمام الصفقة (tamām al-ṣafqah): The full conclusion and finalization of the sale contract, after which neither party can cancel without consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following options prevents the completion of a sale even after receipt of the goods?
A) Khiyar al-shart (option of stipulation)
B) Khiyar al-'ayb (option of defect)
C) Both A and B
D) Neither A nor B

Key Takeaway
The completion of a sale (tamam al-safqah) is prevented by khiyar al-shart and khiyar al-ru'yah unconditionally, and by khiyar al-'ayb only before receipt.

Completion of Sale Despite Possible Defect
Explains when a sale is complete despite a defect being discovered

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a sale is considered complete (tamam al-safqah) despite a possible defect, and distinguish when the sale is vulnerable to rescission.

Core Idea
A sale is completed by possession after expressed satisfaction; a possible defect does not prevent completion but gives a right of rescission.

In Hanafi sales law, the completion of a transaction (tamam al-safqah) is a key concept that determines when a sale becomes binding. This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies that even if a defect is later discovered, the sale can be complete if the buyer has seen the item, expressed satisfaction, and taken possession. However, if possession has not occurred, the sale remains vulnerable to rescission, especially when combined with a defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios leading to completion
The passage mentions three scenarios: (1) no option stipulated, (2) option holder rescinded, (3) buyer saw and accepted. If possession occurs, the sale is complete.
2
Recognize the role of possession (qabd)
Possession after satisfaction makes the sale binding. Without possession, the sale is vulnerable to rescission if a defect exists.
Even if the buyer is satisfied, without possession the sale is not yet complete.
3
Understand the hypothetical possibility of defect
Even after completion, a defect can lead to rescission, but that does not affect the initial completion.
The passage calls it 'hypothetical' to show it does not prevent completion.
4
Apply the principle to the two-slave case
The classic Hanafi example: if one slave is taken possession and a defect found, the sale may be partially or fully rescinded.
This illustrates how non-possession and defect combine to prevent completion of the whole transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما إذا لم يشترط الخيار ، أو شَرَطَ فأجازَ مَن له الخيار، أو المُشتَرِي قد رأى المبيع فرضي به فبعد ذلك إن قبض فقد تَمَّ الصَّفقةُ بحصولِ الرِّضى الكامل. لكن مع ذلك يمكن أن يكون المبيع معيباً، والمشتري لا يرضى به ، فيُفْسَخُ العقد، فذلك أمرٌ مُتَوَهَّم ، فلا يمنع تمام الصفقة، وإن لم يقبض المبيع فالبيع في معرض الفسخ ؛ بأن هلك في يد البائع فيرتفع العقد، فإذا اجتمع الأمران أي عدم القبض ووجود العيب فيتقوى أحدُهُما بالآخر، فلا يَتِمُّ الصفقة، ويظهر هذا في المسألة التي تأتي، وهي قوله : ولو اشترى عبدين صفقة وقبض أحدهما ووَجَدَ به أو بالآخر عيبا. وجده عند الرؤية معيباً وهو لم يرض به فله اختيار الردّ ، فهو ما لم يره متردّد بين الأخذ والرد.
However, if he did not stipulate the option, or stipulated and the one with the option rescinded, or the buyer had seen the merchandise and was satisfied with it, then after that if he takes possession, the transaction is complete by the attainment of complete satisfaction. But even so, it is possible that the merchandise is defective and the buyer is not satisfied with it, so the contract is rescinded. That is a hypothetical matter, so it does not prevent the completion of the transaction. And if he does not take possession of the merchandise, the sale is subject to rescission; for example, if it perishes in the seller's hand, the contract is annulled. So if the two circumstances (i.e., non-possession and existence of defect) combine, each one is strengthened by the other, and the transaction is not complete. This appears in the upcoming case, which is his saying: 'If he bought two slaves in one transaction and took possession of one and found a defect in it or the other.' He finds it defective upon sight and is not satisfied with it, so he has the choice of rejection; he is thus, as long as he has not seen it, undecided between acceptance and rejection.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi commentary (sharh) explaining when a sale is deemed complete (tammam al-safqah) despite a possible defect. The author addresses three scenarios: (1) when no option was stipulated, (2) when the option bearer has rescinded, or (3) when the buyer has seen and accepted the item. In all these, if the buyer takes possession, the sale is complete. However, even after completion, if a defect is discovered, the buyer can rescind—but this does not affect the initial completion. Crucially, if the buyer has not taken possession, the sale is vulnerable to rescission, especially if combined with a defect. The author then references the well-known Hanafi case of two slaves bought together: if one is taken and a defect is found, the sale may be partially or fully rescinded. The material demonstrates the Hanafi principle that khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect) does not prevent the sale from being binding unless the buyer has not taken possession and a defect exists.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-‘ayb): The buyer's right to return a defective item and rescind the sale.
الصفقة (al-safqah): The transaction or deal; its completion (tamām al-safqah) means the sale becomes binding.
الرضى (al-riḍā): The buyer's satisfaction with the merchandise, which when explicit (e.g., after seeing) makes the sale binding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, when is a sale considered complete despite a defect being later discovered?
A) Only if the buyer has paid the full price.
B) If the buyer has taken possession after seeing and accepting the item.
C) If the defect was known to the seller but not the buyer.
D) If the buyer has not taken possession but has expressed satisfaction.

Key Takeaway
A sale is complete upon possession after expressed satisfaction; a possible hidden defect does not prevent completion but gives the buyer the right to rescind.

The Five Conditions for Khiyar al-Ayb (Option of Defect)
Enumerates and explains the five conditions for khiyar al-‘ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five conditions for khiyar al-‘ayb and explain how the buyer's awareness affects the right of return.

Core Idea
A defective item cannot be returned unless the defect existed with the seller and the buyer was unaware at sale and possession.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah enumerates the five essential conditions for the buyer's right to rescind a sale due to a defect. Understanding these conditions is critical for applying khiyar al-‘ayb in real transactions. The passage also highlights a dispute within the Hanafi school on whether obvious defects preclude return.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that khiyar al-‘ayb is the buyer's right to return a defective item. In Hanafi law, this right is wajib (obligatory upon the seller) when certain conditions are met. The passage defines these conditions.
The right prevents the sale from becoming fully binding (luzum).
2
Enumerate the five conditions
List the five conditions exactly as given: (1) defect existed with seller, (2) buyer unaware at sale, (3) buyer unaware at possession, (4) defect not easily removable, (5) no disclaimer for that defect.
Memorize: existence, unawareness at two times, irremovability, no disclaimer.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note the disagreement between Muhammad ibn Salamah (allows return for obvious defects if buyer unaware) and the author of al-Muhit (only hidden defects). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that actual unawareness matters, not the defect's obviousness.
This affects how 'found' (wajada) is interpreted: knew or obtained.
4
Apply conditions to a scenario
Given a case (e.g., buyer sees a stain but thinks it is removable, later learns it is permanent), check each condition: defect existed with seller? buyer unaware at sale? unaware at possession? removable? disclaimer? Then state whether return is allowed.
The key is subjective unawareness, not objective obviousness.
5
Identify the mufta bihi (operative position)
The Hanafi school follows the view that the buyer's knowledge is the decisive factor. If the buyer genuinely did not know the defect existed (even if obvious), the right of return remains. This is supported by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في خيار العيب
ومُشْتَرِ وَجَدَ بمشتريه
[۱] قوله : في خيار العيب ; أخره ; لأنه يمنعُ اللزوم، وإضافة الخيار إلى العيب من قبيل إضافة المسبب إلى السبب.
[٢]قوله : وجد ; اعلم أنه إذا نظر المشتري إلى العيب ولم يعلم أنه عيب، ثمّ علمه فله الردّ ، وهو المنقولُ عن محمد بن سلمة له ، وعن صاحب «المحيط»: إنّه إن كان عيب بيناً لا يخفى على الناس لا يكون له الردّ ، وإلا فله الردّ. كذا في «القُنْية») ، فعلى هذا يحتمل أن يكون وجد بمعنى عَلِم، وأن يكون بمعنى أصاب. كذا في «شرح النقاية» للبرْجَندِي.
[٣٢] قوله : بمشريه ; أطلقه فيشتمل إذا كان به عند البيع، أو حدث بعدها في يد البائع، بخلاف ما إذا كان قبله، وزالَ ثم عاد عند المشتري ; لما في «البزازية ) : لو كان به عرج فبرى بمعالجة البائع ثم عاد عند المشتري لا يردُّه ، وقيل : يرده إن عاد بالسبب الأول. كذا في «رد المحتار» (۳).
واعلم أنه لا بد للمسألة من قيود :
(۲)
الأول : أن يكون العيب عند البائع ، فإن حدث عند المشتري فلا يقدر على الرد.
والثاني : أن لا يعلم به عند البيع.
والثالث : أن لا يعلم به عند القبض، فإنَّ العلم بالعيب عند البيع أو القبض رضاء به.
والرابع : أن لا يتمكن من إزالته بلا مشقة، فإن تمكن فلا : كإحرام الجارية، ونجاسةِ الثّوب ; لتمكنه من تحليلها وغسله.
والخامس : ألا تشترط البراءة من هذا العيب خصوصاً أو من العيوب عموماً.
Section on the Option of Defect. A buyer found a defect in his purchase. [1] His saying: 'Section on the Option of Defect'; he postponed it because it prevents binding. The addition of 'option' to 'defect' is of the type of adding the effect to the cause. [2] His saying: 'found'; know that when the buyer looks at the defect and does not know it is a defect, then later comes to know, he has the right of return. This is the transmitted view from Muhammad ibn Salamah. And from the author of al-Muhit: If it is an obvious defect that is not hidden from people, he does not have the right of return; otherwise he has the right. Thus in al-Qunyah. Based on this, it is possible that 'found' means 'knew' or means 'obtained'. Thus in Sharh al-Niqayah by al-Barjandi. [32] His saying: 'in his purchase'; it is unrestricted, so it includes if the defect existed at the time of sale, or occurred after that in the seller's hand, contrary to if it existed before and disappeared and then returned while in the buyer's possession; because in al-Bazzaziyyah: If there was a limp and it healed by the seller's treatment, then returned when with the buyer, he does not return it. And it is said: he returns it if it returned by the first cause. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. And know that this matter necessarily has conditions: First: That the defect existed with the seller; if it occurs with the buyer, he cannot return. Second: That he was unaware of it at the time of sale. Third: That he was unaware of it at the time of taking possession; because knowledge of the defect at the time of sale or taking possession is acceptance of it. Fourth: That he is not able to remove it without difficulty; if he is able, then no: like the consecration of a slave girl, and the impurity of a garment; because he is able to dissolve the consecration and wash it. Fifth: That a disclaimer is not stipulated for this particular defect or for defects in general.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah, specifically on the chapter of khiyar al-‘ayb (option of defect). The commentator first explains the phrase 'Section on the Option of Defect,' noting that the author delayed this section because the option of defect prevents the sale from becoming binding (luzum). The addition of 'option' to 'defect' is a causal addition (māddat al-musabbab ila al-sabab). Then he explains the word 'wajada' (found), detailing that if the buyer sees a defect but does not know it is a defect, then later learns, he has the right to return. However, there is a difference of opinion: Muhammad ibn Salamah holds the right; the author of al-Muhit distinguishes between obvious defects (which the buyer is assumed to know) and hidden ones. This affects whether 'wajada' means 'knew' or 'obtained'. The commentary on 'bimushrihi' (in his purchase) clarifies that the defect may have existed at sale or occurred in the seller's hand, but not if it existed before and was cured then returned. Finally, the commentator lists five essential conditions (quyud) for the right of return: (1) defect existed with seller, (2) buyer unaware at sale, (3) buyer unaware at possession, (4) defect not easily removable, (5) no disclaimer stipulated. These conditions are foundational in Hanafi sales law.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-‘ayb): The buyer's legal right to rescind a sale due to a defect in the purchased item.
البراءة (al-barā'ah): A contractual clause by which the seller disclaims responsibility for certain defects; if stipulated, the buyer cannot return the item for those defects.
قيود (quyūd): Conditions that must be satisfied for the option of defect to be exercised.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the five conditions mentioned for khiyar al-‘ayb in the passage?
A) The buyer must not be able to remove the defect without difficulty.
B) The defect must exist at the time of the contract or in the seller's possession.
C) The buyer must return the item immediately upon discovery.
D) The seller must not have stipulated a disclaimer for that defect.

Key Takeaway
Five cumulative conditions must be satisfied for khiyar al-‘ayb; a defect alone is insufficient for return.

Conditions for Exercising the Option of Defect (Khiyar al-Ayb)
Commentary on conditions and definition of the option of defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six conditions required for the buyer to exercise the option of defect and apply them to a given sale scenario.

Core Idea
The buyer can return a defective item only if the defect existed at the seller and was unknown to him, among other conditions.

The option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) protects the buyer from being bound to a purchase that is not as sound as expected. This lesson unpacks the commentary on this topic, focusing on the precise conditions that must be met for the option to be valid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal operation
Understand that the option of defect is a non-binding right that prevents the sale from becoming immediately binding. The commentary explains why the option is attributed to the defect.
The option is an effect (musabbab) caused by the defect (sabab).
2
List the six conditions
Extract the six conditions from the commentary: (1) defect with seller, (2) no knowledge at sale, (3) no knowledge at delivery, (4) defect not easily removable, (5) no stipulation of immunity, (6) defect persists until annulment.
Each condition is a separate requirement; missing any one voids the option.
3
Define 'defect' legally
Recognise that the Hanafi definition of defect (ayb) is based on custom: anything that diminishes the market value among merchants, not a fixed list.
Custom (urf) of the relevant trade determines what constitutes a defect.
4
Apply to a scenario
Take a concrete case and check each condition sequentially to see if the buyer has the option.
Use the conditions as a checklist.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Observe that early Hanafi authorities differed on whether an obvious defect that the buyer did not notice still gives the option. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) allows return if the defect was hidden or not known.
Muhammad ibn Salamah vs. author of al-Muhit; resolved by al-Birjandi.
