والسادس : أن لا يزول قبل الفسخ ؛ فإن زال قبل الفسخ فليس لـه الـرد مثل بياض العين إذا انجلى والحمى اذا زالت والبسط في «البحر» [١]قوله : عيباً ؛ وهو لغة : ما يخلو عنه أصل الفطرة السليمة، وشرعاً : ما نقص ثمنه عند التجار ؛ لأنّ التّضرّر بنقصان الماليّة، وذلك بانتقاص القيمة. كذا في «المنح " سواء كان يُنْقِصُ العين أو لا يُنْقِصُها، ولا ينقص المنفعة بل مجرد النظر إليها ؛ لأنه عندهم يوجب نقص ثمنه. ونظيره الظفر الأسود وإن كان ينقص الثمن فهو عيب كما في الأتراك، وإن كان لا ينقصه فليس بعيب، كما في «الحبش»، قال في «المحيط»: «اشترى جارية هندية فوجدها لا تحسن الهندية، وإن كان النّاس يعدونه عيباً فله الردّ وإلا فلا ، ولو اشترى جارية تركية فوجدها لا تعرف التركية فله الرد ؛ لأنّ ذلك عيب». انتهى. [٢] قوله : عند التجار ؛ المراد بهم أرباب المعرفةِ من كل تجارة وصنعة، فإنَّ المبيع قد يكون من المصنوعات، فيعتبر عرفُ الصنّاع لا التجار، وإذا أريد بهم ما يعم الصناع اندفع هذا الإشكال، كذا ذكرَهُ العلامة الطَّحْطَاوي (۳) وقال البرْجَندِي : وهذا بناءً على الغالب، وفي العقار يعتبر نقصانُ الثّمن بتقويم المقومين، ولا مدخل لرأي التجار في ذلك. [٣] قوله : ردُّه أو أخذه بكلِّ ثمنه ؛ لأنَّ مطلق العقدِ يقتضي وصف السلامة، فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر بلزوم ما لا يرضى به وهذا التخير بين الأخذ والرد ما لم يتعيَّن إمساكه ، فإذا وُجد ما يمنع الردَّ يتعيَّن الأخذ، لكن في بعض الصور: يرجعُ بنقصان العيب، وفي بعضها : لا . كما إذا اشترى من آخر عبداً وباعه من غيره ، ثم اشتراه من ذلك الغير، فرأى عيباً كان عند البائع الأول لم يردّه على الذي اشتراه منه ؛ لأنه غير مفيد، إذ لو ردّه يردّه الآخر عليه، ولا على البائع الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ هذا الملك غير مستفاد من جهته.
Chapter on the Option of Defect. And a buyer found a defect in his purchase. [1] His statement: 'on the Option of Defect' – it is delayed because it prevents bindingness, and attributing the option to the defect is a case of attributing the effect to the cause. [2] His statement: 'found' – know that if the buyer looks at the defect and does not know it is a defect, then later learns of it, he has the right of return. This is the transmitted view from Muhammad ibn Salamah concerning him, and from the author of al-Muhit: if it is an obvious defect not hidden from people, he has no right of return; otherwise he has the right of return. Thus in al-Qunyah. According to this, it is possible that 'found' means 'knew' or 'encountered'. Thus in Sharh al-Niqayah of al-Birjandi. [32] His statement: 'in his purchase' – it is absolute, so it includes if the defect was present at the time of sale, or occurred afterwards while in the hand of the seller, contrary to if it was before and then disappeared then returned while with the buyer; because in al-Bazzaziyyah: if there was lameness and it healed by the seller's treatment then returned while with the buyer, he does not return it, and it is said he returns it if it returned by the first cause. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. Know that the matter necessarily has conditions: (1) First: that the defect be with the seller, for if it occurs with the buyer then he cannot return. Second: that he not know of it at the time of sale. Third: that he not know of it at the time of taking possession, for knowledge of the defect at sale or possession is consent to it. Fourth: that he cannot remove it without difficulty, for if he can then not: like the ihram of a slave girl and the impurity of a garment, because he can remove them by making her halal or washing. Fifth: that the seller not stipulate immunity from this defect specifically or from defects generally. (1) Quniyat al-Munyah. (2) Al-Fatawa al-Bazzaziyyah. (3) Radd al-Muhtar. Sixth: that it not disappear before annulment; for if it disappears before annulment, he has no return, like whiteness of the eye when it clears, and fever when it subsides, and the exposition in al-Bahr. [1] His statement: 'a defect' – linguistically: what the original sound nature is free of, and legally: what diminishes its price among merchants, because the harm is by decrease of monetary value, and that is by decrease of value. Thus in al-Manah, whether it decreases the substance or not, and does not decrease the benefit but merely the sight of it, because according to them it causes decrease in its price. Its like is the black toenail; if it decreases the price it is a defect, as among the Turks, and if it does not decrease it, it is not a defect, as among the Abyssinians. He said in al-Muhit: 'If one buys an Indian slave girl and finds she does not know Indian, if people consider it a defect he has the right of return, otherwise not. And if he buys a Turkish slave girl and finds she does not know Turkish, he has the right of return because that is a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'among merchants' – meaning those with knowledge from every trade and craft, for the sold item may be from manufactured goods, so the custom of craftsmen is considered, not merchants. If by them is meant what includes craftsmen, this problem is removed. Thus mentioned by al-Tahtawi. And al-Birjandi said: This is based on the general case, and in real estate the decrease of price is considered by the evaluation of assessors, and the opinion of merchants has no entry in that. [3] His statement: 'return it or accept it at its full price' – because the absolute contract necessitates the attribute of soundness, so when it is lost, he has a choice so as not to be harmed by being bound to what he is not satisfied with. This choice between acceptance and return is as long as retention is not determined. So if there is something preventing return, retention is determined, but in some cases he recovers the decrease due to the defect, and in others not. Such as if he buys a slave from someone, then sells him to another, then buys him from that other, and finds a defect that was with the first seller: he does not return him to the one from whom he bought him because it is useless, for if he returns him, the other returns him to him; nor to the first seller because this ownership is not acquired from him.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the chapter 'Option of Defect' from the matn. It begins by explaining why the option is attributed to the defect: the option prevents the contract from binding immediately, and the defect is the cause of the option. The commentator then clarifies the meaning of 'found' in the matn. If a buyer examines an item and does not realize a defect, then later discovers it, he has the right to return it. However, if the defect was obvious and commonly known, some authorities say he cannot return it because he should have seen it. This leads to interpreting 'found' as either 'knew' or 'encountered'. Next, 'in his purchase' means the defect was present at the time of sale or occurred while the item was in the seller's possession. If the defect existed before sale but disappeared and then returned while with the buyer, the ruling differs: if it returned due to the same cause, some allow return; otherwise not. The core of the lesson is the six conditions for invoking the option of defect: (1) the defect must have existed with the seller; (2) the buyer must not have known of it at sale; (3) he must not have known at delivery (knowledge equals consent); (4) he cannot remove it without hardship; (5) the seller did not stipulate immunity from that defect; (6) the defect must persist until annulment. After stating conditions, the commentary defines 'defect' linguistically and legally: legally, it is anything that diminishes the price among merchants. The definition is based on custom ('urf). It gives examples: a black toenail that reduces value among Turks is a defect, but not among Abyssinians. Language ability can also be a defect if people consider it such. 'Among merchants' is interpreted broadly to include craftsmen and experts, because the sold item may be a manufactured good. For real estate, expert appraisers are considered. Finally, the buyer has two options: return the defective item and get his full price back, or accept it at full price. This choice exists as long as there is no impediment to return. If return is prevented, he must accept but may claim compensation for the defect in some cases. An example is given of a slave sold multiple times where return is futile.

Key Terms
خيار العيب (khiyar al-ayb): The option given to the buyer to return a defective purchased item or accept it at full price.
عيب (ayb): Linguistically: what the sound nature lacks; legally: any deficiency that diminishes the market price among merchants.
عند التجار (inda al-tujjar): Among merchants and experts; refers to the customary standard of those knowledgeable in the relevant trade or craft.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which condition is NOT required for the buyer to exercise the option of defect?
A) The defect must have existed with the seller.
B) The buyer must not have known of the defect at the time of sale.
C) The buyer must have inspected the item thoroughly.
D) The defect must still be present at the time of annulment.

Key Takeaway
The option of defect is conditional upon six precise requirements; absence of any one negates the right of return.

Definition of 'Ayb and Its Criterion in Hanafi Law
Legal definition and scope of 'ayb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of 'ayb and apply the criterion of price reduction among merchants to determine whether a particular attribute constitutes a defect.

Core Idea
A defect is anything that reduces the price among merchants, regardless of physical damage.

In Hanafi sales law, the buyer has the right to return a defective item (khiyar al-'ayb). But what exactly counts as a defect? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya defines 'ayb in both linguistic and legal terms, establishing that the criterion is market value reduction as determined by trade custom. Understanding this definition is crucial for assessing a buyer's return rights in any sale.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish linguistic vs legal definition
Note that linguistically, 'ayb is something absent from a sound nature; legally, it is defined by price reduction among merchants. The legal definition overrides the linguistic one for practical fiqh purposes.
The word عيب has a general meaning, but the Shari'ah gives it a specific operational definition.
2
Identify the key criterion
The core criterion is 'what reduces the price among merchants' (ما نقص ثمنه عند التجار). This is a market-based, not physical, test. Even a cosmetic flaw that decreases value qualifies, while a functional flaw that does not affect price does not.
3
Apply the criterion to examples
The passage gives the black nail example: if it reduces price among Turks, it is a defect; if not among Habash, it is not. Also the slave-girl's language ability: only considered a defect if people generally deem it so. Practice applying the criterion to everyday items.
Custom ('urf) differs by region and trade.
4
Scope the experts
'Among merchants' (عند التجار) means people of expertise in each trade or craft. For manufactured goods, follow the custom of craftsmen; for real estate, rely on evaluators. The definition is not limited to literal merchants but includes all relevant market actors.
Al-Tahtawi and al-Barjandi clarify that if 'merchants' includes craftsmen, the problem of scope is resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1) قوله : عيباً ؛ وهو لغة : ما يخلو عنه أصل الفطرة السليمة، وشرعاً : ما نقص ثمنه عند التجار ؛ لأنّ التّضرّر بنقصان الماليّة، وذلك بانتقاص القيمة. كذا في «المنح» سواء كان يُنْقِصُ العين أو لا يُنْقِصُها، ولا ينقص المنفعة بل مجرد النظر إليها ؛ لأنه عندهم يوجب نقص ثمنه. ونظيره الظفر الأسود وإن كان ينقص الثمن فهو عيب كما في الأتراك، وإن كان لا ينقصه فليس بعيب، كما في «الحبش»، قال في «المحيط»: «اشترى جارية هندية فوجدها لا تحسن الهندية، وإن كان النّاس يعدونه عيباً فله الردّ وإلا فلا ، ولو اشترى جارية تركية فوجدها لا تعرف التركية فله الرد ؛ لأنّ ذلك عيب». انتهى. [٢] قوله : عند التجار ؛ المراد بهم أرباب المعرفةِ من كل تجارة وصنعة، فإنَّ المبيع قد يكون من المصنوعات، فيعتبر عرفُ الصنّاع لا التجار، وإذا أريد بهم ما يعم الصناع اندفع هذا الإشكال، كذا ذكرَهُ العلامة الطَّحْطَاوي. وقال البرْجَندِي : وهذا بناءً على الغالب، وفي العقار يعتبر نقصانُ الثّمن بتقويم المقومين، ولا مدخل لرأي التجار في ذلك.
(1) His statement: 'a defect' - linguistically it is what is free from the foundation of a sound nature, and legally it is what reduces its price among merchants, because harm is due to reduction of monetary value, and that is by decrease in value. Thus in 'Al-Manh' whether it decreases the substance or not, and does not decrease the benefit but merely the sight of it, because according to them it necessitates a decrease in its price. Its analogy is the black nail: if it decreases the price it is a defect, as among the Turks, and if it does not decrease it, it is not a defect, as among the 'Habash'. He said in 'Al-Muhit': 'If someone buys a Hindu slave-girl and finds she does not know Hindi, if people consider it a defect then he has the right of return, otherwise not. And if he buys a Turkish slave-girl and finds she does not know Turkish, he has the right of return because that is a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'among merchants' - meant are the people of expertise from every trade and craft, because the sold item may be from manufactured goods, so the custom of the craftsmen is considered, not the merchants. And if by them is meant what includes the craftsmen, this problem is repelled. Thus mentioned by Allama al-Tahtawi. And al-Barjandi said: This is based on the majority, and in real estate the decrease in price is considered by the valuation of evaluators, and there is no entry for the opinion of merchants in that.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of 'ayb (defect) in the context of the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb) in sales. The commentator first clarifies the linguistic meaning: a defect is what is absent from the original sound nature. The legal definition, however, is based on the custom of merchants: any attribute that reduces the price among merchants is a defect, regardless of whether it actually damages the physical substance or merely affects the perception of value. The commentator gives examples from Al-Muhit: if a slave-girl does not know the language of her people, it is considered a defect only if people generally deem it so. Then he elaborates on 'among merchants' - it refers to experts in each trade or craft. For manufactured items, the custom of the craftsmen is followed, not just traders. For real estate, evaluators determine the reduction, not merchants.

Key Terms
عيب (‘ayb): Anything that reduces the price among merchants, whether it affects the substance or not.
عند التجار (‘inda al-tujjār): Refers to people of expertise in each trade or craft; custom of that trade determines defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal definition of a defect ('ayb) in a sold item?
A) Any physical damage to the item.
B) Anything that reduces the item's price among merchants.
C) Any hidden flaw not visible at purchase.
D) Any attribute that makes the item unusable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a defect is determined by the custom of merchants: any attribute that reduces the market value, even without physical damage, gives the buyer the right to return.

Khiyar al-'Ayb: Return or Keep with Full Price
Explains buyer's khiyar al-'ayb: return or keep, and when compensation is not allowed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two options available to a buyer who discovers a defect in the purchased item and identify the exceptional cases where compensation is allowed.

Core Idea
A buyer may return a defective item or keep it at full price; compensation is only allowed when return is impossible.

The Hanafi school gives the buyer a choice upon discovering a defect: either return the item or keep it with the full price agreed. This rule protects both parties from harm. However, in certain situations where return is not possible, the buyer may claim a reduction in price. This lesson explains the default ruling and its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Read the opening phrase: 'ردُّه أو أخذه بكلِّ ثمنه' – the buyer may return the defective item or keep it and pay the full price. This is the default ruling.
The buyer has a choice; he cannot demand a price reduction unless return is impossible.
2
Understand the condition for the option
The option exists only as long as return is possible. When return is prevented (e.g., due to ownership changes or ihram), the buyer must keep the item and may recover the deficiency (nuqsan).
The text says 'ما لم يتعيَّن إمساكه' – as long as keeping is not determined.
3
Study the first exceptional example
Example from the text: If the buyer resells the item and later repurchases it, he cannot return it to the first seller because the chain would continue endlessly. Instead, he recovers the deficiency.
This is a case where return is useless (ghayr mufid).
4
Study the second exceptional example
If two people in halal (non-ihram) trade game, then one or both enter ihram, return is impossible because the item is now in a state of ihram. Here, the buyer also recovers the deficiency.
Physical return is not feasible due to ritual restrictions.
5
Recognize the cross-madhhab difference
The passage implies that the Shafi'i school allows the buyer to keep the item and demand compensation (arsh) even when return is possible. This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.
The Hanafi position is based on the nature of price (see Lesson 2).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : ردُّه أو أخذه بكلِّ ثمنه ؛ لأنَّ مطلق العقدِ يقتضي وصف السلامة، فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر بلزوم ما لا يرضى به وهذا التخير بين الأخذ والرد ما لم يتعيَّن إمساكه ، فإذا وُجد ما يمنع الردَّ يتعيَّن الأخذ، لكن في بعض الصور: يرجعُ بنقصان العيب، وفي بعضها : لا . كما إذا اشترى من آخر عبداً وباعه من غيره ، ثم اشتراه من ذلك الغير، فرأى عيباً كان عند البائع الأول لم يردّه على الذي اشتراه منه ؛ لأنه غير مفيد، إذ لو ردّه يردّه الآخر عليه، ولا على البائع الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ هذا الملك غير مستفاد من جهته. وكما إذا اشترى أحدُ الحَلالَيْنِ من الآخر صيداً ثم أحرما أو أحدهما، ثم وجد المشتري به عيبا امتنع ردّه ورجع بالنقصان. كذا في رد المحتار). [1] قوله : لا إمساكه وأخذه بنقصانه ؛ لأنّ الفائت وصف، والأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في مجرّد العقد ؛ لأنّ الثَّمن عين ، فيكون مقابلاً بالعين دون الوصف ؛ لأنه عرض غير متقوم فأَنِّي يقابله عين متقوم. ولأن الثمن لا يخلو إما أن يقابل بالوصف والأصل، وفيه تسوية عن الأصل والتبع، أو بالوصف فقط، وفيه ترجيح التبع على الأصل، أو بالأصل فقط، وهو المرام ؛ ولأنَّ البائع لم يرض بزوال المبيع عن ملكه بأقل من الثمن المعين، وفي إمساكه وأخذ النقصان زواله بأقلّ، فلم تكن مرضية، فيتضرر به البائع. فإن قيل : المشتري أيضاً يتضرر بأخذ المبيع المعيوب. أجيب : يمكن دفعُ الضَّرر عنه بالردّ بدون تضرر البائع. وإن قيل : البائع إذا باع معيبا فإذا هو سليم، فالبائع يتضرر لما ظهر أنه نقص الثّمن على ظنّ أنه معيب، ولا خيار له ، وعلى هذا فالواجب إما شمولُ الخيار لهما أو عدمه لهما. أجيب : بأن المبيع كان في يد البائع، وتصرّفه وممارسته طول زمانه، فأُنْزِلَ عالماً بصفة ملكه فلا يكون له الخيار، وإن ظهر بخلافه، وأمّا المشتري فإنّه ما رأى المبيع، فلو ألزمناه العقد مع العيب تضرّر من غير علم حصل له فيثبت له الخيار. هذا ما لخصته من «العناية»(۳) و«الكفاية» وغيرهما.
[3] His statement: 'Return it or take it with all its price' - because the absolute contract requires the description of soundness, so when it is lost he has a choice so as not to be harmed by being bound to what he is not satisfied with. This choice between taking and returning persists as long as keeping it is not determined. So when something prevents return, taking is determined, but in some cases he recovers the deficiency of the defect, and in some cases not. As if he bought a slave from someone and sold him to another, then bought him from that other, and saw a defect that existed with the first seller: he does not return it to the one he bought it from because it is useless, since if he returns it the other will return it to him, nor to the first seller because this property is not obtained from him. And as if one of two people in halal bought game from the other, then both or one entered ihram, then the buyer found a defect in it: return is prevented and he recovers the deficiency. Thus in 'Radd al-Muhtar'. [1] His statement: 'Not keep it and take its deficiency' - because what is missing is a description, and descriptions are not compensated by any part of the price in the mere contract, because the price is a substance, so it is matched with the substance not the description, because it (description) is an accidental not a valuable thing; how can a valuable substance match it? And because the price must either be matched with the description and the substance, which equates the substance and the subordinate, or with the description only, which gives precedence to the subordinate over the substance, or with the substance only, which is intended. And because the seller did not consent to the removal of the sold item from his ownership for less than the specified price, and in keeping it and taking the deficiency it leaves for less, so it is not consented, so the seller suffers. If it is said: the buyer also suffers by taking a defective item; the answer: the harm to him can be repelled by return without harming the seller. And if it is said: if the seller sold it as defective but it is sound, the seller suffers because it appears that the price was reduced on the assumption it is defective, and he has no option; therefore either the option should include both or neither. The answer: because the sold item was in the seller's hand, and his dealing and practice over time, so he is considered knowledgeable of the description of his property, so he has no option even if it appears otherwise. As for the buyer, he did not see the item; if we bind him to the contract with the defect, he suffers without knowledge he had, so option is established for him. This is what I summarized from 'Al-Inayah' and 'Al-Kifayah' and others.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb: the buyer has the choice to either return the defective item or keep it and pay the full price (i.e., he cannot demand a reduction in price unless specific cases prevent return). The commentator provides the legal reasoning: the contract implies an unspoken condition of soundness; when that is absent, the buyer gets an option to avoid harm. However, if return is impossible (e.g., after resale and repurchase, or when both parties enter ihram), the buyer may claim compensation for the defect. Then a detailed rational proof is given for why the buyer cannot simply keep the item and take a price reduction: the price is paid for the substance ('ayn), not for descriptions (awsaf). Descriptions are non-valuable accidents, so they cannot be priced separately. Moreover, the seller did not consent to sell for less. The buyer can avoid harm by returning. The seller is presumed knowledgeable of his own property, so he has no option if the defect appears later. This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الرد أو الأخذ بكل ثمنه (al-radd aw al-akhdh bi-kull thamanih): The buyer's option to either return the defective item or keep it and pay the full price (no reduction).
إمساكه وأخذ بنقصانه (imsakuhu wa akhdh bi-nuqsanih): Keeping the item and claiming compensation for the defect; this is not allowed except when return is impossible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default ruling for a buyer who discovers a defect in a purchased item?
A) He may keep it and demand compensation for the defect.
B) He must return it immediately.
C) He may either return it or keep it with the full price.
D) He may keep it and pay a reduced price.

Key Takeaway
A buyer with a defective item must choose between return and full price, unless return is impossible.

The Rational Proof: Why Compensation Is Not Permitted
Rational proof for why the buyer cannot take a price reduction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) for why the Hanafi school does not allow the buyer to keep a defective item and claim compensation, and respond to common objections.

Core Idea
The price is paid for the substance, not the description, so a description cannot be priced separately; thus keeping and taking a reduction is invalid.

The Hanafi school provides a rigorous rational proof for the rule that the buyer cannot keep the defective item and demand a reduction. This proof hinges on the nature of price and what it compensates. The lesson also addresses objections and explains why the seller does not have a reciprocal option.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the central axiom
The price (thaman) is a substance ('ayn) and is paid for the substance of the sold item. Descriptions (awsaf) are accidental and have no market value in the original contract.
The text says: 'الثَّمن عين، فيكون مقابلاً بالعين دون الوصف'.
2
Follow the three logical possibilities
The text analyses three ways the price could be allocated: (a) to both substance and description, which equates the principal and subordinate; (b) to description only, giving precedence to the subordinate; (c) to substance only, which is the intended (al-muram) outcome.
Only (c) is logically coherent; the others lead to absurdities.
3
Understand the seller's perspective
The seller did not consent to the removal of his property for less than the specified price. Allowing the buyer to keep and take a reduction forces a sale at a lower price without consent, harming the seller.
The buyer can avoid his own harm by returning, which does not harm the seller.
4
Address the objection about the seller's option
If the seller sold a defective item that turns out sound, he suffers a loss (he sold for less than the actual value). Why does he not have an option? The response: the seller is presumed knowledgeable of his own property, having dealt with it, so he is considered to have waived any option.
The buyer, who saw nothing, is not presumed knowledgeable, so he gets the option.
5
Recognize the method: qiyas jali
This proof is a rational analogy (qiyas): the asl is a contract with a sound item; the illah is that the price is for the substance; the far' is a contract with a defective item; the hukm is that the buyer cannot keep and take a reduction.
The qiyas is built on tahrir al-manat of the illah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] قوله : ردُّه أو أخذه بكلِّ ثمنه ؛ لأنَّ مطلق العقدِ يقتضي وصف السلامة، فعند فواته يتخير كيلا يتضرر بلزوم ما لا يرضى به وهذا التخير بين الأخذ والرد ما لم يتعيَّن إمساكه ، فإذا وُجد ما يمنع الردَّ يتعيَّن الأخذ، لكن في بعض الصور: يرجعُ بنقصان العيب، وفي بعضها : لا . كما إذا اشترى من آخر عبداً وباعه من غيره ، ثم اشتراه من ذلك الغير، فرأى عيباً كان عند البائع الأول لم يردّه على الذي اشتراه منه ؛ لأنه غير مفيد، إذ لو ردّه يردّه الآخر عليه، ولا على البائع الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ هذا الملك غير مستفاد من جهته. وكما إذا اشترى أحدُ الحَلالَيْنِ من الآخر صيداً ثم أحرما أو أحدهما، ثم وجد المشتري به عيبا امتنع ردّه ورجع بالنقصان. كذا في رد المحتار). [1] قوله : لا إمساكه وأخذه بنقصانه ؛ لأنّ الفائت وصف، والأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في مجرّد العقد ؛ لأنّ الثَّمن عين ، فيكون مقابلاً بالعين دون الوصف ؛ لأنه عرض غير متقوم فأَنِّي يقابله عين متقوم. ولأن الثمن لا يخلو إما أن يقابل بالوصف والأصل، وفيه تسوية عن الأصل والتبع، أو بالوصف فقط، وفيه ترجيح التبع على الأصل، أو بالأصل فقط، وهو المرام ؛ ولأنَّ البائع لم يرض بزوال المبيع عن ملكه بأقل من الثمن المعين، وفي إمساكه وأخذ النقصان زواله بأقلّ، فلم تكن مرضية، فيتضرر به البائع. فإن قيل : المشتري أيضاً يتضرر بأخذ المبيع المعيوب. أجيب : يمكن دفعُ الضَّرر عنه بالردّ بدون تضرر البائع. وإن قيل : البائع إذا باع معيبا فإذا هو سليم، فالبائع يتضرر لما ظهر أنه نقص الثّمن على ظنّ أنه معيب، ولا خيار له ، وعلى هذا فالواجب إما شمولُ الخيار لهما أو عدمه لهما. أجيب : بأن المبيع كان في يد البائع، وتصرّفه وممارسته طول زمانه، فأُنْزِلَ عالماً بصفة ملكه فلا يكون له الخيار، وإن ظهر بخلافه، وأمّا المشتري فإنّه ما رأى المبيع، فلو ألزمناه العقد مع العيب تضرّر من غير علم حصل له فيثبت له الخيار. هذا ما لخصته من «العناية»(۳) و«الكفاية» وغيرهما.
[3] His statement: 'Return it or take it with all its price' - because the absolute contract requires the description of soundness, so when it is lost he has a choice so as not to be harmed by being bound to what he is not satisfied with. This choice between taking and returning persists as long as keeping it is not determined. So when something prevents return, taking is determined, but in some cases he recovers the deficiency of the defect, and in some cases not. As if he bought a slave from someone and sold him to another, then bought him from that other, and saw a defect that existed with the first seller: he does not return it to the one he bought it from because it is useless, since if he returns it the other will return it to him, nor to the first seller because this property is not obtained from him. And as if one of two people in halal bought game from the other, then both or one entered ihram, then the buyer found a defect in it: return is prevented and he recovers the deficiency. Thus in 'Radd al-Muhtar'. [1] His statement: 'Not keep it and take its deficiency' - because what is missing is a description, and descriptions are not compensated by any part of the price in the mere contract, because the price is a substance, so it is matched with the substance not the description, because it (description) is an accidental not a valuable thing; how can a valuable substance match it? And because the price must either be matched with the description and the substance, which equates the substance and the subordinate, or with the description only, which gives precedence to the subordinate over the substance, or with the substance only, which is intended. And because the seller did not consent to the removal of the sold item from his ownership for less than the specified price, and in keeping it and taking the deficiency it leaves for less, so it is not consented, so the seller suffers. If it is said: the buyer also suffers by taking a defective item; the answer: the harm to him can be repelled by return without harming the seller. And if it is said: if the seller sold it as defective but it is sound, the seller suffers because it appears that the price was reduced on the assumption it is defective, and he has no option; therefore either the option should include both or neither. The answer: because the sold item was in the seller's hand, and his dealing and practice over time, so he is considered knowledgeable of the description of his property, so he has no option even if it appears otherwise. As for the buyer, he did not see the item; if we bind him to the contract with the defect, he suffers without knowledge he had, so option is established for him. This is what I summarized from 'Al-Inayah' and 'Al-Kifayah' and others.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling of khiyar al-'ayb: the buyer has the choice to either return the defective item or keep it and pay the full price (i.e., he cannot demand a reduction in price unless specific cases prevent return). The commentator provides the legal reasoning: the contract implies an unspoken condition of soundness; when that is absent, the buyer gets an option to avoid harm. However, if return is impossible (e.g., after resale and repurchase, or when both parties enter ihram), the buyer may claim compensation for the defect. Then a detailed rational proof is given for why the buyer cannot simply keep the item and take a price reduction: the price is paid for the substance ('ayn), not for descriptions (awsaf). Descriptions are non-valuable accidents, so they cannot be priced separately. Moreover, the seller did not consent to sell for less. The buyer can avoid harm by returning. The seller is presumed knowledgeable of his own property, so he has no option if the defect appears later. This is the Hanafi position.

Key Terms
الرد أو الأخذ بكل ثمنه (al-radd aw al-akhdh bi-kull thamanih): The buyer's option to either return the defective item or keep it and pay the full price (no reduction).
إمساكه وأخذ بنقصانه (imsakuhu wa akhdh bi-nuqsanih): Keeping the item and claiming compensation for the defect; this is not allowed except when return is impossible.
الثمن عين (al-thaman 'ayn): The price is a substance; it is a concrete object (usually money) exchanged for the sold item.
الوصف عرض غير متقوم (al-wasf 'arad ghayr mutaqawwim): A description (attribute) is an accidental quality that is not independently valuable in legal consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following statements correctly reflects the Hanafi rational proof for not allowing the buyer to take a price reduction?
A) The price is for the description because the item is defective.
B) The price is for the substance, and descriptions are accidental and not priced.
C) The seller must consent to any reduction, which he never does.
D) The buyer must always return the item.

Key Takeaway
The price compensates only the substance, not descriptions, so keeping and taking a reduction is invalid unless return is impossible.

Ibaq as a Defect: Definition and Ruling
Defines ibaq and distinguishes it from harab; states ruling on defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define ibaq, distinguish it from harab, and state the Hanafi ruling that ibaq is a defect while harab is not.

Core Idea
Ibaq is a defect only when the slave flees without oppression; fleeing from oppression is not a defect.

In Hanafi sales law, the presence of a defect in a purchased slave gives the buyer the option to return the slave (khiyar al-ayb). This lesson clarifies what constitutes a defect in the context of a slave running away. The passage from 'Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira' defines a specific term and draws a crucial distinction that affects buyers' and sellers' rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'ibaq'. Locate the Arabic term and note that it refers to a slave's fleeing.
2
Understand the defining condition
The key condition is that the slave flees 'without oppression' from the master. If oppression is present, the term changes.
Oppression (zulm) could include severe mistreatment, excessive work, or abuse.
3
Contrast with the opposite case
When the slave flees due to oppression, it is called 'harab' (harib), not ibaq. This distinction is fundamental to the legal ruling.
The passage explicitly says: 'if he flees from oppression he is not called abiq, but rather harib.'
4
Derive the legal consequence
Ibaq is considered a defect ('ayb) for which the buyer has the option of defect. Harab is not a defect, so the buyer cannot return the slave on that ground.
The ruling is stated at the end: 'ibaq is a defect and fleeing (harab) is not a defect.'
5
Note the source authority
The passage is quoted from Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira, a Hanafi text. This definition is from the zahir al-riwayah, the relied-upon transmission.
Knowing the authority level helps assess the ruling's strength.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : والإبـاق ؛ في «الجوهرة» (٤) : قال التَّعَالِبيُّ : الآبقُ الهارب من غيرِ ظلم السيد، فإن هرب من الظلم لا يُسمَّى ابقاً، بل يسمّى هارباً، فعلى هذا الإباق عيب والهرب ليس بعيب.
[2] His statement: 'And ibaq' - in 'Al-Jawhara': Al-Ta'alibi said: The 'abiq is the one who flees without oppression from the master, because if he flees from oppression he is not called 'abiq, but rather 'harib'. Based on this, ibaq is a defect, and fleeing (harab) is not a defect.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines ibaq (running away) specifically as fleeing without the master's oppression. If a slave flees due to oppression, it is called harab, not ibaq, and is not considered a defect. Thus, ibaq is a defect for which the buyer may exercise the option of defect, while harab from oppression is not a defect.

Key Terms
إباق (ibāq): A slave's running away from the master without oppression; considered a defect.
هارب (hārib): A slave fleeing due to oppression; not a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when is a slave's running away considered a defect?
A) When the slave runs away for any reason.
B) When the slave runs away without oppression from the master.
C) When the slave runs away due to oppression.
D) When the slave runs away to a foreign land.

Key Takeaway
Ibaq is a defect only when the slave flees without oppression; fleeing from oppression (harab) is not a defect.

The Ruling on Retaining a Defective Item and Claiming Compensation
Commentary proving that buyer cannot retain defective item and claim damages

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on retaining a defective item and state the two rational proofs supporting it.

Core Idea
A buyer cannot both keep a defective item and claim compensation—the defect is a description not met by price, and the seller's consent is to full price only.

In this passage, the commentator unpacks the concise matn ruling that a buyer who discovers a defect in a purchased item must choose between returning the item or keeping it as is. The commentary provides two rational proofs why retaining and claiming damages is invalid. Understanding these proofs strengthens your grasp of Hanafi khiyar al-'ayb and the principle that compensation is tied to the return of the item.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling
Identify exactly what the matn prohibits: the buyer cannot retain the defective item and also claim compensation for the deficiency.
The ruling is clear: either return or retain, but not both.
2
Identify the first proof
Examine the argument based on the nature of price and description. The price is an 'ayin (corporeal thing) and corresponds to the 'ayin of the sold item, not to a description (wasf), which is a non-valuable accident.
Thus, claiming compensation for a defect while retaining the item has no basis in the contract.
3
Understand the second proof
Consider the argument based on seller's consent. The seller agreed to sell only for the full specified price; retaining and claiming damages forces acceptance of a reduced price without the seller's consent.
This would harm the seller, so it is not permitted.
4
Follow the objections and answers
Review the two objections raised and the commentator's responses: (1) buyer's harm can be avoided by return; (2) seller is deemed knowledgeable of his own property, so he cannot claim ignorance after sale.
The buyer's option is protected, but not at the seller's expense.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dispute
Recognize that some scholars (e.g., Shafi'i) allow retaining and claiming compensation, but the Hanafi school firmly prohibits it.
Know that this is a legitimate ikhtilaf with different proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع خيار العيب
لا إمساكه وأخذ نقصانه، والإباقُ
لا إمساكه وأخذ نقصانه) : رَدُّهُ مُبتدأ، وَلِمُشَتِرٍ خَبَرِهِ، ونقص ثمنه : صفة العيب.
والإباق
وكما إذا اشترى أحدُ الحَلالَيْنِ من الآخر صيداً ثم أحرما أو أحدهما، ثم وجد المشتري به عيبا امتنع ردّه ورجع بالنقصان. كذا في رد المحتار). [1] قوله : لا إمساكه وأخذه بنقصانه ؛ لأنّ الفائت ،وصف، والأوصاف لا يقابلها شيء من الثمن في مجرّد العقد ؛ لأنّ الثَّمن عين ، فيكون مقابلاً بالعين دون الوصف ؛ لأنه عرض غير متقوم فأَنِّي يقابله عين متقوم.
ولأن الثمن لا يخلو إما أن يقابل بالوصف والأصل، وفيه تسوية عن الأصل والتبع، أو بالوصف فقط، وفيه ترجيح التبع على الأصل، أو بالأصل فقط، وهو المرام ؛ ولأنَّ البائع لم يرض بزوال المبيع عن ملكه بأقل من الثمن المعين، وفي إمساكه وأخذ النقصان زواله بأقلّ، فلم تكن مرضية، فيتضرر به البائع.
فإن قيل : المشتري أيضاً يتضرر بأخذ المبيع المعيوب.
أجيب : يمكن دفعُ الضَّرر عنه بالردّ بدون تضرر البائع.
وإن قيل : البائع إذا باع معيبا فإذا هو سليم، فالبائع يتضرر لما ظهر أنه نقص الثّمن على ظنّ أنه معيب، ولا خيار له ، وعلى هذا فالواجب إما شمولُ الخيار لهما أو عدمه لهما.
أجيب : بأن المبيع كان في يد البائع، وتصرّفه وممارسته طول زمانه، فأُنْزِلَ عالماً بصفة ملكه فلا يكون له الخيار، وإن ظهر بخلافه، وأمّا المشتري فإنّه ما رأى المبيع، فلو ألزمناه العقد مع العيب تضرّر من غير علم حصل له فيثبت له الخيار. هذا ما لخصته من «العناية»(۳) و«الكفاية» وغيرهما.
[Kitab al-Bay' Khiyar al-'Ayb] [79] [Statement: He does not retain it and take its deficiency, and absconding.] [Commentary: 'He does not retain it and take its deficiency' - its rejection is a mubtada, and 'for the buyer' is its khabar, and 'deficiency of its price' is the description of the defect. And absconding. And just as if one of the two people in ihram buys game from the other, then they both or one of them enter ihram, then the buyer finds a defect in it, he is prevented from returning it and claims the deficiency. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar.] [1] His statement: 'He does not retain it and take its deficiency' - because the missing part is a description, and descriptions are not met by any part of the price in the mere contract, because the price is an 'ayin (corporeal thing), so it corresponds to the 'ayin, not the description, since the description is a non-valuable accident, so how can a valuable 'ayin correspond to it? And because the price is either: it corresponds to the description and the essence, which equates the essence and the accident; or to the description only, preferring the accident over the essence; or to the essence only, which is intended. And because the seller did not consent to the removal of the sold item from his ownership for less than the specified price, and in retaining it and taking the deficiency, its removal is for less, so it is not consentable, and the seller is harmed. If it is said: The buyer is also harmed by taking the defective sold item. The answer: Harm to him can be removed by returning without harm to the seller. And if it is said: When the seller sells a defective item and then it turns out to be sound, the seller is harmed because it appears that he decreased the price on the assumption it was defective, and he has no option; this would require either the option for both or neither. The answer: Because the sold item was in the seller's hand, and he dealt with and handled it for a long time, so he is considered knowledgeable of the quality of his property, so he has no option, even if the opposite appears. As for the buyer, he did not see the sold item, so if we enforce the contract with the defect, he is harmed without knowledge, so the option is established for him. This is what I summarized from al-'Inayah, al-Kifayah, and others.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn that states the buyer cannot retain the defective item and accept compensation for the defect. The commentator provides two rational proofs: first, the defect is a description (wasf), and descriptions are not met with part of the price in the contract because the price is an 'ayin (corporeal thing) and corresponds only to the 'ayin of the sold item. Second, the seller did not consent to the sale at a lesser price; allowing the buyer to retain and claim damages would force the seller to accept a reduced price without his consent. The commentary then refutes objections: the buyer's harm can be avoided by returning the item; and the seller is deemed knowledgeable of his own property, so he cannot claim after sale that he was unaware of a defect, whereas the buyer had no prior knowledge, so he is granted the option.

Key Terms
إمساك (imsaak): Retaining the defective item by the buyer after discovering the defect.
نقصان (nuqsaan): The monetary compensation claimed for the reduction in value due to the defect.
عين (ʿayn): A corporeal thing; the physical item itself as opposed to its attributes.
وصف (waṣf): A description or attribute of the item, such as soundness or defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the buyer cannot retain the defective item and take the deficiency?
A) Because the defect is a description and descriptions are not met with price in the contract.
B) Because the seller did not consent to the sale at a lesser price.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because the buyer must always return a defective item.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi khiyar al-'ayb, a buyer who retains a defective item forfeits any claim for compensation; the only valid options are return for full refund or retention without compensation.

Defects in Slaves: 'Ibaq, Sarqah, and Bawl
Defining three defects in slaves with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which ibaaq, sariqah, and bawl qualify as defects in a slave, and apply the rule that the defect must be present with a unified cause for the right of return.

Core Idea
Ibaq, sariqah, and bawl are only defects if the underlying cause is the same at sale and discovery.

In Hanafi sales law, when you buy a slave, certain defects entitle you to return the slave (khiyar al-'ayb). This passage defines three particular defects: absconding, theft, and bedwetting. But not every instance of these acts is a defect—the law looks at the cause. A slave who flees from oppression is not an absconder; a child who wets the bed has a weak bladder, not a disease. Understanding these nuances protects both buyer and seller.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts
The passage identifies three acts: absconding (ibaaq), theft (sariqah), and bedwetting (bawl). For each, determine when it becomes a legal defect.
These are specific to the context of slave sales.
2
Define absconding (ibaaq)
Ibaq is fleeing without oppression. If the slave flees due to oppression, it is not a defect. Absconding is a defect whether the slave travels a journey distance or less, because both cause loss of benefits. If the slave does not leave the town, the scholars differed: in large towns it is a defect, in small towns it is not.
The distinction between large and small towns depends on whether the slave can hide.
3
Define theft (sariqah)
Theft is a defect except when the slave steals food for consumption from the master. Stealing to sell or stealing from non-master is a defect. Trivial amounts (like a fils) are not a defect, but breaking into a house without taking is a defect.
The exception is based on the purpose and the victim.
4
Define bedwetting (bawl)
Bedwetting is a defect. The cause differs: in minors it is bladder weakness; in adults it is internal disease. This distinction matters for the rule of unity of cause.
Age changes the legal classification of the same act.
5
Apply the rule of unity of cause
The right to return exists only if the defect was present at the time of sale. If the same act occurs later, but the cause is different (e.g., child wets bed due to weakness, adult due to disease), the buyer cannot return the slave because the defect is not the same.
This principle ensures that the seller is not liable for defects arising after sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢]قوله : والإبـاق ؛ في «الجوهرة» (٤) : قال التَّعَالِبيُّ : الآبقُ الهارب من غيرِ ظلم السيد، فإن هرب من الظلم لا يُسمَّى ابقاً، بل يسمّى هارباً، فعلى هذا الإباق عيب والهرب ليس بعيب.
[ ١ ] قوله : ولو إلى ما دون سفر ؛ لأنّ الإباق إنما كان عيباً ؛ لأنه يوجب فوات المنافع على المولى، وفي هذا المعنى السفر وما دونه سواء.
وفي «كمال الدراية»: «الإباق عيب بالاتفاق، إن خرج الآبق من البلد إلى غير مولاه سواءً أيق من المولى أو ممن كان عنده بإجارة أو بإعارة أو بوديعة أو بغصب وهو يعرف منزله ، ويقوى على الرّجوع إليه ، وإن لم يخرج من البلد اختلف المشايخ ، والأشبه أن يقال : إن كانت البلدة كبيرة كالقاهرة يكون عيباً، وإن كانت صغيرة بحيث لا يخفى عليه أهلُها وبيوتُها لا يكون عيباً». انتهى
[٢]قوله : وسرقة صغير؛ إلا إذا سرق شيئاً للأكل من المولى، فإنّه لا يكون عيباً، بخلاف ما إذا سرق لبيعه ، أو سرقه من غير المولى ليأكله فإنه عيب فيهما. كذا في «البحر»، وفي «كمال الدراية» : ولو سرق نحو الفلس والفلسين لا يكون عيباً، ولو نقب البيت ولم يأخذ منه فهو عيب.
[٣] قوله : صغير ; اعلم أنّ هذه الأشياءُ الثَّلاثة عيب يردّ بها العبد والأمة، لكنَّ الردّ إنما يثبت إذا وَجَدَ المشتري عيباً منها كان عند البائع، ولا يثبت بالعيب الحادث ، فإذا وجد أحد هذه الأشياء في الصَّغير أو في الكبير عند البائع والمشتري جميعاً يُرَد. وإن وُجد أحدها في الصغير عند البائع ثم في الكبير عند المشتري لا يُرَد؛ لعدم اتحادِ السبب ؛ لأنَّ البول في الفراش في الصغير لضعف المثانة، وفي الكبير لداء في الباطن، والإباق في الصَّغير لحب اللعب، والسّرقة لقلة المبالاة، وهما بعد الكبر لخبث في الباطن فراراً عن العمل ورغبة في المال، وهذا هو المفهوم من «الهداية»، و«الكافي». ويظهر من هذا أن الصغر ليس هنا قيداً في السرقة كما يُوْهِمُ ظاهر عبارة المتن ، فينبغي أن يجعل اللام في الإباق، والبول عوضاً عن المضاف إليه : أي إباق صغير وبول
[2] His statement: 'And absconding'; in al-Jawharah: al-Ta'alibi said: The absconder (abaaq) is the one who flees without oppression from the master. If he flees due to oppression, he is not called an absconder (abiq) but a fugitive (haarib). Thus, absconding is a defect, but fleeing is not a defect. [1] His statement: 'Even if it is less than a journey' - because absconding is a defect because it causes loss of benefits for the master, and in this meaning, a journey and less than a journey are equal. And in Kamal al-Dirayah: 'Absconding is a defect by agreement. If the absconder leaves the town to another place, whether he is from the master or from someone with whom he is by hire, loan, deposit, or usurpation, and he knows his home and is able to return to it, then it is a defect. If he does not leave the town, the mashaa'ikh differed. The more appropriate view is that if the town is large, like Cairo, it is a defect; if it is small, such that its people and houses are not hidden from him, it is not a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'And theft of a minor' - except if he steals something for food from the master, then it is not a defect. As opposed to if he steals to sell it, or steals from someone other than the master to eat it, then it is a defect in both cases. Thus in al-Bahr. And in Kamal al-Dirayah: If he steals something like a fils or two fils, it is not a defect; if he breaks into a house but does not take anything, it is a defect. [3] His statement: 'Minor' - know that these three things are defects for which a male or female slave is returned. However, the return is established only if the buyer finds such a defect that was present with the seller, and it is not established by a later defect. So if one of these things is found in a minor or an adult with the seller and the buyer together, it is returned. If it is found in a minor with the seller and then in an adult with the buyer, it is not returned due to the lack of unity of cause. Because bedwetting in a minor is due to bladder weakness, but in an adult due to internal disease; absconding in a minor is due to love of play, and theft due to lack of care; while both after adulthood are due to inner wickedness, avoiding work and desiring money. This is what is understood from al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. And from this it appears that being minor is not a restriction for theft as the apparent wording of the matn might suggest. So the lam in 'absconding' and 'bedwetting' should be considered as a substitute for the added particle: meaning 'absconding of a minor' and 'bedwetting'
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines and elaborates upon three specific defects in slaves: absconding (Ibaq), theft (Sarqah), and bedwetting (Bawl). The commentator begins by defining Ibaq: it is fleeing without oppression; if the slave flees due to oppression, it is not considered a defect. He then clarifies that absconding is a defect whether the slave goes on a journey or stays within a journey's distance because both cause loss of benefits. A quotation from Kamal al-Dirayah adds that there is consensus that absconding is a defect if the slave leaves the town, but if he remains within the town, the scholars differed: in a large town like Cairo it is a defect, but in a small town it is not. Regarding theft, the passage distinguishes: stealing food from the master for consumption is not a defect; but stealing to sell or stealing from a non-master for consumption is a defect. Trivial theft (a few coins) is not a defect, but breaking into a house without taking anything is a defect. Finally, the passage notes that these three defects are grounds for return only if they existed at the time of sale. If the same act occurs in the slave after it becomes adult, it is not the same defect because the causes are different: in minors, these acts stem from weakness or immaturity, while in adults they stem from inherent vice. Thus the minor status is relevant for absconding and bedwetting but not for theft.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaaq): Absconding of a slave; fleeing from the master without oppression. Considered a defect if the slave leaves the town or, in a large town, even within it.
سرقة (sariqah): Theft by a slave. Considered a defect except when stealing food for consumption from the master or stealing a trivial amount.
بول (bawl): Urination in bed. Considered a defect in a slave, but the cause differs between minors (bladder weakness) and adults (internal disease).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what circumstance is theft by a slave NOT considered a defect?
A) When the stolen item is worth a large amount.
B) When the slave steals food from the master for consumption.
C) When the slave steals from another neighbor.
D) When the slave steals to sell the item.

Key Takeaway
Ibaq, sariqah, and bawl are defects only under specific conditions; the most critical point is that the cause of the defect must be the same at sale and at discovery for the right of return.

Ibaq (Running Away) as a Defect in a Slave Sale
Commentary on ibaq as a defect with talil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of ibaq as a defect and distinguish between its application in large and small towns.

Core Idea
Ibaq is a defect because it causes loss of benefits; distance is irrelevant, and within a large town it may still be a defect.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the defect of ibaq (running away) in the sale of a slave. The commentary emphasizes that the underlying reason is the loss of benefits, so the distance to which the slave runs is irrelevant as long as benefits are lost. It also resolves a dispute among scholars about ibaq within the town.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
State that ibaq is a defect giving the buyer the right to return the slave, provided it causes loss of benefits to the master.
The defect is based on the 'illa of fawat al-manafi'.
2
Analyze the 'illa
Recognize that the effective cause (al-'illa) is the loss of benefits; this is why even a short distance counts.
The text says: 'because ibaq is a defect only because it causes loss of benefits'.
3
Apply the condition of distance
Even if the slave runs less than a journey (safar), it is still a defect because the 'illa is the same.
The matn says 'even if to less than a journey'.
4
Distinguish large vs small town
If the slave does not leave the town, scholars differ. The preferred view is: if the town is large (like Cairo), it is a defect; if small, it is not.
This is from Kamil al-Dirayah.

Core of the final commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: 'And even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, and even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, etc.' [1] His statement: 'And even if to less than a journey' because running away is a defect only because it causes loss of benefits to the master, and in this meaning, journey and less than it are equal. (1) And in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'Ibaq is a defect by agreement, if the runaway leaves the town to other than his master, whether he ran from the master or from someone who had him by hire, loan, trust, or usurpation, while he knows his home and is able to return to it. If he did not leave the town, the mashayikh differ, and the most appropriate is: if the town is large like Cairo, it is a defect; if small such that its people and houses are not hidden from him, it is not a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'And theft of a minor' except if he steals something to eat from the master, then it is not a defect, contrary to if he steals to sell it, or steals from other than the master to eat it, then it is a defect in both. Thus in 'al-Bahr', and in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'If he steals something like a fils or two fils, it is not a defect; if he breaks into the house and does not take anything, it is a defect.' [3] His statement: 'minor'; know that these three things are defects by which a male or female slave is returned, but the return is only established if the buyer finds the defect existed with the seller, and not by a defect that arises later. So if one of these things is found in the minor or in the adult with both the seller and buyer, he is returned. If one of them is found in the minor with the seller and then in the adult with the buyer, he is not returned due to lack of unity of cause. Because urinating in the bed in a minor is due to weakness of the bladder, and in an adult due to internal disease; running away in a minor is due to love of play; theft due to lack of care; and both after adulthood are due to internal malice, fleeing from work and desire for wealth. This is understood from 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Kafi'. It appears from this that minor is not a condition for theft here, as the apparent wording of the matn suggests, so the lam in 'ibaq' and 'bowl' should be considered in place of the annexed: i.e., ibaq of a minor and urination of one who is discerning are a defect, and for an adult another defect. So if he stole from both seller and buyer in his childhood discerningly, he returns it; and madness of a minor is always a defect. 'Discerning' defect: He only said 'discerning' because theft of a nondiscerning minor is not a defect. 'And for an adult another defect' is conjunction of two different operators, and the genitive is fronted. 'So if he stole in his childhood from both' i.e., with the seller and buyer, in his childhood with discernment, he returns him; but if it occurred with him as a child, and with the buyer as an adult, no. 'And madness of a minor is always a defect' for a minor, according to what al-Birjandi chose, and if he said 'of a minor' it would be better and more inclusive. [1] His statement: 'discerning' i.e., buying and selling, al-Birjandi chose it; and it is said that these things in a minor are not defects if he eats and drinks alone; and it is said they are only defects if he is five years or above, otherwise not. [2] His statement: 'defect' because these things cause decrease in value among merchants. [3] His statement: 'He only said... etc.' The commentator's specification is not valid, for the minor has relation to the three things in the ruling, not only theft. [4] His statement: 'another defect' for urinating on the bed before puberty is due to bladder weakness, after it due to internal disease; running away before it due to love of play, theft before it due to lack of care, after it both are due to internal malice. So when the state is the same, he returns; when different, not. [5] His statement: 'and the genitive is fronted'; as it is said: 'In the house is Zaid and in the room is Amr'; so his word 'adult' is coordinated with 'minor' and is genitive, and his word 'another defect' is coordinated with 'defect' and is nominative. The more apparent is that it should be said: theft in a minor is a defect, so in an adult it is another defect. [6] His statement: 'and madness of a minor' and madness is the disturbance of the power that distinguishes between good and bad things and perceives consequences, such that its effects do not appear and its actions are rendered inactive, either due to a deficiency in the brain's constitution, or due to imbalance of the brain's temperament from a humor or disease, or due to the devil's domination and casting of false imaginations so that he rejoices or fears without cause. In analogy it is invalidating of all acts of worship because it negates capability; and for this reason prophets are protected from it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the chapter of defects in sales, specifically addressing three defects that allow the buyer to return a slave: running away (ibaq), bed-wetting (al-bawl fi al-firash), and theft (sariqah). The commentary elaborates on the matn's statement that these defects apply even if the running away is to a distance less than a journey, because the underlying reason is the loss of benefit to the master, which is the same regardless of distance. It cites 'Kamil al-Dirayah' to clarify that ibaq is unanimously a defect if the slave leaves the town, but if he does not leave the town, scholars differ; a large town like Cairo makes it a defect, a small town does not. For theft, stealing food from the master for eating is not a defect, but stealing for sale or from others is; also stealing a very small amount is not a defect, but breaking into a house without taking is a defect. The key distinction is between the same act in childhood vs. adulthood. In a child, bed-wetting is due to a weak bladder, running away due to love of play, theft due to lack of care; in an adult, they indicate internal malice. Therefore, if the defect existed in the same state at both seller and buyer, return is allowed; but if it occurred as a child at the seller and as an adult at the buyer, it is considered a different defect and return is not allowed. The text emphasizes that for theft, the child must be discerning (ya'qil); theft by a nons discerning child is not a defect. Madness (junun) is always a defect. The commentator also discusses the grammatical construction of the matn's phrasing.

Key Terms
إباق (ibaq): Running away of a slave from the master, considered a defect in the sale if it causes loss of benefits.
عيب ('ayb): Defect in the subject of sale; a physical or moral flaw that reduces value, allowing the buyer to return the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is true about ibaq (running away) as a defect?
A) It is a defect only if the slave leaves the town.
B) It is a defect if it causes loss of benefits, even within a large town.
C) It is a defect only if the slave runs to a journey's distance.
D) It is never a defect if the slave stays within the town.

Key Takeaway
Ibaq is a defect due to loss of benefits; distance is irrelevant, and within a large town it may still be a defect.

Theft and Bed-Wetting as Defects in Slaves
Commentary on theft and bed-wetting as defects with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on theft and bed-wetting as defects in a slave, including exceptions for stealing from the master and small amounts, and the requirement of discernment.

Core Idea
Theft is a defect unless it is for eating from the master; bed-wetting in a child is due to weak bladder, not malice.

This passage addresses two further defects: theft (sariqah) and bed-wetting (al-bawl fi al-firash). The commentary provides important exceptions—stealing food from the master is not a defect, and stealing a minimal amount is also not a defect. It also stresses that the child must be discerning for theft to be considered a defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for theft
Theft by a discerning minor is generally a defect, with exceptions.
The text says 'theft of a minor' implies a discerning minor.
2
List the exceptions for theft
Sleeping food from the master for eating is not a defect; stealing for sale or from others is. Stealing a very small amount (like a fils) is not a defect; breaking into a house without taking is a defect.
These exceptions are from al-Bahr and Kamil al-Dirayah.
3
Analyze bed-wetting
Bed-wetting in a child is due to weak bladder (not malice), so it is a defect only if it existed at the time of sale.
The cause changes in adulthood.
4
Note the discernment requirement
For theft to be a defect, the child must be discerning (ya'qil), meaning he understands buying and selling.
The text explicitly says 'theft of a non-discerning minor is not a defect'.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية ولو إلى ما دون سفر، والبول في الفراش، وسرقة صغير ولو إلى ما دون سفر ، والبول في الفراش، وسرقة صغير لدا
[ ١ ] قوله : ولو إلى ما دون سفر ؛ لأنّ الإباق إنما كان عيباً ؛ لأنه يوجب فوات المنافع على المولى، وفي هذا المعنى السفر وما دونه سواء.
(1)
وفي «كمال الدراية»: «الإباق عيب بالاتفاق، إن خرج الآبق من البلد إلى غير مولاه سواءً أيق من المولى أو ممن كان عنده بإجارة أو بإعارة أو بوديعة أو بغصب وهو يعرف منزله ، ويقوى على الرّجوع إليه ، وإن لم يخرج من البلد اختلف المشايخ ، والأشبه أن يقال : إن كانت البلدة كبيرة كالقاهرة يكون عيباً، وإن كانت صغيرة بحيث لا يخفى عليه أهلُها وبيوتُها لا يكون عيباً». انتهى [٢]قوله : وسرقة صغير؛ إلا إذا سرق شيئاً للأكل من المولى، فإنّه لا يكون عيباً، بخلاف ما إذا سرق لبيعه ، أو سرقه من غير المولى ليأكله فإنه عيب فيهما. كذا في «البحر»)، وفي «كمال الدراية (۳) : ولو سرق نحو الفلس والفلسين لا يكون عيباً، ولو نقب البيت ولم يأخذ منه فهو عيب.
[٣] قوله : صغير ؛ اعلم أنّ هذه الأشياءُ الثَّلاثة عيب يردّ بها العبد والأمة، لكنَّ الردّ إنما يثبت إذا وَجَدَ المشتري عيباً منها كان عند البائع، ولا يثبت بالعيب الحادث ،
فإذا وجد أحد هذه الأشياء في الصَّغير أو في الكبير عند البائع والمشتري جميعاً يُرَد. وإن وُجد أحدها في الصغير عند البائع ثم في الكبير عند المشتري لا يُرَد؛ لعدم اتحادِ السبب ؛ لأنَّ البول في الفراش في الصغير لضعف المثانة، وفي الكبير لداء في الباطن، والإباق في الصَّغير لحب اللعب، والسّرقة لقلة المبالاة، وهما بعد الكبر لخبث في الباطن فراراً عن العمل ورغبة في المال، وهذا هو المفهوم من «الهداية»(4)، و«الكافي». ويظهر من هذا أن الصغر ليس هنا قيداً في السرقة كما يُوْهِمُ ظاهر عبارة المتن ، فينبغي أن يجعل اللام في الإباق، والبول عوضاً عن المضاف إليه : أي إباق صغير وبول يَعْقِلُ عيب، وبالغ عيب آخر ، فلو سرق عندهما في صغرِهِ رَدَّهُ، وجنون الصغير عيب أبداً
يَعْقِلُ" عيب " : إِنَّمَا قال : يَعْقِلُ ؛ لأنَّ سرقة صغير لا يَعْقِلُ ليست بعيب، (وبالغ عيب آخر) ، عطف على مَعْمُولّي عاملين مختلفين، والمجرور مقدم، (فلو سرق عندهما) : أي عند البائع والمشتري، في صغره) : أي في صغره مع
العقل (رَدَّهُ) وإن حدث عنده في صغره، وعند مشتريه في كِبَرِهِ لا .
( وجنون الصغير" عيب أبدا
صغير، لما اختاره البرْجَنْدِي، ولو قال : من صغير لكان أحسن وأشمل.
[١) قوله : يعقل ؛ أي البيع والشراء، اختاره البرْجَندِي ، وقيل : بأن هذه الأشياء في الصغير، قيل: أن يأكل وحده ويشرب وحده ليست بعيوب ، وقيل : إنما يكون عيباً
إذا كان خمس سنين فما فوقها، وأمّا فيما دون ذلك فليس بعيب.
[٢] قوله : عيب ؛ لأنّ هذه الأشياءَ تُوجب نقصان القيمة عند التجار. [٣]قوله : إنما قال ... الخ ؛ تخصيص الشارح غير وجيه ، فإِنَّ الصَّغيرَ له تعلق بالأشياء الثلاثة في الحكم لا بالسرقة فقط.
[٤] قوله : عيب آخر ؛ فإنَّ البول على الفراش قبل البلوغ لضعف المثانة، وبعده لداء في الباطن، والإباق قبله لحب اللعب، والسّرقة قبله ؛ لقلة المبالات، وبعده يكونان لخبث في الباطن ، فعند اتحاد الحالة يردُّ، وفي الاختلاف لا.
[0] قوله : والمجرور مقدَّم ؛ كما يقال : في الدار زيد والحجرة عمرو ، فقوله : «بالغ» ؛ معطوف على قوله : «صغير»، وهو مجرور ، وقوله : «عيب آخر» معطوف على قوله: «عيب»، وهو مرفوع، والأظهر أن يقال : والسرقة في الصغير عيب، ففي الكبير
عيب آخر. [٦] قوله : وجنون الصغير ؛ والجنونُ اختلالُ القوة المميزة بين الأمور الحسنة والقبيحة المدركة للعواقب، بأن لا يظهر آثارها ، وبتعطَّل أفعالها : إمّا لنقصان جُيل عليه الدماغ في أصل الخلقة، وإمّا لخروج مِزاج الدماغ عن الاعتدال بسبب خلط وآفة ، وإما لاستيلاء الشيطان عليه وإلقاء الخيالاتِ الفاسدة إليه بحيث يفرح ويفزع من غير ما يصلح سبباً، وهو في القياس مسقط لكلِّ العبادات ؛ لمنافاته القدرة ؛ ولذا عصم الأنبياء
عليهم السلام عنه.
Core of the final commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: 'And even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, and even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, etc.' [1] His statement: 'And even if to less than a journey' because running away is a defect only because it causes loss of benefits to the master, and in this meaning, journey and less than it are equal. (1) And in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'Ibaq is a defect by agreement, if the runaway leaves the town to other than his master, whether he ran from the master or from someone who had him by hire, loan, trust, or usurpation, while he knows his home and is able to return to it. If he did not leave the town, the mashayikh differ, and the most appropriate is: if the town is large like Cairo, it is a defect; if small such that its people and houses are not hidden from him, it is not a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'And theft of a minor' except if he steals something to eat from the master, then it is not a defect, contrary to if he steals to sell it, or steals from other than the master to eat it, then it is a defect in both. Thus in 'al-Bahr', and in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'If he steals something like a fils or two fils, it is not a defect; if he breaks into the house and does not take anything, it is a defect.' [3] His statement: 'minor'; know that these three things are defects by which a male or female slave is returned, but the return is only established if the buyer finds the defect existed with the seller, and not by a defect that arises later. So if one of these things is found in the minor or in the adult with both the seller and buyer, he is returned. If one of them is found in the minor with the seller and then in the adult with the buyer, he is not returned due to lack of unity of cause. Because urinating in the bed in a minor is due to weakness of the bladder, and in an adult due to internal disease; running away in a minor is due to love of play; theft due to lack of care; and both after adulthood are due to internal malice, fleeing from work and desire for wealth. This is understood from 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Kafi'. It appears from this that minor is not a condition for theft here, as the apparent wording of the matn suggests, so the lam in 'ibaq' and 'bowl' should be considered in place of the annexed: i.e., ibaq of a minor and urination of one who is discerning are a defect, and for an adult another defect. So if he stole from both seller and buyer in his childhood discerningly, he returns it; and madness of a minor is always a defect. 'Discerning' defect: He only said 'discerning' because theft of a nondiscerning minor is not a defect. 'And for an adult another defect' is conjunction of two different operators, and the genitive is fronted. 'So if he stole in his childhood from both' i.e., with the seller and buyer, in his childhood with discernment, he returns him; but if it occurred with him as a child, and with the buyer as an adult, no. 'And madness of a minor is always a defect' for a minor, according to what al-Birjandi chose, and if he said 'of a minor' it would be better and more inclusive. [1] His statement: 'discerning' i.e., buying and selling, al-Birjandi chose it; and it is said that these things in a minor are not defects if he eats and drinks alone; and it is said they are only defects if he is five years or above, otherwise not. [2] His statement: 'defect' because these things cause decrease in value among merchants. [3] His statement: 'He only said... etc.' The commentator's specification is not valid, for the minor has relation to the three things in the ruling, not only theft. [4] His statement: 'another defect' for urinating on the bed before puberty is due to bladder weakness, after it due to internal disease; running away before it due to love of play, theft before it due to lack of care, after it both are due to internal malice. So when the state is the same, he returns; when different, not. [5] His statement: 'and the genitive is fronted'; as it is said: 'In the house is Zaid and in the room is Amr'; so his word 'adult' is coordinated with 'minor' and is genitive, and his word 'another defect' is coordinated with 'defect' and is nominative. The more apparent is that it should be said: theft in a minor is a defect, so in an adult it is another defect. [6] His statement: 'and madness of a minor' and madness is the disturbance of the power that distinguishes between good and bad things and perceives consequences, such that its effects do not appear and its actions are rendered inactive, either due to a deficiency in the brain's constitution, or due to imbalance of the brain's temperament from a humor or disease, or due to the devil's domination and casting of false imaginations so that he rejoices or fears without cause. In analogy it is invalidating of all acts of worship because it negates capability; and for this reason prophets are protected from it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the chapter of defects in sales, specifically addressing three defects that allow the buyer to return a slave: running away (ibaq), bed-wetting (al-bawl fi al-firash), and theft (sariqah). The commentary elaborates on the matn's statement that these defects apply even if the running away is to a distance less than a journey, because the underlying reason is the loss of benefit to the master, which is the same regardless of distance. It cites 'Kamil al-Dirayah' to clarify that ibaq is unanimously a defect if the slave leaves the town, but if he does not leave the town, scholars differ; a large town like Cairo makes it a defect, a small town does not. For theft, stealing food from the master for eating is not a defect, but stealing for sale or from others is; also stealing a very small amount is not a defect, but breaking into a house without taking is a defect. The key distinction is between the same act in childhood vs. adulthood. In a child, bed-wetting is due to a weak bladder, running away due to love of play, theft due to lack of care; in an adult, they indicate internal malice. Therefore, if the defect existed in the same state at both seller and buyer, return is allowed; but if it occurred as a child at the seller and as an adult at the buyer, it is considered a different defect and return is not allowed. The text emphasizes that for theft, the child must be discerning (ya'qil); theft by a nons discerning child is not a defect. Madness (junun) is always a defect. The commentator also discusses the grammatical construction of the matn's phrasing.

Key Terms
سرقة (sariqah): Theft; in a discerning child it is a defect unless it is for eating from the master's food or negligible amount.
يعقل (ya'qil): Discerning, having the ability to understand buying and selling; a prerequisite for theft to be considered a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following is NOT a defect in a slave?
A) A discerning minor steals a coin from a neighbor to buy candy.
B) A discerning minor steals a loaf of bread from the master to eat.
C) A discerning minor breaks into the master's house but takes nothing.
D) A non-discerning minor steals a valuable item.

Key Takeaway
Theft is a defect only if it involves a discerning minor, is not for eating from the master, and is not negligible.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Unity of Cause and Madness as a Defect
Explanation of unity of cause and madness as a defect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a defect must be the same at the time of seller and buyer for the return to be valid, and apply this to the case of madness.

Core Idea
A defect in a slave is only returnable if it is the same cause; childhood and adulthood causes differ, so no return; madness is always a defect.

This passage introduces the crucial concept of unity of cause ('illah). If a defect like theft occurs in childhood for one reason and in adulthood for another, it is not the same defect and cannot be a ground for return. Madness, however, is always a defect regardless of age because it permanently affects the slave's value.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the principle of unity of cause
The defect must be the same in cause at both seller and buyer; if the cause differs, the defect is not the same.
The text says: 'لا يرد لعدم اتحاد السبب'.
2
Examine the examples
Bed-wetting in a child is due to weak bladder; in an adult it is due to disease. Running away in a child is love of play; in an adult it is malice. Theft in a child is carelessness; in an adult it is malice.
These differences break the unity of cause.
3
Apply the principle
If the defect occurred in childhood at the seller's and in adulthood at the buyer's, return is not allowed. If it occurred in the same stage (both child or both adult) at both ends, return is allowed.
The text gives the example of theft: if the child stole while discerning at both seller and buyer, return is allowed.
4
Identify madness as an exception
Madness (junun) is always a defect, regardless of age, because it is a permanent disturbance of the distinguishing faculty.
The text says 'جنون الصغير عيب أبداً'.
