عليهم السلام عنه.
Core of the final commentary of 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah: 'And even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, and even if to less than a journey, and urinating in the bed, and theft of a minor, etc.' [1] His statement: 'And even if to less than a journey' because running away is a defect only because it causes loss of benefits to the master, and in this meaning, journey and less than it are equal. (1) And in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'Ibaq is a defect by agreement, if the runaway leaves the town to other than his master, whether he ran from the master or from someone who had him by hire, loan, trust, or usurpation, while he knows his home and is able to return to it. If he did not leave the town, the mashayikh differ, and the most appropriate is: if the town is large like Cairo, it is a defect; if small such that its people and houses are not hidden from him, it is not a defect.' End. [2] His statement: 'And theft of a minor' except if he steals something to eat from the master, then it is not a defect, contrary to if he steals to sell it, or steals from other than the master to eat it, then it is a defect in both. Thus in 'al-Bahr', and in 'Kamil al-Dirayah': 'If he steals something like a fils or two fils, it is not a defect; if he breaks into the house and does not take anything, it is a defect.' [3] His statement: 'minor'; know that these three things are defects by which a male or female slave is returned, but the return is only established if the buyer finds the defect existed with the seller, and not by a defect that arises later. So if one of these things is found in the minor or in the adult with both the seller and buyer, he is returned. If one of them is found in the minor with the seller and then in the adult with the buyer, he is not returned due to lack of unity of cause. Because urinating in the bed in a minor is due to weakness of the bladder, and in an adult due to internal disease; running away in a minor is due to love of play; theft due to lack of care; and both after adulthood are due to internal malice, fleeing from work and desire for wealth. This is understood from 'al-Hidayah' and 'al-Kafi'. It appears from this that minor is not a condition for theft here, as the apparent wording of the matn suggests, so the lam in 'ibaq' and 'bowl' should be considered in place of the annexed: i.e., ibaq of a minor and urination of one who is discerning are a defect, and for an adult another defect. So if he stole from both seller and buyer in his childhood discerningly, he returns it; and madness of a minor is always a defect. 'Discerning' defect: He only said 'discerning' because theft of a nondiscerning minor is not a defect. 'And for an adult another defect' is conjunction of two different operators, and the genitive is fronted. 'So if he stole in his childhood from both' i.e., with the seller and buyer, in his childhood with discernment, he returns him; but if it occurred with him as a child, and with the buyer as an adult, no. 'And madness of a minor is always a defect' for a minor, according to what al-Birjandi chose, and if he said 'of a minor' it would be better and more inclusive. [1] His statement: 'discerning' i.e., buying and selling, al-Birjandi chose it; and it is said that these things in a minor are not defects if he eats and drinks alone; and it is said they are only defects if he is five years or above, otherwise not. [2] His statement: 'defect' because these things cause decrease in value among merchants. [3] His statement: 'He only said... etc.' The commentator's specification is not valid, for the minor has relation to the three things in the ruling, not only theft. [4] His statement: 'another defect' for urinating on the bed before puberty is due to bladder weakness, after it due to internal disease; running away before it due to love of play, theft before it due to lack of care, after it both are due to internal malice. So when the state is the same, he returns; when different, not. [5] His statement: 'and the genitive is fronted'; as it is said: 'In the house is Zaid and in the room is Amr'; so his word 'adult' is coordinated with 'minor' and is genitive, and his word 'another defect' is coordinated with 'defect' and is nominative. The more apparent is that it should be said: theft in a minor is a defect, so in an adult it is another defect. [6] His statement: 'and madness of a minor' and madness is the disturbance of the power that distinguishes between good and bad things and perceives consequences, such that its effects do not appear and its actions are rendered inactive, either due to a deficiency in the brain's constitution, or due to imbalance of the brain's temperament from a humor or disease, or due to the devil's domination and casting of false imaginations so that he rejoices or fears without cause. In analogy it is invalidating of all acts of worship because it negates capability; and for this reason prophets are protected from it.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the chapter of defects in sales, specifically addressing three defects that allow the buyer to return a slave: running away (ibaq), bed-wetting (al-bawl fi al-firash), and theft (sariqah). The commentary elaborates on the matn's statement that these defects apply even if the running away is to a distance less than a journey, because the underlying reason is the loss of benefit to the master, which is the same regardless of distance. It cites 'Kamil al-Dirayah' to clarify that ibaq is unanimously a defect if the slave leaves the town, but if he does not leave the town, scholars differ; a large town like Cairo makes it a defect, a small town does not. For theft, stealing food from the master for eating is not a defect, but stealing for sale or from others is; also stealing a very small amount is not a defect, but breaking into a house without taking is a defect. The key distinction is between the same act in childhood vs. adulthood. In a child, bed-wetting is due to a weak bladder, running away due to love of play, theft due to lack of care; in an adult, they indicate internal malice. Therefore, if the defect existed in the same state at both seller and buyer, return is allowed; but if it occurred as a child at the seller and as an adult at the buyer, it is considered a different defect and return is not allowed. The text emphasizes that for theft, the child must be discerning (ya'qil); theft by a nons discerning child is not a defect. Madness (junun) is always a defect. The commentator also discusses the grammatical construction of the matn's phrasing.

Key Terms
اتحاد السبب (ittihad al-sabab): Unity of cause; the requirement that the defect has the same effective cause at both seller and buyer.
جنون (junun): Madness, disturbance of the distinguishing faculty; a defect always, and it invalidates acts of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when is a slave not returnable due to a defect?
A) When the defect existed at the seller but not at the buyer.
B) When the defect occurred in childhood at the seller and in adulthood at the buyer because the cause differs.
C) When the defect is madness in a minor.
D) When the buyer did not inspect the slave before purchase.

Key Takeaway
Unity of cause is essential for return; madness is an absolute defect.

The Requirement of Recurrence for Returning a Slave Due to Madness
Clarifying the requirement of recurrence for madness as a defect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of recurrence (mu'awadah) for returning a slave due to madness according to the Hanafi school and explain the reasoning based on the cause of madness.

Core Idea
A slave's madness is a defect only if it recurs after purchase; recurrence proves the defect existed at sale.

This lesson delves into a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding defects in sales: the return of a slave due to madness. The passage clarifies that mere existence of madness at the seller's time is insufficient; the defect must recur under the buyer's possession to ensure it was not cured. Understanding this nuance is essential for correctly applying the law of khiyar al-ayb (option of defect).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The buyer's right to return a slave due to madness. The ruling is that the buyer may return the slave, but under specific conditions.
2
Locate the key condition
The passage emphasizes that the madness must recur (mu'awadah) after the slave comes into the buyer's possession. Mere existence at the seller's time is not enough.
This is the central point.
3
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Why is recurrence required? Because the cause of madness is a corruption of the inner brain (fasād bāṭin al-dimāgh), which Allah can remove. Without recurrence, we cannot be certain the defect existed at the time of sale.
This is based on istihsan (juristic preference) because the cause might be cured.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
A misinterpretation claimed that Imam Muhammad's statement 'al-junoon 'ayb' meant no recurrence is needed. The commentator rejects this as mistaken.
The correct position is recorded in al-Asl and al-Jami' al-Kabir.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position is that recurrence is necessary. This is the correct view chosen by al-Ispijabi and confirmed in al-Fath.
This is the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د مَن جُنَّ في صغره عنده ، ثمّ عند مشتريه فيه، أو في كبَرِه. وإن شئت زيادة التوضيح فارجع إلى «التلويح» ، واختلفوا في مقدار الجنون، فقيل : هو عيب وإن كان ساعة ، وقيل : إن كان أكثر من يوم وليلة، وإن كان أقل، وقيل : المطبق عيب وغيره لا. [1] قوله : يُرد ... الخ؛ حاصله : أنَّ العبد إذا جُنَّ في صغره في يد البائع ، ثم عاده في يد المشتري يَرُدُّه، سواء كان هذا العود في الصغر أو في الكبر؛ لأنه عين الأول؛ إذ سبب الجنون في حالِ الصغرِ والكبرِ، متحد، وهو فساد باطن الدماغ. وهذا معنى قول محمد الله : والجنون عيب، لا ما قيل : إن معناه لا تشترط المعاودة للجنون في يد المشتري فيثبت الردُّ بمجرد وجوده عند البائع ؛ فإنه غلط؛ لأنّ الله قادر على إزالته بإزالة سببه، وإن كان قلما يزول. فإذا لم يعاوده جاز كون البيع صدرَ بعد الإزالة، فلا يرد بلا تحقيق قيام العيب، ولا بد من المعاودة، وهذا هو الصحيح، وهو المذكور في «الأصل»، و«الجامع الكبير»، واختاره الإِسبيجابي. كذا في «الفتح»
And one who became mad in his childhood at the seller, then (it recurred) at the buyer, whether in childhood or adulthood. If you wish further clarification, refer to al-Talwih. They differed regarding the extent of madness: some said it is a defect even if for an hour; some said if it is more than a day and night, but less than that (it is not a defect); some said continuous madness is a defect and other than that is not. [1] His statement: 'He is returned... etc.' Its summary: When a slave became mad in his childhood while in the possession of the seller, then it recurred in the possession of the buyer, he returns him, whether this recurrence is in childhood or adulthood, because it is the same (madness) as the first; since the cause of madness in childhood and adulthood is unified, which is corruption of the inner brain. This is the meaning of Muhammad's statement: 'Madness is a defect,' not what is said that its meaning is that recurrence of madness at the buyer is not required and return is established merely by its existence at the seller; for that is a mistake, because Allah is able to remove it by removing its cause, though it rarely disappears. If it does not recur, it is possible that the sale took place after removal, so it is not returned without certainty of the defect's existence, and recurrence is necessary. This is the correct view, and it is mentioned in al-Asl and al-Jami' al-Kabir, and al-Ispijabi chose it. As in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the ruling of returning a male slave due to junoon (madness). The base text states that if the slave became mad as a child while with the seller, and then the madness recurs at the buyer—whether in the buyer's childhood or adulthood—the buyer may return him. The commentator clarifies that recurrence is necessary; mere existence of madness at the seller's time is insufficient, because the cause (a brain condition) might have been removed by Allah, however rare. The correct position, as recorded in the authoritative Zahir al-Riwayah works (al-Asl and al-Jami' al-Kabir) and chosen by al-Ispijabi, requires that the madness actually recur after purchase. The passage also mentions an ikhtilaf over how long the madness must last to be a defect: some say any duration, others require more than a day, and still others require continuous (mutbiq) madness. This shows the nuanced approach of the Hanafi school in defining what constitutes a legal defect in sale.

Key Terms
الجنون (al-junoon): Insanity or madness, defined as a corruption of the inner brain (fasād bāṭin al-dimāgh).
المعاودة (al-mu'āwadah): Recurrence of the same defect after a period of absence.
المطبق (al-mutbiq): Continuous madness that never ceases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the underlying cause (illa) of madness?
A) Possession by jinn
B) Corruption of the inner brain
C) Evil eye
D) Temporary imbalance

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi sale law, a defect in a slave is actionable only if it recurs after purchase, because the defect's cause might be removed.

Defects Specific to Female Slaves and Common Defects
Defines defects specific to female slaves and common defects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects specific to female slaves and common defects, and apply the Hanafi rules regarding khiyar al-ayb (option of defect) in sales of slaves.

Core Idea
The purpose of a female slave is concubinage; defects impairing that purpose are defects only in her.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all defects are treated equally between male and female slaves. This lesson explains which defects are specific to female slaves, which are common to both, and the legal reasoning behind these classifications. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for applying the option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose
Recognize that the Hanafi school assigns different primary purposes to male and female slaves: concubinage (istifrash) for females and employment (istikhadam) for males.
This purpose-based reasoning is the key to classification.
2
Classify the defects
Sort defects into those specific to female slaves (bakhar, dafar, zina, tawallud) and those common to both (kufr, istihadah, irtifa' al-hayd).
Note that bakhar and dafar are only defects in females unless they become excessive in a male.
3
Apply the purpose test
For each defect, ask: Does this defect impair the intended purpose? If yes, it is a defect; if no, it is not. For example, bad breath does not affect a male slave's ability to work, but it hinders intimacy with a female slave.
Adultery and childbearing are also specific because they hinder the purpose of concubinage.
4
Understand the exceptions
Bad breath and body odor in a male slave become defects if they are so foul that they prevent physical closeness. Adultery becomes a defect in a male if it is habitual (more than twice).
These exceptions show the rule is not absolute.
5
Know the procedural rules
For cessation of menstruation, the age threshold is 17 (Abu Hanifa) or 15 (Sahibayn), and the minimum duration is one month. The buyer must get the seller to take an oath for return; the female slave's statement is accepted but not sufficient alone.
The age ikhtilaf is resolved in favor of the Sahibayn (15 years) for fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والبَخَرُ والدَّفَرُ والزنا والتَّوَلِّدُ منه عيب فيها : أي في الأمة لا فيه. والكفر عيب فيهما، والاستحاضة، وارتفاعُ الحيض في بنت سبع عشرة سنة لا أقل عيب. [2] قوله : والبَخَر؛ محركة كندكي دهان وجز آن، والبَخَر ككتف بوي. والدفر: بفتح الدال المهملة، يقال : دَفْراً له : أى مُنْتِنَاً له، والدَّفَر : ككتف كنده، تند. والدفر بالذال المعجمة محه كتربوی اتیر و تند خوش یا ناخوش وفرق وامتياز بمضاف إليه يابموصوف توان كرد وذفر ككتف مرد گنده، بغل و اذفر كنده بغل ومسك اذفر شك تيزبوي. كذا في منتهى الأرب»، وفي أكثر نسخ الكتاب بالدال المهملة، وفي بعضها بالمعجمة. [1] قوله : عيب؛ لأنَّ استمرار الدم وارتفاعه علامة الداء، والمعتبر في الارتفاع أقصى غاية البلوغ، وهو عند الإمام سبع عشرة سنة، وعندهما: خمس عشرة سنة، وبقولهما يُفتى كما صرح به العلامة الطحطاوي، والشامي في «حاشية الــدر المختار». ويعرف ذلك بقول الأمة؛ لأنه لا يعرفه غيرُها، ولكن لا ترد بقولها، بل لا بد من استحلاف البائع فتردّ بنكوله إن كان بعد القبض، وإن كان قبله فكذلك في الصحيح. وحاصله : إنه إذا صحح دعواه سُئِلَ البائع فإن صدقه رُدَّت عليه، وإلا لم يحلف عند الإمام، وإن أقرَّ به وأنكر كونه عنده. حَلَفَ فإن نكل رُدَّت عليه، ولا تقبلُ البينة على أن الانقطاع كان عند البائع للتيقن بكذبهم كذا في «البحر». واعلم أن أدنى مُدَّةٍ في ارتفاع الحيض حتى يُعَدَّ عيباً شهر واحد. كذا في «فتاوى قاضي خان
And bad smell of the mouth (bakhar) and bad smell of the body (dafar) and adultery and childbearing from him is a defect in her: i.e., in the female slave, not in him. (linguistic definitions). [3] His statement: 'a defect in her, not in him' because that detracts from the intended purpose in her, which is bed-mate... and disbelief is a defect in both, and istihadah and cessation of menstruation in a girl of seventeen years, not less, is a defect and desire for children, and the purpose from the male slave is employment, and these things do not detract from that, unless the first two (bakhar and dafar) become so foul in him that they prevent closeness to the master, and the correct view is that the beardless and others are the same. Or if adultery becomes his habit—i.e., it is repeated from him more than twice. As in al-Manh; and in Majma' al-Anhur: recurrence at purchase is not stipulated for zina as in most books. [1] His statement: 'a defect' because the continuance of blood and its cessation is a sign of illness, and the maximum limit of puberty is considered for cessation; with Imam (Abu Hanifa) it is seventeen years, and with them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is fifteen years, and fatwa is given according to their statement as stated by allamah al-Tahawi and al-Shami in Hashiyat al-Durr al-Mukhtar. And that is known by the statement of the female slave because no one else knows it, but she is not believed to the extent of return; rather the seller must be made to take an oath, and she is returned by his refusal (nukul) if it is after delivery, and if before, similarly in the correct view. Its summary: If he proves his claim, the seller is asked; if he confirms, she is returned; otherwise at Imam's view he is not made to take an oath; and if he admits the defect but denies it was present at his time, he takes an oath, and if he refuses, she is returned. And evidence is not accepted that the cessation occurred at the seller because of certainty of their lying—as in al-Bahr. And know that the minimum period of cessation of menstruation to be considered a defect is one month. As in Fatawa Qadi Khan.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines several defects (uyub) relevant to the sale of slaves. It distinguishes between defects specific to female slaves (amah) and those common to both sexes. Bad breath (bakhar), body odor (dafar), adultery (zina), and childbearing (tawallud) are defects only in the female slave because they impair her primary purpose: concubinage (istifrash). In a male slave (ghulam), these do not affect his purpose of employment (istikhadam) unless they become excessive. Disbelief (kufr) is a defect in both. Istihadah (chronic uterine bleeding) and cessation of menstruation (irtifa' al-hayd) in a girl who has reached the maximum age of puberty (17 according to Abu Hanifa, 15 according to the Sahibayn) are defects because they indicate infertility. The passage also details the evidentiary procedure: the female slave's word is accepted to prove the cessation, but return requires the seller's oath; if he refuses, she is returned. The minimum duration for cessation to be a defect is one month.

Key Terms
البَخَر (al-bakhar): Bad smell from the mouth.
الدَّفَر (al-dafar): Bad smell from the body (armpit or body odor).
الاستحاضة (al-istihādah): Chronic uterine bleeding outside the menstrual period.
ارتفاع الحيض (irtifā' al-ḥayḍ): Cessation of menstruation, indicating possible infertility.
الاستفراش (al-istifrāsh): Concubinage; the primary purpose of a female slave in Hanafi law.
الاستخدام (al-istikhdām): Employment; the primary purpose of a male slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One week
B) One month
C) Three months
D) One year

Key Takeaway
Defects in slaves are classified based on the intended purpose; defects impairing that purpose give the buyer the right to return the slave.
